Visual Domain Gap — rendered textures │ │     vs. real-world lighting variation    │ └──────────────────────────────────────────┘


Mitigation strategies: Domain Randomization ──► vary textures, lighting, masses during training System Identification ──► calibrate sim to match real actuator dynamics Residual RL ──► fine-tune with real-world rollouts post sim training
Figure 2.3: The sim-to-real gap pipeline showing four primary contributing factors that degrade policy performance when transferring from simulation to physical deployment. Domain randomization and system identification are the primary mitigation strategies.

2.5.3 Mitigation Strategies

The robotics community has developed several effective strategies for closing the sim-to-real gap:

Domain Randomization

Domain randomization, introduced by OpenAI in 2017 for the Dexterous In-Hand Manipulation project and formalized by Tobin et al. [6], deliberately varies simulation parameters during training to expose the policy to a wide range of conditions.

Parameters that are randomized include:

  • Friction coefficients (floor, object surfaces, robot feet)
  • Mass and inertia of objects
  • Lighting conditions (intensity, color, position)
  • Camera properties (field of view, noise level, exposure)
  • Motor parameters (delay, torque noise)
  • Object textures and colors

The intuition: if the policy is trained across a distribution of simulation conditions, the real world becomes just another sample from that distribution — one the policy has seen something like before.

<h1>Example: Domain randomization in NVIDIA Isaac Lab</h1>

from isaaclab.envs import ManagerBasedRLEnv from isaaclab.utils.noise import GaussianNoiseCfg


<h1>Randomize friction during training</h1> friction_cfg = GaussianNoiseCfg( mean=0.0, std=0.1,          # ±10% variation around nominal friction operation="add", )


<h1>Randomize mass of objects the robot interacts with</h1> mass_cfg = GaussianNoiseCfg( mean=0.0, std=0.05,          # ±5% variation around nominal mass operation="scale", )
Figure 2.4 — Domain Randomization 
Domain Randomization: same robot, same task — varied environment parameters

┌───────────────┬───────────────┬───────────────┐ │  Env 1        │  Env 2        │  Env 3        │ │  Floor: wood  │  Floor: tile  │  Floor: carpet│ │  Light: warm  │  Light: cold  │  Light: dim   │ │  Object: red  │  Object: blue │  Object: white│ │  [Robot ──►]  │  [Robot ──►]  │  [Robot ──►]  │ ├───────────────┼───────────────┼───────────────┤ │  Env 4        │  Env 5        │  Env 6        │ │  Floor: metal │  Floor: stone │  Floor: grass │ │  Light: harsh │  Light: mixed │  Light: neon  │ │  Object: green│  Object: black│  Object: strip│ │  [Robot ──►]  │  [Robot ──►]  │  [Robot ──►]  │ ├───────────────┼───────────────┼───────────────┤ │  Env 7        │  Env 8        │  Env 9        │ │  Floor: dirt  │  Floor: wet   │  Floor: outdoo│ │  Light: sunset│  Light: overc │  Light: sun   │ │  Object: ceram│  Object: plstc│  Object: metal│ │  [Robot ──►]  │  [Robot ──►]  │  [Robot ──►]  │ └───────────────┴───────────────┴───────────────┘


Randomized parameters: Visual: floor texture, object color/material, lighting intensity, lighting direction Physical: object mass ±20%, joint friction ±30%, actuator latency ±5ms Sensor: camera noise level, depth sensor range, IMU drift


Result: policy trained on distribution P(env) transfers robustly to real world
Figure 2.4: Domain randomization exposes the trained policy to a distribution of 9 environments with varied floor textures, lighting conditions, object colors, and physical parameters. Policies trained with domain randomization transfer significantly better to physical deployment.

System Identification

System identification involves measuring the real robot's physical parameters — mass, inertia, motor constants, joint friction — and calibrating the simulation model to match. Rather than randomizing over a range, system identification seeks to make the simulation as accurate as possible.

This approach is more accurate than domain randomization for known parameters but does not handle unmodeled dynamics or parameters that change over time (e.g., friction coefficients that vary with temperature or surface wear).

Sim-to-Real Fine-Tuning

A practical and widely-used strategy: pre-train in simulation, fine-tune on real hardware. The simulation phase produces a policy that is approximately correct — it knows roughly how to walk, grasp, or navigate. The fine-tuning phase on real hardware corrects the residual errors using a small amount of real-world experience.

This approach is computationally efficient: most of the training (the expensive exploration phase) happens in simulation; only the refinement requires physical hardware. Tesla's approach with Optimus, and Figure AI's approach with Figure 02, both reportedly use variants of this strategy.

Real2Sim

An increasingly powerful approach reverses the typical direction: rather than deploying simulation-trained policies to the real world, Real2Sim reconstructs real environments as simulation assets.

Using techniques from neural radiance fields (NeRF) or 3D Gaussian splatting, a real workspace can be scanned and reconstructed as a photorealistic simulation scene. Policies fine-tuned in this reconstructed environment can then be directly deployed in the real environment it was scanned from.

2.5.4 Case Study: Isaac Lab Locomotion Training on Unitree G1

NVIDIA Isaac Lab provides a concrete demonstration of successful sim-to-real transfer. The following summarizes the training pipeline for a bipedal locomotion policy on the Unitree G1, as described in NVIDIA's published technical documentation [7]:

Simulation environment: Isaac Lab, running on NVIDIA GPU cluster at 4,096× real-time speed. 4,096 parallel robot instances trained simultaneously. Training algorithm: Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO) with asymmetric actor-critic. The critic (used only during training) receives privileged information about the simulation state; the actor (used during deployment) receives only observations available on the real robot. Domain randomization applied:
  • Floor friction: uniform distribution [0.4, 1.2]
  • Robot mass: ±10% per link
  • Motor torque limits: ±20% of nominal
  • Observation noise: Gaussian noise on all sensor readings
  • Terrain: random slopes, steps, and uneven surfaces
Training time: ~24 hours on 4× NVIDIA A100 GPUs. Deployment result: The trained policy transferred directly to the physical Unitree G1 with no additional fine-tuning, achieving stable locomotion over varied indoor terrain including carpets, tile, and uneven flooring.

This case study represents the current state of the art in sim-to-real transfer and demonstrates that the gap, while not eliminated, has been dramatically narrowed for locomotion tasks.

2.6 Physical Laws Every AI Robot Engineer Must Know

AI engineers who transition into robotics frequently underestimate the importance of physical intuition. A language model can describe Newton's laws; a robot engineer must feel their consequences in every design decision. This section provides the essential physical background.

2.6.1 Friction

Definition: Friction is the force resisting relative motion between two surfaces in contact. It acts parallel to the contact surface, opposing the direction of motion or intended motion. Two regimes:
  • Static friction (f_s ≤ μ_s · N): The force preventing motion when surfaces are not sliding. The maximum static friction is proportional to the normal force N and the static friction coefficient μ_s. Until the applied force exceeds this maximum, the surfaces do not move relative to each other.
  • Kinetic friction (f_k = μ_k · N): The force opposing motion when surfaces are sliding. Kinetic friction is approximately constant, proportional to normal force, and typically lower than maximum static friction (μ_k < μ_s).
Robotics implications: Locomotion: Walking requires friction. When a foot pushes backward against the floor, friction pushes the robot forward. On surfaces with insufficient friction (ice, wet tile), the foot slides and locomotion fails. A locomotion controller must estimate available friction and keep ground reaction forces within the friction cone — the set of forces that friction can support without slipping. Grasping: A robotic hand grips an object by applying normal force against its surface. The maximum tangential force (shear) the grasp can resist is μ_s · N — the product of friction coefficient and grip force. On smooth, oily, or wet surfaces, the friction coefficient drops dramatically. A grasp force adequate for a dry object may be insufficient for a wet one. Design implication: Robots operating in real environments must handle varying friction conditions. Controllers that assume constant friction will fail. Domain randomization of friction coefficients (§2.5.3) is therefore essential for training robust policies.

2.6.2 Inertia

Definition: Inertia is the resistance of a body to changes in its state of motion. For linear motion, inertial resistance is simply mass (F = ma). For rotation, the relevant quantity is the moment of inertia (τ = Iα), which depends on both mass and its distribution relative to the rotation axis. Robotics implications: Arm control: A robot arm moving at high speed carries significant rotational kinetic energy. Stopping it quickly requires large braking torques. If the controller does not account for the arm's inertia correctly, the arm will overshoot its target position, potentially colliding with objects or the robot's own body. Balance: A humanoid robot's center of mass (CoM) must remain above the support polygon (the convex hull of its foot contacts) for static balance. When the robot accelerates its arms or torso, the reaction forces shift the CoM. A balance controller must anticipate these inertial effects and compensate proactively. Leg swing: During walking, the swing leg must be accelerated and decelerated once per step. The moment of inertia of the leg determines how much torque the hip and knee actuators must produce. Heavier legs require more powerful actuators for fast walking. Design implication: The mass distribution of a humanoid robot is an engineering decision with profound control implications. Moving mass from the distal limbs (feet, lower legs) to the proximal body (torso) reduces leg inertia and improves walking efficiency — one reason many humanoids have lightweight lower legs and heavier torsos.

2.6.3 Compliance

Definition: Compliance is the inverse of stiffness — it describes how much a structure deforms in response to an applied force. A compliant structure deforms significantly under small forces; a stiff structure deforms very little. Why compliance matters in humanoid robotics: Safety: A robot that collides with a human while operating stiff joints transmits the full impact force to the human. A robot operating compliant joints absorbs part of the impact in joint deformation, reducing injury risk. This is the safety argument for Series Elastic Actuators (§1.5.3) and compliant joint designs. Contact stability: Rigid robots struggle with contact tasks. When a rigid robot touches a surface and pushes with controlled force, small errors in position translate to large, uncontrolled force variations — because there is no mechanical buffer between the position error and the contact force. A compliant robot absorbs position errors through joint compliance, producing smoother, more controlled forces. Energy storage: Compliant elements (springs) can store and return energy. In human walking, tendons store energy during heel strike and return it during toe-off, improving metabolic efficiency. Robot legs with compliant elements can achieve similar energy benefits — Series Elastic Actuators were partly motivated by this consideration. Terrain adaptation: Compliant feet and ankles allow a walking robot to conform to uneven terrain passively, without requiring the controller to explicitly plan for every surface irregularity. Design implication: The optimal stiffness for a robot joint depends on its application. High stiffness is appropriate for precise manufacturing tasks; high compliance is appropriate for safe human contact and uneven terrain locomotion. Many modern humanoid designs use variable stiffness actuators — mechanisms that can adjust their compliance in software — to handle both requirements.

2.6.4 Contact Mechanics

Definition: Contact mechanics describes what happens at the interface between two bodies when they touch. It encompasses the distribution of stress within the contact zone, the onset of sliding, and the relationship between contact geometry and contact force. Hertzian contact: When two elastic bodies with curved surfaces (such as a robot fingertip and a spherical object) come into contact under a normal load, they deform elastically. The contact area is not a point but an ellipse or circle whose size depends on the applied force and the elastic properties of both materials. This is Hertzian contact theory, developed by Heinrich Hertz in 1882. Why contact mechanics matters for grasping: Grasp stability: Whether a grasp is stable depends on the geometry of contact, the friction at each contact, and the forces and torques that can be resisted. A grasp with three contacts can be analyzed using the theory of force closure — whether the contact forces can resist arbitrary external disturbances. Tactile sensing: The distribution of contact stress across a fingertip contains information about the object's shape, the contact location, and the applied force. Robotic tactile sensors attempt to measure this stress distribution. Understanding contact mechanics is prerequisite to interpreting tactile sensor data. Soft manipulation: When robot fingers made of soft materials (silicone, foam) contact an object, Hertzian contact theory does not apply directly — the deformations are large and nonlinear. Soft robotic grippers exploit this: large contact areas, passive form closure, and high friction enable reliable grasping of varied objects without precise position control. Practical implication for engineers: Contact is where theory meets reality most violently. Every manipulation task involves contact. Controllers that ignore contact mechanics will fail in practice; controllers that explicitly account for it — through force feedback, impedance control, or learned contact models — are dramatically more robust.

2.7 The Data Advantage of Humanoid Form

One of the most compelling arguments for the humanoid form factor is one that was not anticipated by early roboticists: the data advantage.

2.7.1 The Learning from Demonstration Paradigm

Modern robot learning is dominated by imitation learning — training robot policies by having them imitate demonstrations of desired behavior. The quality and quantity of demonstrations determines the quality of the learned policy.

Collecting robot demonstration data is expensive. A human teleoperator must control the robot through each desired behavior, one demonstration at a time. Building a dataset of 10,000 demonstrations might require weeks of expert operator time and specialized hardware.

Unless you can use demonstrations that already exist.

2.7.2 Human Video Data as Robot Training Data

The internet contains billions of hours of video of humans performing physical tasks: cooking, cleaning, assembling furniture, performing surgery, playing sports. This data was not collected for robotics, but it captures exactly the kind of physical intelligence that humanoid robots need to develop.

For a robot with a non-humanoid morphology — a robot arm, a wheeled platform — this data is largely inaccessible. The kinematics of a human arm and a robot arm are different; the motion patterns of a walking human and a wheeled robot are incomparable.

A humanoid robot, by contrast, has the same basic morphology as the humans in those videos. With appropriate motion retargeting — mapping human body poses to robot joint angles — the vast archive of human video becomes potential training data for humanoid robots.

Google DeepMind's work on learning from human video demonstrated that policies trained with human demonstrations transferred to robots significantly better than those trained without them [8]. The shared morphology enables knowledge transfer at a scale that is simply unavailable to non-humanoid platforms.

2.7.3 Motion Capture and the SMPL Model

A more controlled form of human motion data comes from motion capture (MoCap): precisely tracking the 3D positions of markers attached to a human body during movement. MoCap data captures the rich dynamics of human motion — the subtle weight shifts, the anticipatory adjustments, the smooth transitions between postures.

The SMPL (Skinned Multi-Person Linear) model [9] provides a parametric representation of the human body that enables compact description of body shape and pose. SMPL represents a human body pose as a 72-dimensional vector (24 joints × 3 rotation parameters each) and body shape as a 10-dimensional vector.

Motion capture data annotated with SMPL parameters can be retargeted to humanoid robot joint angles through a process called motion retargeting:

<h1>Simplified motion retargeting from SMPL to robot URDF</h1>

<h1>In practice, this requires solving an inverse kinematics problem</h1> <h1>for each frame of the motion capture sequence</h1>


def retarget_smpl_to_robot(smpl_pose: np.ndarray, smpl_to_robot_joint_map: dict) -> np.ndarray: """ Retarget SMPL body pose to robot joint angles.


Args: smpl_pose: (72,) array of SMPL pose parameters (24 joints × 3 axis-angle parameters) smpl_to_robot_joint_map: dict mapping SMPL joint indices to robot joint names


Returns: robot_joint_angles: array of target joint angles for robot """ # Reshape SMPL pose to (24, 3) axis-angle representation joint_rotations = smpl_pose.reshape(24, 3)


robot_angles = {} for smpl_idx, robot_joint_name in smpl_to_robot_joint_map.items(): # Convert axis-angle to rotation angle (magnitude of rotation vector) rotation_angle = np.linalg.norm(joint_rotations[smpl_idx])


# Scale by joint range ratio (human joint range / robot joint range) # This is a simplification; real retargeting uses IK solvers robot_angles[robot_joint_name] = rotation_angle


return robot_angles

2.7.4 The DROID Dataset

The most significant large-scale robot demonstration dataset as of 2025 is DROID (Distributed Robot Interaction Dataset) [10], collected by a consortium of 13 academic institutions across the United States and Europe. DROID contains:

  • 76,000 robot manipulation demonstrations
  • Collected across 86 different environments
  • Covering diverse household and laboratory objects
  • Using a 7-DOF Franka robot arm with two wrist cameras and one overhead camera

Policies trained on DROID demonstrated substantially better generalization to new objects and environments than policies trained on single-institution datasets. The lesson: scale and diversity of demonstration data matters enormously.

For humanoid robots, the DROID philosophy applied at the scale of the internet — using the vast archive of human motion data as implicit demonstrations — represents the most promising path toward general physical intelligence.

2.7.5 The Scale Argument

The argument for humanoid form ultimately comes down to data scale:

Data SourceScaleAccessible to Humanoid?Accessible to Non-Humanoid?
Internet video of humansBillions of hoursYes (with retargeting)No
Motion capture databasesMillions of sequencesYes (with retargeting)No
Robot demonstration dataThousands of demonstrationsYesYes
Simulation-generated dataEffectively unlimitedYesYes

The humanoid form factor unlocks the two largest data sources — internet video and motion capture — that are unavailable to non-humanoid platforms. As learning algorithms improve and the ability to extract training signal from human video matures, this data advantage will become increasingly decisive.

2.8 Chapter Summary

The argument of this chapter can be stated briefly: intelligence without a body is a different — and more limited — thing than intelligence with one. Brooks's subsumption architecture demonstrated that complex adaptive behavior can emerge from simple reactive layers without central world models. Harnad's symbol grounding problem identified why: symbols acquire meaning through sensorimotor experience, not through formal definition. These are not historical curiosities; they are the theoretical basis for why large language models, despite their remarkable language capabilities, cannot substitute for an embodied controller operating at 1,000 Hz.

The practical consequence is the hierarchical hybrid architecture that runs through every technical chapter that follows: LLM reasoning at the strategic level, embodied control at the reactive level, with the challenge of bridging between them. The sim-to-real gap — the performance degradation when simulation-trained policies encounter real hardware — is the central obstacle to that bridge. Domain randomization, system identification, and the Real2Sim pipeline represent the current engineering response. They are partial solutions to a problem that does not yet have a complete one.

The data advantage of humanoid morphology is the least obvious but ultimately most consequential point. Billions of hours of internet video and millions of motion capture sequences document how human bodies interact with a human-built world. Non-humanoid platforms cannot learn from this data. Humanoid platforms, with appropriate retargeting, can. As learning algorithms mature and the ability to extract training signal from human video improves, this advantage will only grow. Part II turns to the software infrastructure that makes all of this possible: the ROS 2 stack that connects the hardware described in Chapter 1 to the AI systems described here.

Chapter 3: ROS 2 Architecture

Part II — The Robotic Nervous System
Version 1.0 — Draft | Author: Muhammad Faisal Laiq Siddiqui

3.1 Introduction — Why Robots Need Middleware

Imagine building a humanoid robot without any shared communication infrastructure. Every sensor driver, every motion controller, and every perception algorithm would need to speak directly to every other component using bespoke, hand-crafted protocols. A LiDAR driver would need to know the exact memory layout expected by the SLAM algorithm. The joint velocity controller would need to directly poll the IMU at precisely the right moment. Adding a new camera would require modifying every module that consumes visual data. The result would be a tightly coupled monolith — fragile, untestable, and impossible to port between robot platforms.

This is precisely the problem that middleware solves. In computing, middleware sits between application software and the underlying operating system, providing a standardized set of communication, discovery, and data management services. For robotics, the challenge is uniquely demanding: middleware must handle real-time sensor streams, coordinate dozens of concurrent processes running across multiple CPU cores (or even multiple networked computers), and do all of this with latency measured in milliseconds.

ROS 2 (Robot Operating System 2) [1] is the de facto standard robotic middleware as of 2026. It is not a traditional operating system — it runs atop Linux (or Windows or macOS) and provides a rich set of libraries, tools, and conventions that allow software developers to build robot applications as loosely coupled, independently testable components called nodes. Nodes communicate through well-defined interfaces: topics for streaming data, services for request-response transactions, and actions for long-running tasks with feedback. The entire communication layer is built on top of the DDS (Data Distribution Service) standard [2], a publish-subscribe middleware specification maintained by the Object Management Group (OMG) that brings with it industrial-grade reliability, quality-of-service controls, and optional security.

For a humanoid robot, this architecture is not a convenience — it is a necessity. A system with 28–44 degrees of freedom, multiple cameras, IMUs, force-torque sensors, microphones, and an onboard GPU running inference workloads simultaneously cannot be coordinated by a monolithic program. It demands an architecture where each concern is isolated in its own node, where failures are contained rather than propagating through the entire system, and where new capabilities can be added by writing new nodes without touching existing ones.

This chapter provides a complete technical grounding in ROS 2 architecture — from the history of why it replaced its predecessor, through the DDS middleware layer, the four core communication primitives, lifecycle node management, and the build system, to a full workspace setup guide. Every concept is illustrated with code that runs on a standard Ubuntu 22.04 development machine.

3.2 Why ROS 2 Replaced ROS 1

ROS 1, first released by Willow Garage in 2007 [3], was a transformative achievement. It gave the robotics research community a common software platform and enabled an explosion of open-source robot algorithms. However, ROS 1 was designed with a set of architectural assumptions that proved inadequate for production deployment:

Single master dependency. ROS 1 required a central process called rosmaster to broker all communication. If this process crashed, the entire robot communication infrastructure failed — an unacceptable single point of failure for a deployed system. No real-time support. ROS 1 was designed for research robots running on standard Linux kernels. It provided no latency guarantees, making it unsuitable for hard real-time control loops that require deterministic execution within microseconds. No security. ROS 1 nodes communicated over unencrypted TCP/UDP connections with no authentication. On a research network, this was acceptable. On a humanoid robot operating in a hospital or factory, it is a critical vulnerability — any device on the same network can inject arbitrary commands. Multi-robot limitations. Coordinating multiple robots in ROS 1 required complex namespacing conventions and was never a first-class design concern. Python 2 dependency. ROS 1 was tightly coupled to Python 2, which reached end-of-life in January 2020.

ROS 2, developed by Open Robotics (now part of the Intrinsic organization at Alphabet) in collaboration with industry partners, was redesigned from the ground up to address all of these limitations [1]. Its first stable release was Ardent Apalone in 2017. The current Long Term Support (LTS) release is ROS 2 Humble Hawksbill, released in May 2022 and supported through May 2027 on Ubuntu 22.04 LTS [4].

The four architectural advances that define ROS 2:

3.2.1 DDS as the Communication Foundation

Rather than building a custom transport layer, ROS 2 delegates all communication to vendor implementations of the DDS (Data Distribution Service) standard [2]. DDS is a peer-to-peer publish-subscribe middleware that was originally developed for defense, aerospace, and medical systems — exactly the domains that demand reliability and real-time performance. By using DDS, ROS 2 inherits decades of industrial middleware engineering and gains the ability to plug in different DDS vendors depending on use case requirements.

3.2.2 Real-Time Support

ROS 2 is designed to work with real-time Linux kernels (PREEMPT-RT patches) and provides mechanisms for deterministic execution. The ros2_control framework (Chapter 4) operates control loops with deterministic timing on dedicated threads. Quality-of-service (QoS) policies allow developers to specify reliability and deadline requirements for each communication channel.

3.2.3 Security via DDS-Security

ROS 2 integrates with the DDS-Security specification [5] through the SROS2 (Secure ROS 2) tooling, providing mutual authentication, access control, and encryption for all inter-node communication. This is covered in depth in Chapter 5.

3.2.4 Multi-Robot and Multi-Host Support

Because DDS is fundamentally a networked, peer-to-peer protocol, multiple robots can participate in the same ROS 2 graph simply by being on the same network and sharing a Domain ID. Isolation between robot systems is achieved by using different Domain IDs. No central master process is required.

Figure 3.1 — ROS 1 vs. ROS 2 Architecture 
        ROS 1                                      ROS 2

(centralized master)                    (peer-to-peer DDS)


┌─────────────────┐                    ┌─────────────────────────────────┐ │   rosmaster     │  ← single point    │         DDS Domain ID: 0        │ │  (xmlrpc:11311) │    of failure      │                                 │ └────────┬────────┘                    │  [Node A]         [Node B]      │ │                             │  camera_node      slam_node     │ ┌────────┼────────┐                    │      │                 │        │ │        │        │                    │      └──── /image ─────┘        │ [Node A] [Node B] [Node C]              │      (RTPS over UDP multicast)  │ camera   slam     controller            │                                 │ \     |     /                       │  [Node C]         [Node D]      │ \    |    /                        │  controller       planner       │ TCP/IP                            │      │                 │        │ (serial)                           │      └── /cmd_vel ─────┘        │ └─────────────────────────────────┘


Key differences: rosmaster required ──► No master needed (automatic peer discovery) TCP transport only ──► DDS RTPS (UDP multicast + reliable delivery) No QoS control     ──► Full QoS policy control per topic Linux only          ──► Cross-platform (Linux, Windows, macOS, RTOS) No real-time        ──► Real-time capable middleware options
Figure 3.1: Architectural comparison between ROS 1 (centralized rosmaster) and ROS 2 (peer-to-peer DDS). ROS 2 eliminates the single point of failure by using DDS's built-in peer discovery over RTPS/UDP, while adding full QoS control and cross-platform support.

3.3 DDS Middleware Deep Dive

Understanding DDS is essential for diagnosing performance issues, configuring quality-of-service behavior, and understanding the security model. DDS operates on a publish-subscribe paradigm: publishers write data to named topics, and subscribers receive data from those topics. The DDS layer handles discovery, matching publishers to subscribers, and delivering data according to configurable quality policies.

3.3.1 The RTPS Wire Protocol

DDS implementations communicate using the RTPS (Real-Time Publish-Subscribe) protocol [2], which defines the wire format for discovery messages and data exchange. RTPS runs over UDP/IP (by default) or TCP/IP and supports both unicast and multicast communication. When a ROS 2 node starts, it uses RTPS's Simple Discovery Protocol (SDP) to announce its publishers and subscribers on the network, allowing other nodes to discover and connect to it without a central broker.

3.3.2 DDS Vendor Implementations in ROS 2

ROS 2 supports multiple DDS vendor implementations through an abstraction layer called the RMW (ROS 2 Middleware Interface). The two most widely used implementations are:

Fast DDS (eProsima) — The default DDS implementation for ROS 2 Humble. Fast DDS is open source under the Apache 2.0 license and is maintained by eProsima. It supports all DDS-Security features and provides good performance for most robotics workloads. The RMW package is rmw_fastrtps_cpp. Cyclone DDS (Eclipse Foundation) — An alternative open-source DDS implementation, originally developed at ADLINK Technology and now maintained by the Eclipse Foundation. Cyclone DDS is known for lower latency in some configurations and is widely used in production robotics deployments. The RMW package is rmw_cyclonedds_cpp.

Switching between DDS implementations requires only setting an environment variable:

<h1>Use Cyclone DDS</h1>

export RMW_IMPLEMENTATION=rmw_cyclonedds_cpp


<h1>Use Fast DDS (default for ROS 2 Humble)</h1> export RMW_IMPLEMENTATION=rmw_fastrtps_cpp

3.3.3 Quality of Service Policies

One of the most powerful features DDS brings to ROS 2 is a rich set of Quality of Service (QoS) policies that allow developers to express requirements about how data should be delivered. The following table summarizes the QoS policies most relevant to robotics applications:

QoS PolicyOptionsUse Case
ReliabilityRELIABLE, BEST_EFFORTUse RELIABLE for sensor data that must not be dropped; BEST_EFFORT for high-frequency data where staleness is worse than loss
DurabilityVOLATILE, TRANSIENT_LOCALUse TRANSIENT_LOCAL to deliver the last published message to late-joining subscribers (useful for robot description topics)
HistoryKEEP_LAST(N), KEEP_ALLKEEP_LAST(1) for real-time sensor streams; KEEP_ALL for audit logs
DeadlineDurationPublisher commits to publishing within the deadline; subscriber is notified if deadline is missed
LivelinessAUTOMATIC, MANUAL_BY_PARTICIPANTDetects when a publisher becomes unresponsive — critical for fault detection
LifespanDurationData older than the lifespan is discarded — prevents stale sensor readings from being consumed

ROS 2 provides several pre-configured QoS profiles for common use cases. The SENSOR_DATA profile uses BEST_EFFORT reliability and KEEP_LAST(5) history — appropriate for camera images where a dropped frame is preferable to a delayed one. The SERVICES_DEFAULT profile uses RELIABLE reliability — appropriate for service calls that must complete successfully.

3.3.4 Discovery and Domain IDs

Every ROS 2 process is assigned to a Domain ID (an integer from 0 to 232). Nodes in different Domain IDs cannot discover each other, providing a simple isolation mechanism. The Domain ID is set via the ROS_DOMAIN_ID environment variable:

<h1>Isolate this terminal session to Domain 42</h1>

export ROS_DOMAIN_ID=42

Within a domain, DDS discovery uses multicast UDP (on local networks) or configured peer lists (for cross-subnet communication) to allow nodes to find each other automatically. This peer-to-peer discovery eliminates the single point of failure that plagued ROS 1's rosmaster.

Figure 3.2 — DDS Communication Model 
┌─────────────────────────────────── DDS Domain ID: 42 ────────────────────────────────────┐

│                                                                                            │ │   PUBLISHER                           TOPIC                      SUBSCRIBERS              │ │                                                                                            │ │  ┌─────────────────┐                                           ┌──────────────────┐       │ │  │  JointState     │  ──── /joint_states ─────────────────►   │   SLAM Node      │       │ │  │  Broadcaster    │       QoS: RELIABLE                  │    │  (nav2_slam_sync)│       │ │  │  (hardware iface)│       Depth: KEEP_LAST(10)           │    └──────────────────┘       │ │  └─────────────────┘       History: KEEP_LAST              │                              │ │                                                             │    ┌──────────────────┐       │ │                                                             └──► │  Controller      │       │ │  ┌─────────────────┐                                            │  Manager         │       │ │  │  Camera Node    │  ──── /image_raw ──────────────────────►  │  (ros2_control)  │       │ │  │  (rgb-d driver) │       QoS: BEST_EFFORT                     └──────────────────┘       │ │  └─────────────────┘       Depth: KEEP_LAST(1)                                            │ │                             Durability: VOLATILE               ┌──────────────────┐       │ │                                                                 │  ObjectDetect    │       │ │                                                            ─────►  Node (YOLOv8)  │       │ │                                                                 └──────────────────┘       │ │                                                                                            │ │  QoS Presets: SENSOR_DATA = BestEffort+Volatile  │  SERVICES = Reliable+Volatile          │ └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
Figure 3.2: DDS communication model showing a publisher (JointState Broadcaster) writing sensor data to the /joint_states topic, with two subscriber nodes (SLAM and Controller Manager) receiving the data. QoS policies—Reliable delivery with Keep Last(10) depth—are applied per-topic and negotiated between publisher and subscriber.

3.4 Core Communication Primitives

ROS 2 provides four communication primitives, each designed for a different interaction pattern. Understanding when to use each primitive is fundamental to designing well-structured robot software.

3.4.1 Topics — Asynchronous Publish/Subscribe

Topics are the primary mechanism for streaming data in ROS 2. A publisher node writes messages to a named topic, and any number of subscriber nodes can receive those messages. Communication is asynchronous — the publisher does not wait for subscribers to process the message, and subscribers do not request data on demand. This makes topics ideal for continuous sensor streams such as camera images, LiDAR point clouds, joint states, and IMU readings.

Messages on topics are typed. Each topic has an associated message type (defined in .msg files), and the ROS 2 type system ensures that publishers and subscribers agree on the message format at connection time.

The following example demonstrates a publisher node that publishes joint velocity commands at 100 Hz, and a subscriber node that receives and logs them:

<h1>joint_velocity_publisher.py</h1>

<h1>Publishes joint velocity commands for a 3-DOF humanoid arm at 100 Hz.</h1> <h1>ROS 2 Humble | Python 3.10+ | rclpy</h1>


from rclpy.node import Node from rclpy.qos import QoSProfile, ReliabilityPolicy, HistoryPolicy from sensor_msgs.msg import JointState


class JointVelocityPublisher(Node): """ Publishes sinusoidal joint velocity commands on the /joint_velocity_commands topic. Intended to demonstrate topic-based communication in ROS 2. """


def __init__(self) -> None: super().__init__('joint_velocity_publisher')


# Define QoS profile: best-effort reliability for high-frequency control data. # Dropping an occasional message is preferable to queuing stale commands. qos_profile = QoSProfile( reliability=ReliabilityPolicy.BEST_EFFORT, history=HistoryPolicy.KEEP_LAST, depth=10, )


# Create the publisher on the /joint_velocity_commands topic self._publisher = self.create_publisher( msg_type=JointState, topic='/joint_velocity_commands', qos_profile=qos_profile, )


# Timer fires at 100 Hz (period = 0.01 seconds) self._timer = self.create_timer( timer_period_sec=0.01, callback=self._publish_velocities, )


# Track elapsed time for sinusoidal trajectory generation self._elapsed_time: float = 0.0


self.get_logger().info('JointVelocityPublisher started at 100 Hz')


def _publish_velocities(self) -> None: """Callback invoked by the timer every 10 ms. Publishes one JointState message.""" msg = JointState() msg.header.stamp = self.get_clock().now().to_msg()


# Three joints: shoulder_pitch, elbow_flex, wrist_roll msg.name = ['shoulder_pitch', 'elbow_flex', 'wrist_roll']


# Sinusoidal velocity profile — amplitude 0.5 rad/s, frequency 0.5 Hz msg.velocity = [ 0.5  math.sin(2.0  math.pi  0.5  self._elapsed_time), 0.3  math.sin(2.0  math.pi  0.3  self._elapsed_time + 0.5), 0.2  math.sin(2.0  math.pi  0.2  self._elapsed_time + 1.0), ]


self._publisher.publish(msg) self._elapsed_time += 0.01  # Advance by one timer period


def main(args=None) -> None: rclpy.init(args=args) node = JointVelocityPublisher() try: rclpy.spin(node) except KeyboardInterrupt: pass finally: node.destroy_node() rclpy.shutdown()


if __name__ == '__main__': main()


<h1>joint_velocity_subscriber.py</h1>

<h1>Subscribes to joint velocity commands and logs received data.</h1> <h1>ROS 2 Humble | Python 3.10+ | rclpy</h1>


from rclpy.node import Node from rclpy.qos import QoSProfile, ReliabilityPolicy, HistoryPolicy from sensor_msgs.msg import JointState


class JointVelocitySubscriber(Node): """ Subscribes to the /joint_velocity_commands topic and logs each received message. Demonstrates how QoS profiles must be compatible between publisher and subscriber. """


def __init__(self) -> None: super().__init__('joint_velocity_subscriber')


# QoS profile must be compatible with the publisher's profile. # BEST_EFFORT subscriber can receive from BEST_EFFORT publisher. qos_profile = QoSProfile( reliability=ReliabilityPolicy.BEST_EFFORT, history=HistoryPolicy.KEEP_LAST, depth=10, )


self._subscription = self.create_subscription( msg_type=JointState, topic='/joint_velocity_commands', callback=self._velocity_callback, qos_profile=qos_profile, )


self._message_count: int = 0


def _velocity_callback(self, msg: JointState) -> None: """Called each time a new JointState message is received.""" self._message_count += 1


# Log every 100th message to avoid flooding the terminal if self._message_count % 100 == 0: vel_str = ', '.join( f'{name}: {vel:.3f} rad/s' for name, vel in zip(msg.name, msg.velocity) ) self.get_logger().info( f'[msg #{self._message_count}] Velocities: {vel_str}' )


def main(args=None) -> None: rclpy.init(args=args) node = JointVelocitySubscriber() try: rclpy.spin(node) except KeyboardInterrupt: pass finally: node.destroy_node() rclpy.shutdown()


if __name__ == '__main__': main()

3.4.2 Services — Synchronous Request/Response

Services implement a synchronous request-response pattern. A service server advertises a named service with a defined request and response type. A service client sends a request and blocks (or uses a callback) until the server returns a response. Services are appropriate for discrete operations that must complete before the caller can proceed — for example, querying the current kinematic configuration, setting a control mode, or triggering a calibration routine.

Services are not appropriate for continuous data streams, as each transaction requires a round-trip through the communication layer. Calling a service at control-loop frequency (100+ Hz) would impose unacceptable overhead.

<h1>gripper_service.py</h1>

<h1>Demonstrates a service server that opens/closes a gripper on command.</h1> <h1>ROS 2 Humble | Python 3.10+ | rclpy</h1> <h1>Uses the standard SetBool service type: request.data=True opens, False closes.</h1>


from rclpy.node import Node from std_srvs.srv import SetBool


class GripperControlServer(Node): """ Service server that controls a binary gripper (open/closed). Service type: std_srvs/srv/SetBool Service name: /gripper/set_state """


def __init__(self) -> None: super().__init__('gripper_control_server')


# Create the service server self._service = self.create_service( srv_type=SetBool, srv_name='/gripper/set_state', callback=self._handle_gripper_request, )


# Track current gripper state self._is_open: bool = False


self.get_logger().info('GripperControlServer ready on /gripper/set_state')


def _handle_gripper_request( self, request: SetBool.Request, response: SetBool.Response, ) -> SetBool.Response: """ Handles an incoming SetBool request. request.data: True = open gripper, False = close gripper response.success: True if state changed or already in desired state response.message: Human-readable description of the outcome """ desired_open: bool = request.data action_str = 'open' if desired_open else 'close'


if self._is_open == desired_open: # Already in the desired state — no hardware command required response.success = True response.message = f'Gripper already {action_str}ed; no action taken' else: # In a real system, this would send a hardware command to the gripper. # Here we simulate successful execution. self._is_open = desired_open response.success = True response.message = f'Gripper successfully {action_str}ed' self.get_logger().info(response.message)


return response


<h1>--- Client side ---</h1>


class GripperControlClient(Node): """ Service client that sends open/close commands to the GripperControlServer. Demonstrates both synchronous (spin_until_future_complete) and asynchronous usage. """


def __init__(self) -> None: super().__init__('gripper_control_client')


self._client = self.create_client( srv_type=SetBool, srv_name='/gripper/set_state', )


# Wait until the server is available before proceeding while not self._client.wait_for_service(timeout_sec=1.0): self.get_logger().info('Waiting for /gripper/set_state service...')


def send_command(self, open_gripper: bool) -> bool: """ Sends a gripper command and waits for the response. Returns True if the command was accepted successfully. """ request = SetBool.Request() request.data = open_gripper


# send_request is asynchronous; spin_until_future_complete makes it synchronous future = self._client.call_async(request) rclpy.spin_until_future_complete(self, future)


result: SetBool.Response = future.result() self.get_logger().info(f'Service response: {result.message}') return result.success

3.4.3 Actions — Long-Running Tasks with Feedback

Actions are ROS 2's mechanism for long-running tasks that produce intermediate feedback. An action server accepts a goal from an action client, executes it over time while publishing feedback messages, and returns a result when the task completes (or an error if it fails or is cancelled). The client can cancel the goal at any time.

Actions are essential for humanoid robot operations such as navigating to a waypoint, executing a manipulation sequence, or performing a calibration routine — tasks that take seconds to minutes and where the client needs progress updates. Internally, actions are implemented as a combination of three topics and two services, but the rclpy API presents a clean, unified abstraction.

<h1>joint_trajectory_action.py</h1>

<h1>Action server that executes a joint trajectory for a 3-DOF arm.</h1> <h1>ROS 2 Humble | Python 3.10+ | rclpy</h1> <h1>Uses control_msgs/action/FollowJointTrajectory as the action type.</h1> # <h1>NOTE: This example uses a simplified custom action to avoid external dependencies.</h1> <h1>In production, use control_msgs/action/FollowJointTrajectory from ros2_control.</h1>


from rclpy.node import Node from rclpy.action import ActionServer, ActionClient, CancelResponse, GoalResponse from rclpy.action.server import ServerGoalHandle


<h1>We use the standard FollowJointTrajectory action type from control_msgs.</h1> <h1>Ensure ros-humble-control-msgs is installed:</h1> <h1>  sudo apt install ros-humble-control-msgs</h1> from control_msgs.action import FollowJointTrajectory from trajectory_msgs.msg import JointTrajectoryPoint


class JointTrajectoryActionServer(Node): """ Action server that executes joint trajectories for a simulated 3-DOF arm. Publishes feedback (current joint positions) during execution. """


def __init__(self) -> None: super().__init__('joint_trajectory_action_server')


self._action_server = ActionServer( node=self, action_type=FollowJointTrajectory, action_name='/arm_controller/follow_joint_trajectory', execute_callback=self._execute_trajectory, goal_callback=self._goal_callback, cancel_callback=self._cancel_callback, )


# Simulated current joint positions (radians) self._current_positions: list[float] = [0.0, 0.0, 0.0]


self.get_logger().info('JointTrajectoryActionServer ready')


def _goal_callback(self, goal_request: FollowJointTrajectory.Goal) -> GoalResponse: """Called when a new goal arrives. Returns ACCEPT or REJECT.""" num_points = len(goal_request.trajectory.points) self.get_logger().info(f'Received trajectory goal with {num_points} waypoints')


# Reject empty trajectories if num_points == 0: self.get_logger().warn('Rejecting empty trajectory') return GoalResponse.REJECT


return GoalResponse.ACCEPT


def _cancel_callback(self, goal_handle: ServerGoalHandle) -> CancelResponse: """Called when the client requests cancellation. Returns ACCEPT or REJECT.""" self.get_logger().info('Received cancellation request — accepting') return CancelResponse.ACCEPT


def _execute_trajectory( self, goal_handle: ServerGoalHandle ) -> FollowJointTrajectory.Result: """ Main execution callback. Called in a separate thread when a goal is accepted. Iterates through trajectory waypoints, publishing feedback at each step. """ trajectory = goal_handle.request.trajectory feedback_msg = FollowJointTrajectory.Feedback()


self.get_logger().info( f'Executing trajectory with {len(trajectory.points)} waypoints' )


for i, point in enumerate(trajectory.points): # Check if the client has requested cancellation if goal_handle.is_cancel_requested: goal_handle.canceled() self.get_logger().info('Trajectory execution cancelled') return FollowJointTrajectory.Result()


# Simulate moving toward the target point self._current_positions = list(point.positions)


# Publish feedback: current positions, desired positions, and error feedback_msg.joint_names = trajectory.joint_names feedback_msg.actual.positions = self._current_positions feedback_msg.desired.positions = list(point.positions) feedback_msg.error.positions = [0.0] * len(point.positions)


goal_handle.publish_feedback(feedback_msg)


self.get_logger().info( f'Waypoint {i + 1}/{len(trajectory.points)}: ' f'positions = {[f"{p:.3f}" for p in self._current_positions]}' )


# Simulate execution time — in a real system, poll hardware state time.sleep(0.1)


# All waypoints executed successfully goal_handle.succeed() result = FollowJointTrajectory.Result() self.get_logger().info('Trajectory execution completed successfully') return result

3.4.4 Parameters — Runtime Configuration

Parameters allow ROS 2 nodes to expose configuration values that can be read and modified at runtime without restarting the node. Parameters are strongly typed (bool, int, double, string, arrays of each) and are declared by the node at startup. Other nodes can read and set parameters via a standard service interface, and parameter changes can trigger callbacks in the node.

For a humanoid robot, parameters are essential for values that need to be tuned without recompiling — PID controller gains, velocity limits, sensor topic names, and safety thresholds.

<h1>velocity_controller_with_params.py</h1>

<h1>Demonstrates ROS 2 parameters for a joint velocity controller.</h1> <h1>ROS 2 Humble | Python 3.10+ | rclpy</h1>


from rclpy.node import Node from rclpy.parameter import Parameter from rcl_interfaces.msg import ParameterDescriptor, SetParametersResult


class VelocityControllerWithParams(Node): """ A joint velocity controller that exposes its configuration as ROS 2 parameters. Parameters can be set from the command line: ros2 param set /velocity_controller max_velocity 1.5 Or from a YAML parameter file passed at launch time. """


def __init__(self) -> None: super().__init__('velocity_controller')


# --- Declare parameters with types, defaults, and descriptions ---


self.declare_parameter( name='max_velocity', value=1.0,  # rad/s descriptor=ParameterDescriptor( description='Maximum joint velocity in rad/s. Hard limit enforced.', ), )


self.declare_parameter( name='control_frequency', value=100.0,  # Hz descriptor=ParameterDescriptor( description='Control loop update frequency in Hz.', ), )


self.declare_parameter( name='joint_names', value=['shoulder_pitch', 'elbow_flex', 'wrist_roll'], descriptor=ParameterDescriptor( description='List of joint names this controller manages.', ), )


self.declare_parameter( name='enable_safety_limits', value=True, descriptor=ParameterDescriptor( description='If True, velocity commands are clamped to max_velocity.', ), )


# Register a callback invoked when any parameter changes at runtime self.add_on_set_parameters_callback(self._on_parameter_change)


# Read initial parameter values into instance variables self._load_parameters()


# Start the control loop timer using the declared frequency period = 1.0 / self._control_frequency self._timer = self.create_timer(period, self._control_loop)


self.get_logger().info( f'VelocityController started: ' f'frequency={self._control_frequency} Hz, ' f'max_velocity={self._max_velocity} rad/s, ' f'joints={self._joint_names}' )


def _load_parameters(self) -> None: """Read current parameter values into instance variables.""" self._max_velocity: float = ( self.get_parameter('max_velocity').get_parameter_value().double_value ) self._control_frequency: float = ( self.get_parameter('control_frequency').get_parameter_value().double_value ) self._joint_names: list[str] = ( self.get_parameter('joint_names').get_parameter_value().string_array_value ) self._enable_safety_limits: bool = ( self.get_parameter('enable_safety_limits') .get_parameter_value() .bool_value )


def _on_parameter_change( self, params: list[Parameter] ) -> SetParametersResult: """ Callback invoked before a parameter change is committed. Validate new values and return success/failure. """ for param in params: if param.name == 'max_velocity': if param.value <= 0.0 or param.value > 5.0: return SetParametersResult( successful=False, reason='max_velocity must be in range (0.0, 5.0] rad/s', ) if param.name == 'control_frequency': if param.value <= 0.0 or param.value > 1000.0: return SetParametersResult( successful=False, reason='control_frequency must be in range (0.0, 1000.0] Hz', )


# All validations passed — reload parameters into instance variables self._load_parameters() self.get_logger().info(f'Parameters updated: {[p.name for p in params]}') return SetParametersResult(successful=True)


def _control_loop(self) -> None: """Main control loop callback. In a real controller, read state and publish commands.""" # Placeholder for actual control logic pass

3.5 Node Lifecycle Management

Standard ROS 2 nodes start executing immediately when created and continue until the process terminates. For production systems, this is insufficient. A humanoid robot powering on needs a deterministic, ordered startup sequence: sensors must be initialized before perception algorithms read from them, hardware interfaces must be verified before controllers command them, and the system must be able to transition to a safe, low-power state without a full shutdown.

Lifecycle nodes [6] (also called managed nodes) implement a finite state machine that gives external orchestrators fine-grained control over node execution. The four primary states are: Unconfigured — The node has been constructed but not yet initialized. No resources (timers, publishers, subscribers, hardware connections) have been allocated. Inactive — The node has been configured (resources allocated, parameters loaded, hardware connections verified) but is not yet processing data or publishing commands. This is a "ready but paused" state. Active — The node is fully operational: processing data, publishing outputs, executing control loops. Finalized — The node has been cleanly shut down. All resources have been released.

Transitions between states are triggered by external commands (configure, activate, deactivate, cleanup, shutdown) and can be automated by a launch system or a system health manager node.

Figure 3.3 — Lifecycle Node State Machine 
                    ┌───────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐

│               LIFECYCLE NODE STATE MACHINE             │ └───────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘


[START] │ ▼ ┌─────────────┐  configure cmd     ┌─────────────┐  activate cmd     ┌─────────────┐ │             │  on_configure()    │             │  on_activate()    │             │ │UNCONFIGURED │ ─────────────────► │  INACTIVE   │ ────────────────► │   ACTIVE    │ │             │                   │             │                   │             │ └─────────────┘                   └─────────────┘                   └─────────────┘ │                                 ▲ │                               │ │ │                     cleanup cmd │ │ deactivate cmd                │ │ │                     on_cleanup()│ │ on_deactivate()               │ │ │                                 │ ▼                               │ │ │                           ┌─────────────┐                        │ │ │                           │  INACTIVE   │◄───────────────────────┘ │ │                           │  (returned) │    deactivate             │ │                           └─────────────┘                          │ │                                                                     │ │  shutdown cmd            ┌─────────────┐    shutdown cmd            │ │  on_shutdown()           │             │    on_shutdown()            │ └─────────────────────────►│  FINALIZED  │◄───────────────────────────┘ │             │ └─────────────┘


Error Handling: Any state ──► ErrorProcessing ──► (recover) ──► UNCONFIGURED ──► (fail)    ──► FINALIZED


Transition callbacks: on_configure()    — allocate resources, open hardware interfaces on_activate()     — start publishing, enable control loops on_deactivate()   — pause publishing, disable control loops on_cleanup()      — deallocate resources, close hardware interfaces on_shutdown()     — emergency stop, save state if needed
Figure 3.3: Lifecycle Node state machine with four primary states (Unconfigured, Inactive, Active, Finalized) and five transition callbacks. Error handling transitions allow nodes to recover to Unconfigured or safely reach Finalized on unrecoverable failure. 
<h1>imu_lifecycle_node.py</h1>

<h1>Demonstrates a lifecycle node for an IMU sensor driver.</h1> <h1>ROS 2 Humble | Python 3.10+ | rclpy</h1>


from rclpy.lifecycle import LifecycleNode, TransitionCallbackReturn, State from sensor_msgs.msg import Imu


class ImuLifecycleNode(LifecycleNode): """ Lifecycle-managed IMU sensor node.


State transitions: Unconfigured -> Inactive:  on_configure()  — open device, declare params Inactive -> Active:        on_activate()   — start publishing Active -> Inactive:        on_deactivate() — stop publishing, device idle Inactive -> Unconfigured:  on_cleanup()    — close device, release resources Any -> Finalized:          on_shutdown()   — emergency stop """


def __init__(self, node_name: str = 'imu_driver') -> None: super().__init__(node_name) self._publisher = None self._timer = None self._device_handle = None  # Simulated hardware handle


def on_configure(self, state: State) -> TransitionCallbackReturn: """ Called when the 'configure' transition is triggered. Allocate resources: open hardware device, declare parameters, create publisher. Return SUCCESS to proceed to Inactive, FAILURE to stay in Unconfigured. """ self.get_logger().info('Configuring IMU node...')


# Declare parameters self.declare_parameter('imu_frame', 'imu_link') self.declare_parameter('publish_frequency', 200.0)  # Hz


# Create publisher — allocated in Inactive state but not publishing yet self._publisher = self.create_lifecycle_publisher( msg_type=Imu, topic='/imu/data', qos_profile=10, )


# Simulate hardware initialization self._device_handle = True  # In real code: open serial/I2C connection if not self._device_handle: self.get_logger().error('Failed to open IMU device') return TransitionCallbackReturn.FAILURE


self.get_logger().info('IMU node configured successfully') return TransitionCallbackReturn.SUCCESS


def on_activate(self, state: State) -> TransitionCallbackReturn: """ Called when the 'activate' transition is triggered. Start the publishing timer. Node is now fully operational. """ self.get_logger().info('Activating IMU node...')


frequency = self.get_parameter('publish_frequency').value period = 1.0 / frequency


self._timer = self.create_timer(period, self._publish_imu_data)


# Activate the lifecycle publisher (required step) super().on_activate(state)


self.get_logger().info(f'IMU node active at {frequency} Hz') return TransitionCallbackReturn.SUCCESS


def on_deactivate(self, state: State) -> TransitionCallbackReturn: """ Called when the 'deactivate' transition is triggered. Stop the timer but keep hardware connection open. """ self.get_logger().info('Deactivating IMU node...')


if self._timer is not None: self._timer.cancel() self._timer = None


super().on_deactivate(state) self.get_logger().info('IMU node deactivated (device still connected)') return TransitionCallbackReturn.SUCCESS


def on_cleanup(self, state: State) -> TransitionCallbackReturn: """ Called when the 'cleanup' transition is triggered. Release all resources. Node returns to Unconfigured. """ self.get_logger().info('Cleaning up IMU node...') self._publisher = None self._device_handle = None  # In real code: close hardware connection return TransitionCallbackReturn.SUCCESS


def on_shutdown(self, state: State) -> TransitionCallbackReturn: """Emergency shutdown from any state.""" self.get_logger().info('Shutting down IMU node') return TransitionCallbackReturn.SUCCESS


def _publish_imu_data(self) -> None: """Timer callback: reads IMU hardware and publishes a sensor_msgs/Imu message.""" if not self._publisher or not self._publisher.is_activated: return


msg = Imu() msg.header.stamp = self.get_clock().now().to_msg() msg.header.frame_id = self.get_parameter('imu_frame').value


# In a real driver, read actual sensor registers here. # Placeholder: zero acceleration and angular velocity msg.linear_acceleration.x = 0.0 msg.linear_acceleration.y = 0.0 msg.linear_acceleration.z = 9.81  # Gravity


self._publisher.publish(msg)


def main(args=None) -> None: rclpy.init(args=args) node = ImuLifecycleNode() try: rclpy.spin(node) except KeyboardInterrupt: pass finally: node.destroy_node() rclpy.shutdown()


if __name__ == '__main__': main()

3.6 Package Structure and Build System

ROS 2 software is organized into packages — the fundamental unit of build, distribution, and dependency management. Every package contains source code, resource files (meshes, configuration, launch files), and a package.xml manifest that declares the package's metadata and dependencies.

3.6.1 Package Types

ROS 2 supports three package build types, declared in package.xml:

ament_cmake — For C++ packages (and mixed C++/Python packages). Uses CMake as the underlying build system with ament's extensions for dependency management and installation conventions. ament_python — For pure Python packages. Uses Python's setuptools under the hood and follows standard Python packaging conventions. Recommended for Python-only nodes. cmake — For packages that use plain CMake without ament extensions. Rarely used in new ROS 2 code.

3.6.2 package.xml Structure

Every package has a package.xml file in its root directory that declares:

<?xml version="1.0"?>

<?xml-model href="http://download.ros.org/schema/package_format3.xsd" schematypens="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema"?> <package format="3"> <!-- Package identity --> <name>humanoid_arm_controller</name> <version>0.1.0</version> <description> Joint velocity and trajectory controller for a 3-DOF humanoid arm. Implements ROS 2 Humble lifecycle nodes and ros2_control integration. </description>


<!-- Maintainer and license information (required for release) --> <maintainer email="faisal@example.com">Muhammad Faisal Laiq Siddiqui</maintainer> <license>Apache-2.0</license>


<!-- Build-time dependencies: tools and libraries needed at build/test time --> <buildtool_depend>ament_python</buildtool_depend>


<!-- Runtime dependencies: required when executing the node --> <depend>rclpy</depend> <depend>sensor_msgs</depend> <depend>control_msgs</depend> <depend>trajectory_msgs</depend> <depend>std_srvs</depend> <depend>rcl_interfaces</depend>


<!-- Test dependencies: only needed during testing --> <test_depend>ament_copyright</test_depend> <test_depend>ament_flake8</test_depend> <test_depend>ament_pep257</test_depend> <test_depend>pytest</test_depend>


<!-- Export: declares this package uses ament_python build type --> <export> <build_type>ament_python</build_type> </export> </package>

3.6.3 Directory Structure for an ament_python Package

humanoid_arm_controller/          # Package root (matches <name> in package.xml)

├── package.xml                   # Package manifest ├── setup.py                      # Python package setup (entry points for nodes) ├── setup.cfg                     # Configuration for setuptools ├── resource/ │   └── humanoid_arm_controller   # Empty marker file required by ament ├── humanoid_arm_controller/      # Python package (same name as ROS package) │   ├── __init__.py │   ├── joint_velocity_publisher.py │   ├── joint_velocity_subscriber.py │   ├── gripper_service.py │   ├── imu_lifecycle_node.py │   └── velocity_controller_with_params.py ├── launch/ │   └── arm_controller.launch.py  # Launch file (Python, not XML) ├── config/ │   └── controller_params.yaml    # Parameter file └── test/ ├── test_copyright.py ├── test_flake8.py └── test_pep257.py

3.6.4 The colcon Build Tool

colcon (collective construction) [7] is the build tool used for ROS 2 workspaces. Unlike ROS 1's catkin_make, colcon supports building packages in parallel and handles packages of different build types in the same workspace. Common colcon commands: 
<h1>Build all packages in the workspace</h1>

colcon build


<h1>Build only specific packages (and their dependencies)</h1> colcon build --packages-select humanoid_arm_controller


<h1>Build with symlinks instead of copying files (faster iteration for Python packages)</h1> colcon build --symlink-install


<h1>Run tests for all packages</h1> colcon test


<h1>Show test results</h1> colcon test-result --verbose

After building, source the workspace overlay to make the packages available:

source install/setup.bash

3.8 Chapter Summary

ROS 2 is the software layer that transforms a collection of sensors, actuators, and compute modules into a coordinated robot. Its architecture — peer-to-peer DDS communication without a central broker, Quality of Service policies that match delivery semantics to real-time requirements, and lifecycle management that enforces deterministic startup and shutdown — was engineered specifically to solve the limitations that made ROS 1 inadequate for production deployment.

The middleware foundation begins with the DDS standard: why it exists, why ROS 2 chose it over a custom transport, and how the RTPS protocol enables multi-host, multi-robot coordination without a rosmaster equivalent. Fast DDS and Cyclone DDS differ in latency profiles and configuration flexibility, but both implement the same QoS policy vocabulary — and choosing the right QoS profile for each topic (SENSOR_DATA for camera streams, SERVICES_DEFAULT for configuration commands) is the difference between a system that degrades gracefully and one that stalls unpredictably.

The DDS layer received detailed treatment: the RTPS wire protocol, vendor implementations (Fast DDS and Cyclone DDS), Quality of Service policies that give developers precise control over delivery semantics, and domain-based isolation for multi-robot systems.

We implemented all four communication primitives in Python:

  • Topics for asynchronous streaming of joint velocity commands at 100 Hz
  • Services for synchronous gripper control commands
  • Actions for trajectory execution with intermediate feedback and cancellation support
  • Parameters for runtime-configurable controller gains with validation callbacks

Lifecycle nodes were introduced as the mechanism for deterministic startup and shutdown in production systems — essential when a 44-DOF humanoid cannot afford unordered initialization or uncontrolled power-down sequences.

The build system section covered package types, package.xml structure, directory conventions for ament_python packages, and the colcon build tool. A complete, reproducible installation guide for ROS 2 Humble on Ubuntu 22.04 was provided.

Chapter 4 builds directly on this foundation: we move from ROS 2 communication architecture into the practical challenge of building humanoid robot control software with rclpy. We will implement multi-threaded executors, integrate with the ros2_control framework, author a complete URDF for a simplified humanoid arm, and connect motion planning through MoveIt 2.

References
