Chapter 4: Building Humanoid Control with rclpy

Part II — The Robotic Nervous System
Version 1.0 — Draft | Author: Muhammad Faisal Laiq Siddiqui

4.1 Introduction — From ROS 2 Theory to Humanoid Control Practice

Chapter 3 established the architectural foundation: DDS-based communication, the four primitives (topics, services, actions, parameters), lifecycle nodes, and the workspace build system. That foundation is necessary, but it is not sufficient. Building software that actually controls a humanoid robot — with 28 to 44 degrees of freedom moving simultaneously, real-time force feedback from hands, and navigation through a hospital corridor — requires a deeper layer of engineering.

In this chapter, we transition from theory to practice. We will write Python nodes with proper type annotations, implement multi-threaded execution strategies to prevent callback starvation, integrate with the ros2_control framework that manages hardware interfaces, author a complete URDF (Unified Robot Description Format) description for a simplified humanoid arm, and connect motion planning through MoveIt 2. We close with Nav2 for navigation — with an honest discussion of why Nav2, designed primarily for wheeled robots, requires significant adaptation for bipedal humanoids.

Every code block in this chapter is written to ROS 2 Humble standards [1], targeting Python 3.10+ with full type annotations. The examples are designed to be runnable in a real workspace with the dependencies installed in Chapter 3.

A clarifying note on scope: this chapter covers the software layer that commands a robot's joints, plans motions, and coordinates behavior. It does not cover the mechanical design of the joints themselves, the power electronics, or the actuator firmware — those subjects belong to robotics hardware engineering texts. Our focus is the software that bridges between high-level task commands and the hardware abstraction layer provided by ros2_control.

4.2 Python Node Development — Complete Publisher and Subscriber with Type Annotations

Before tackling multi-threading and hardware interfaces, we establish the correct pattern for writing production-quality Python nodes in ROS 2. The examples in Chapter 3 introduced the basics; here we add type annotations, proper error handling, and the structural patterns that make nodes maintainable.

4.2.1 The Joint State Monitor Node

The following is a complete, production-quality node that subscribes to joint states, tracks joint positions over time, and publishes a simple status message. It demonstrates the structural patterns we will use throughout the chapter.

<h1>joint_state_monitor.py</h1>

<h1>Monitors joint states for a humanoid arm and publishes health status.</h1> <h1>ROS 2 Humble | Python 3.10+ | rclpy</h1> # <h1>Installation:</h1> <h1>  sudo apt install ros-humble-sensor-msgs ros-humble-std-msgs</h1> <h1>  colcon build --packages-select humanoid_arm_controller</h1>


from __future__ import annotations


from typing import Optional


from rclpy.node import Node from rclpy.qos import QoSProfile, ReliabilityPolicy, DurabilityPolicy, HistoryPolicy from sensor_msgs.msg import JointState from std_msgs.msg import String


<h1>Type alias for joint position dictionary: maps joint name to position in radians</h1> JointPositionMap = dict[str, float]


<h1>Joint position limits for a simplified 3-DOF humanoid arm (in radians)</h1> <h1>These limits are illustrative; real limits come from URDF joint tags.</h1> JOINT_LIMITS: dict[str, tuple[float, float]] = { 'shoulder_pitch': (-2.617, 2.617),   # ±150 degrees 'elbow_flex':     (-0.087, 2.967),   # -5 to +170 degrees 'wrist_roll':     (-3.141, 3.141),   # ±180 degrees }


class JointStateMonitor(Node): """ Monitors joint states published on /joint_states and: 1. Detects joints approaching their position limits (within 10 degrees) 2. Publishes a warning string on /arm/health_status when a limit is approached 3. Logs message receipt rate every 5 seconds


Subscriptions: /joint_states  (sensor_msgs/JointState) — joint positions, velocities, efforts


Publications: /arm/health_status  (std_msgs/String) — health warnings, JSON-like format """


def __init__(self) -> None: super().__init__('joint_state_monitor')


# --- QoS profiles --- # Joint state topic: high frequency, best-effort is acceptable sensor_qos = QoSProfile( reliability=ReliabilityPolicy.BEST_EFFORT, durability=DurabilityPolicy.VOLATILE, history=HistoryPolicy.KEEP_LAST, depth=10, )


# Health status: reliable, so downstream monitors never miss a warning status_qos = QoSProfile( reliability=ReliabilityPolicy.RELIABLE, durability=DurabilityPolicy.VOLATILE, history=HistoryPolicy.KEEP_LAST, depth=10, )


# --- Subscriptions --- self._joint_state_sub = self.create_subscription( msg_type=JointState, topic='/joint_states', callback=self._joint_state_callback, qos_profile=sensor_qos, )


# --- Publishers --- self._health_pub = self.create_publisher( msg_type=String, topic='/arm/health_status', qos_profile=status_qos, )


# --- Internal state --- self._current_positions: JointPositionMap = {} self._message_count: int = 0 self._warning_threshold_rad: float = math.radians(10.0)  # 10-degree warning zone


# --- Diagnostic timer: log rate every 5 seconds --- self._rate_timer = self.create_timer(5.0, self._log_message_rate) self._last_rate_check_count: int = 0


self.get_logger().info('JointStateMonitor initialized')


def _joint_state_callback(self, msg: JointState) -> None: """ Primary callback for joint state messages. Called at whatever frequency the hardware or simulation publishes joint states (typically 100–500 Hz for a real robot, or up to 1000 Hz in simulation). """ self._message_count += 1


# Update our position map from the received message for name, position in zip(msg.name, msg.position): self._current_positions[name] = position


# Check for joints approaching limits warnings = self._check_position_limits() if warnings: status_msg = String() status_msg.data = ( f'{{"timestamp": "{self.get_clock().now().nanoseconds}", ' f'"warnings": {warnings}}}' ) self._health_pub.publish(status_msg)


def _check_position_limits(self) -> list[str]: """ Checks all monitored joints against their limits. Returns a list of warning strings for joints within the warning threshold. """ warnings: list[str] = []


for joint_name, position in self._current_positions.items(): if joint_name not in JOINT_LIMITS: continue  # Only check joints we have limits for


lower_limit, upper_limit = JOINT_LIMITS[joint_name]


# Check proximity to lower limit if position < lower_limit + self._warning_threshold_rad: margin_deg = math.degrees(position - lower_limit) warnings.append( f'{joint_name}_near_lower_limit_{margin_deg:.1f}deg' )


# Check proximity to upper limit elif position > upper_limit - self._warning_threshold_rad: margin_deg = math.degrees(upper_limit - position) warnings.append( f'{joint_name}_near_upper_limit_{margin_deg:.1f}deg' )


return warnings


def _log_message_rate(self) -> None: """Timer callback: logs joint state message frequency every 5 seconds.""" messages_in_period = self._message_count - self._last_rate_check_count rate_hz = messages_in_period / 5.0 self._last_rate_check_count = self._message_count


self.get_logger().info( f'Joint state receive rate: {rate_hz:.1f} Hz ' f'(total messages: {self._message_count})' )


@property def current_positions(self) -> JointPositionMap: """Read-only access to the latest joint positions.""" return dict(self._current_positions)


def main(args: Optional[list[str]] = None) -> None: rclpy.init(args=args) node = JointStateMonitor() try: rclpy.spin(node) except KeyboardInterrupt: pass finally: node.destroy_node() rclpy.shutdown()


if __name__ == '__main__': main()

4.3 Multi-Threaded Execution — Why It Matters for Real-Time Humanoid Control

The rclpy.spin() function used in Chapter 3's examples processes all callbacks in a single thread, one at a time. For a simple sensor logger, this is adequate. For a humanoid robot, it is a recipe for failure.

Consider a node that simultaneously:

  • Subscribes to joint states at 500 Hz (every 2 ms)
  • Provides a service to change control mode (occasionally, but must respond within 50 ms)
  • Runs a 100 Hz control loop timer (every 10 ms)

With single-threaded execution, if the joint state callback takes 3 ms to process, the control loop timer's callback may miss its 10 ms deadline. Worse, a service call that arrives during a long perception computation will wait in the queue, potentially violating safety response time requirements.

ROS 2 solves this with MultiThreadedExecutor and callback groups [2].

4.3.1 Callback Groups

A callback group is an assignment of callbacks to either of two execution policies:

MutuallyExclusiveCallbackGroup — Only one callback in this group executes at a time. This is the default behavior and is safe for any callback that modifies shared state, since no two callbacks in the group can run concurrently. ReentrantCallbackGroup — Multiple callbacks in this group can execute concurrently. This is appropriate for stateless callbacks (e.g., publishing a message based only on the received data), but requires the developer to protect shared state with locks.

4.3.2 Multi-Threaded Humanoid Control Node

<h1>multithreaded_humanoid_controller.py</h1>

<h1>Demonstrates MultiThreadedExecutor and callback groups for a humanoid arm controller.</h1> <h1>ROS 2 Humble | Python 3.10+ | rclpy</h1>


from __future__ import annotations


from typing import Optional


from rclpy.node import Node from rclpy.executors import MultiThreadedExecutor from rclpy.callback_groups import MutuallyExclusiveCallbackGroup, ReentrantCallbackGroup from rclpy.qos import QoSProfile, ReliabilityPolicy, HistoryPolicy from sensor_msgs.msg import JointState from std_msgs.msg import Float64MultiArray from std_srvs.srv import SetBool


class MultithreadedArmController(Node): """ Humanoid arm controller demonstrating multi-threaded execution.


Three callback groups: 1. sensor_group (MutuallyExclusive): Processes joint state callbacks. Accesses self._current_positions — must not run concurrently with itself. 2. control_group (MutuallyExclusive): Runs the 100 Hz control loop timer. Reads self._current_positions — must not run while joint state is updating. 3. service_group (Reentrant): Handles control mode service requests. Stateless — can run concurrently with other service calls.


By placing sensor_group and control_group in separate MutuallyExclusive groups, the joint state callback and control loop can run concurrently (in different threads), but two joint state callbacks will never overlap each other. """


def __init__(self) -> None: super().__init__('multithreaded_arm_controller')


# --- Callback groups --- # Separate group for sensor callbacks so they don't block the control loop self._sensor_group = MutuallyExclusiveCallbackGroup() # Separate group for the control loop timer self._control_group = MutuallyExclusiveCallbackGroup() # Reentrant group for service calls (stateless) self._service_group = ReentrantCallbackGroup()


# --- Shared state protected by a lock --- self._state_lock = threading.Lock() self._current_positions: list[float] = [0.0, 0.0, 0.0] self._control_enabled: bool = False


# --- QoS for joint states: high-frequency sensor data --- sensor_qos = QoSProfile( reliability=ReliabilityPolicy.BEST_EFFORT, history=HistoryPolicy.KEEP_LAST, depth=5, )


# --- Subscription to joint states — uses sensor_group --- self._joint_state_sub = self.create_subscription( msg_type=JointState, topic='/joint_states', callback=self._joint_state_callback, qos_profile=sensor_qos, callback_group=self._sensor_group, )


# --- Publisher for velocity commands --- self._velocity_pub = self.create_publisher( msg_type=Float64MultiArray, topic='/forward_velocity_controller/commands', qos_profile=10, )


# --- Control loop timer at 100 Hz — uses control_group --- self._control_timer = self.create_timer( timer_period_sec=0.01, callback=self._control_loop, callback_group=self._control_group, )


# --- Service to enable/disable control — uses service_group --- self._enable_service = self.create_service( srv_type=SetBool, srv_name='/arm_controller/enable', callback=self._handle_enable_request, callback_group=self._service_group, )


self.get_logger().info( 'MultithreadedArmController ready. ' 'POST to /arm_controller/enable to start.' )


def _joint_state_callback(self, msg: JointState) -> None: """ Runs in the sensor_group thread. Updates shared state under lock — safe for concurrent reads by control loop. """ with self._state_lock: # Extract positions for our three arm joints for i, name in enumerate(msg.name): if name == 'shoulder_pitch' and len(msg.position) > i: self._current_positions[0] = msg.position[i] elif name == 'elbow_flex' and len(msg.position) > i: self._current_positions[1] = msg.position[i] elif name == 'wrist_roll' and len(msg.position) > i: self._current_positions[2] = msg.position[i]


def _control_loop(self) -> None: """ Runs in the control_group thread at 100 Hz. Reads shared state under lock, computes velocity commands, publishes them.


This callback runs CONCURRENTLY with _joint_state_callback because they are in DIFFERENT MutuallyExclusive groups. The lock ensures safe data access. """ if not self._control_enabled: return


# Read current state under lock with self._state_lock: positions = list(self._current_positions)


# Simple proportional controller: drive joints toward zero (home position) target_positions = [0.0, 0.5, 0.0]  # elbow slightly bent kp = 2.0  # Proportional gain — rad/s per rad of error


velocity_commands = [ kp * (target - current) for target, current in zip(target_positions, positions) ]


# Clamp to maximum velocity max_vel = 1.0  # rad/s velocity_commands = [ max(-max_vel, min(max_vel, v)) for v in velocity_commands ]


# Publish commands cmd_msg = Float64MultiArray() cmd_msg.data = velocity_commands self._velocity_pub.publish(cmd_msg)


def _handle_enable_request( self, request: SetBool.Request, response: SetBool.Response, ) -> SetBool.Response: """ Runs in the service_group thread (potentially reentrant). Enables or disables the control loop. """ self._control_enabled = request.data action = 'enabled' if request.data else 'disabled' response.success = True response.message = f'Arm controller {action}' self.get_logger().info(response.message) return response


def main(args: Optional[list[str]] = None) -> None: rclpy.init(args=args) node = MultithreadedArmController()


# MultiThreadedExecutor with 4 threads. # Each callback group's callbacks are dispatched to available threads. # The sensor_group and control_group can run simultaneously because they # have different group assignments and different threads are available. executor = MultiThreadedExecutor(num_threads=4) executor.add_node(node)


try: executor.spin() except KeyboardInterrupt: pass finally: executor.shutdown() node.destroy_node() rclpy.shutdown()


if __name__ == '__main__': main()
Figure 4.1 — MultiThreadedExecutor Callback Dispatch 
MultiThreadedExecutor  (max_threads=4)

│ ├── Thread Pool: [Thread-0] [Thread-1] [Thread-2] [Thread-3] │ ├── CALLBACK GROUP: sensor_queue  (MutuallyExclusive) │     ├── imu_callback()         → dispatched to Thread-0 │     └── joint_state_callback() → waits for Thread-0 to finish │                                  (MutuallyExclusive: only one at a time) │ ├── CALLBACK GROUP: control_queue (MutuallyExclusive) │     └── control_loop_callback() → dispatched to Thread-1 (simultaneously!) │                                   ↑ does NOT block sensor_queue │ └── CALLBACK GROUP: service_queue  (Reentrant) ├── get_status_callback()  → dispatched to Thread-2 └── set_param_callback()   → dispatched to Thread-3 (simultaneously!) (Reentrant: multiple allowed in parallel)


Shared State Access: ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │  self._joint_positions (shared between sensor+ctrl) │ │  protected by: threading.Lock()                     │ │    with self._lock:  ← acquires before read/write   │ │       self._joint_positions = new_data              │ └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘


Result: sensor callbacks and control callbacks run in parallel; shared state is protected; services never block control loop.
Figure 4.1: MultiThreadedExecutor dispatching callbacks from three callback groups to a pool of four threads. Sensor and control callback groups (MutuallyExclusive) run simultaneously on separate threads, while the Reentrant service group allows multiple service callbacks in parallel. A threading.Lock guards shared joint position state.

4.4 The ros2_control Framework

ros2_control [3] is the standard framework for hardware abstraction and deterministic control in ROS 2. It provides a clean separation between the controller (which implements control algorithms and is unaware of specific hardware) and the hardware interface (which implements hardware-specific read/write operations). This separation allows controllers to be developed and tested independently of the robot hardware.

4.4.1 Architecture Overview

The ros2_control architecture has three main components:

Hardware Interface — A C++ plugin that implements the hardware_interface::SystemInterface (or SensorInterface, ActuatorInterface) abstract class. It communicates with physical hardware via CAN bus, EtherCAT, serial, or any other protocol, and exposes the robot's state and command channels through StateInterface and CommandInterface objects. Controller Manager — A ROS 2 node that loads hardware interface plugins and controller plugins, manages their lifecycle, and orchestrates the real-time control loop. The control loop runs at a configurable rate (typically 100–1000 Hz) and calls hardware.read(), then controller.update(), then hardware.write() in sequence. Controllers — C++ or Python plugins that implement controller_interface::ControllerInterface. They read state from state interfaces and write commands to command interfaces. ROS 2 ships with a library of standard controllers: JointTrajectoryController, ForwardCommandController, JointStateBroadcaster, and others. Figure 4.2 — ros2_control Architecture 
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐

│                         LAYER 1: ROS 2 Interface                            │ │                                                                             │ │   /joint_trajectory   /joint_states   /controller_manager/list_controllers  │ │   (action server)     (publisher)     (service)                             │ └────────────────────────────┬────────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │ ┌────────────────────────────▼────────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │                     LAYER 2: Controller Manager                             │ │                                                                             │ │  ┌──────────────────────────┐    ┌──────────────────────────────────┐      │ │  │  JointStateBroadcaster   │    │  JointTrajectoryController       │      │ │  │  (reads state interfaces)│    │  (writes command interfaces)     │      │ │  │  → publishes /joint_states│   │  → accepts /joint_trajectory     │      │ │  └──────────────────────────┘    └──────────────────────────────────┘      │ │                                                                             │ │  Control loop: READ → UPDATE → WRITE   @ 500 Hz (2ms cycle time)           │ └────────────────────────────┬────────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │  state/command interfaces ┌────────────────────────────▼────────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │                     LAYER 3: Hardware Interface Plugin                      │ │                                                                             │ │  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐      │ │  │  HumanoidHardware (custom plugin: SystemInterface)               │      │ │  │    read()  → poll joint encoders, F/T sensors                    │      │ │  │    write() → send torque/position commands to actuators          │      │ │  └────────────────────────────┬─────────────────────────────────────┘      │ └───────────────────────────────┼─────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │ CAN bus / EtherCAT / serial ┌──────────▼─────────────┐ │  Physical / Simulated   │ │  Joints (28–43 DOF)     │ │  [Gazebo / Real Robot]  │ └─────────────────────────┘
Figure 4.2: Three-layer ros2_control architecture. The Controller Manager hosts loaded controller plugins (JointStateBroadcaster and JointTrajectoryController) that communicate with the hardware via a Hardware Interface plugin at 500 Hz. The same controller stack works identically with Gazebo simulation or physical hardware.

4.4.2 Hardware Interface Implementation

While hardware interfaces are typically written in C++ for maximum performance, the following Python pseudocode illustrates the conceptual structure. Production implementations should use the C++ API for real-time safety.

<h1>hardware_interface_concept.py</h1>

<h1>Conceptual illustration of a ros2_control hardware interface.</h1> <h1>NOTE: Production hardware interfaces are written in C++ for real-time performance.</h1> <h1>This Python pseudocode illustrates the interface contract.</h1> # <h1>The actual C++ base class is hardware_interface::SystemInterface from:</h1> <h1>  ros-humble-hardware-interface</h1>


from dataclasses import dataclass, field from typing import Protocol


@dataclass class StateInterface: """ Exposes a read-only state value from hardware (e.g., current joint position). The Controller Manager makes these values available to all loaded controllers. """ joint_name: str       # e.g., "shoulder_pitch" interface_name: str   # e.g., "position", "velocity", "effort" value: float = 0.0    # Current value, updated by hardware.read()


@dataclass class CommandInterface: """ Exposes a write-only command channel to hardware (e.g., target joint velocity). Controllers write to these; hardware.write() sends them to actuators. """ joint_name: str interface_name: str   # e.g., "velocity", "position", "effort" value: float = 0.0    # Commanded value, written by the active controller


class HumanoidArmHardwareInterface: """ Conceptual hardware interface for a 3-DOF humanoid arm. In production C++: class HumanoidArmHardware : public hardware_interface::SystemInterface { ... }


The three lifecycle methods correspond to ros2_control lifecycle transitions: on_configure()  — open hardware connection, allocate buffers on_activate()   — enable servos, start real-time loop on_deactivate() — disable servos safely """


JOINT_NAMES = ['shoulder_pitch', 'elbow_flex', 'wrist_roll']


def __init__(self) -> None: # State interfaces: position and velocity for each joint self.state_interfaces: list[StateInterface] = [ StateInterface(joint, interface_type) for joint in self.JOINT_NAMES for interface_type in ['position', 'velocity', 'effort'] ]


# Command interfaces: velocity commands for each joint self.command_interfaces: list[CommandInterface] = [ CommandInterface(joint, 'velocity') for joint in self.JOINT_NAMES ]


def read(self, time: float, period: float) -> None: """ Called by Controller Manager at the start of each control cycle. Read current joint states from hardware and update state_interfaces. """ for state_if in self.state_interfaces: if state_if.interface_name == 'position': # In production: read encoder register via CAN/EtherCAT state_if.value = self._read_encoder(state_if.joint_name) elif state_if.interface_name == 'velocity': state_if.value = self._read_velocity(state_if.joint_name) elif state_if.interface_name == 'effort': state_if.value = self._read_current(state_if.joint_name)


def write(self, time: float, period: float) -> None: """ Called by Controller Manager at the end of each control cycle. Send command_interfaces values to hardware actuators. """ for cmd_if in self.command_interfaces: # In production: write to motor driver register via CAN/EtherCAT self._send_velocity_command(cmd_if.joint_name, cmd_if.value)


def _read_encoder(self, joint_name: str) -> float: """Hardware-specific encoder read — implementation varies by bus type.""" return 0.0  # Placeholder


def _read_velocity(self, joint_name: str) -> float: return 0.0  # Placeholder


def _read_current(self, joint_name: str) -> float: return 0.0  # Placeholder


def _send_velocity_command(self, joint_name: str, velocity: float) -> None: pass  # Placeholder

4.4.3 URDF Integration with ros2_control Tags

The ros2_control framework discovers hardware interfaces through tags embedded in the robot's URDF file. The following is the ros2_control section for the 3-DOF arm:

<!-- ros2_control section for 3-DOF humanoid arm -->

<!-- Embedded in the URDF file (see Section 4.5 for the full URDF) -->


<ros2_control name="HumanoidArmSystem" type="system"> <hardware> <!-- Plugin name must match the C++ class exported via pluginlib --> <plugin>humanoid_arm_hardware/HumanoidArmHardware</plugin> <!-- Hardware-specific parameters passed to on_configure() --> <param name="serial_port">/dev/ttyUSB0</param> <param name="baud_rate">1000000</param> <param name="control_frequency">500</param> </hardware>


<!-- Joint: shoulder_pitch --> <joint name="shoulder_pitch"> <command_interface name="velocity"> <param name="min">-3.14</param>  <!-- rad/s --> <param name="max">3.14</param> </command_interface> <state_interface name="position"/> <state_interface name="velocity"/> <state_interface name="effort"/> </joint>


<!-- Joint: elbow_flex --> <joint name="elbow_flex"> <command_interface name="velocity"> <param name="min">-3.14</param> <param name="max">3.14</param> </command_interface> <state_interface name="position"/> <state_interface name="velocity"/> <state_interface name="effort"/> </joint>


<!-- Joint: wrist_roll --> <joint name="wrist_roll"> <command_interface name="velocity"> <param name="min">-6.28</param> <param name="max">6.28</param> </command_interface> <state_interface name="position"/> <state_interface name="velocity"/> <state_interface name="effort"/> </joint> </ros2_control>

4.5 URDF Authoring for a Simplified 3-DOF Humanoid Arm

The Unified Robot Description Format (URDF) [4] is an XML specification for describing robot structure: links (rigid bodies), joints (connections between links), and their physical properties (mass, inertia, geometry, and collision bounds). ROS 2 tools — including RViz2, robot_state_publisher, and MoveIt 2 — consume URDF files to understand the robot's kinematic and dynamic structure.

The following is a complete, syntactically valid URDF for a 3-DOF humanoid arm. It includes visual geometry, collision geometry, inertial properties, and joint limits. Link dimensions are illustrative; a real system would derive them from CAD measurements.

<?xml version="1.0" encoding="UTF-8"?>

<!-- humanoid_arm.urdf 3-DOF humanoid arm: shoulder_pitch -> upper_arm -> elbow_flex -> forearm -> wrist_roll -> hand All lengths in meters, masses in kilograms, inertias in kg·m². Joint angles in radians.


Kinematic chain: world -> base_link -> upper_arm_link [shoulder_pitch] -> forearm_link [elbow_flex] -> hand_link [wrist_roll] --> <robot name="humanoid_arm">


<!-- ============================================================ --> <!-- LINKS                                                          --> <!-- ============================================================ -->


<!-- Base link: fixed reference frame (torso attachment point) --> <link name="base_link"> <inertial> <origin xyz="0 0 0" rpy="0 0 0"/> <mass value="2.0"/> <inertia ixx="0.01" ixy="0" ixz="0" iyy="0.01" iyz="0" izz="0.01"/> </inertial> <visual> <origin xyz="0 0 0.05" rpy="0 0 0"/> <geometry> <cylinder radius="0.05" length="0.10"/> </geometry> <material name="dark_grey"> <color rgba="0.3 0.3 0.3 1.0"/> </material> </visual> <collision> <origin xyz="0 0 0.05" rpy="0 0 0"/> <geometry> <cylinder radius="0.05" length="0.10"/> </geometry> </collision> </link>


<!-- Upper arm link: from shoulder to elbow --> <!-- Length: 0.30 m (approximately upper arm of a human adult) --> <link name="upper_arm_link"> <inertial> <!-- Center of mass at geometric center of the cylinder --> <origin xyz="0 0 -0.15" rpy="0 0 0"/> <mass value="1.5"/> <!-- Solid cylinder: Ixx = Iyy = m(3r² + h²)/12, Izz = mr²/2 --> <!-- m=1.5 kg, r=0.04 m, h=0.30 m --> <inertia ixx="0.01184" ixy="0" ixz="0" iyy="0.01184" iyz="0" izz="0.00120"/> </inertial> <visual> <origin xyz="0 0 -0.15" rpy="0 0 0"/> <geometry> <cylinder radius="0.04" length="0.30"/> </geometry> <material name="light_grey"> <color rgba="0.7 0.7 0.7 1.0"/> </material> </visual> <collision> <origin xyz="0 0 -0.15" rpy="0 0 0"/> <geometry> <!-- Slightly oversized collision cylinder for conservative planning --> <cylinder radius="0.045" length="0.32"/> </geometry> </collision> </link>


<!-- Forearm link: from elbow to wrist --> <link name="forearm_link"> <inertial> <origin xyz="0 0 -0.13" rpy="0 0 0"/> <mass value="1.0"/> <!-- m=1.0 kg, r=0.035 m, h=0.26 m --> <inertia ixx="0.00580" ixy="0" ixz="0" iyy="0.00580" iyz="0" izz="0.00061"/> </inertial> <visual> <origin xyz="0 0 -0.13" rpy="0 0 0"/> <geometry> <cylinder radius="0.035" length="0.26"/> </geometry> <material name="light_grey"> <color rgba="0.7 0.7 0.7 1.0"/> </material> </visual> <collision> <origin xyz="0 0 -0.13" rpy="0 0 0"/> <geometry> <cylinder radius="0.040" length="0.28"/> </geometry> </collision> </link>


<!-- Hand link: simplified palm representation --> <link name="hand_link"> <inertial> <origin xyz="0 0 -0.06" rpy="0 0 0"/> <mass value="0.5"/> <inertia ixx="0.00042" ixy="0" ixz="0" iyy="0.00042" iyz="0" izz="0.00020"/> </inertial> <visual> <origin xyz="0 0 -0.06" rpy="0 0 0"/> <geometry> <box size="0.08 0.10 0.12"/> </geometry> <material name="skin_tone"> <color rgba="0.9 0.75 0.65 1.0"/> </material> </visual> <collision> <origin xyz="0 0 -0.06" rpy="0 0 0"/> <geometry> <box size="0.09 0.11 0.13"/> </geometry> </collision> </link>


<!-- ============================================================ --> <!-- JOINTS                                                         --> <!-- ============================================================ -->


<!-- Joint 1: shoulder_pitch — rotates upper arm about Y axis (forward/backward) --> <joint name="shoulder_pitch" type="revolute"> <parent link="base_link"/> <child link="upper_arm_link"/> <!-- Origin at the shoulder center, 10 cm above base_link origin --> <origin xyz="0 0 0.10" rpy="0 0 0"/> <!-- Rotation axis: Y (pitch = forward/backward arm motion) --> <axis xyz="0 1 0"/> <limit lower="-2.617"   <!-- -150 degrees --> upper="2.617"    <!-- +150 degrees --> effort="50.0"    <!-- N·m maximum joint torque --> velocity="3.14"/> <!-- rad/s maximum velocity --> <dynamics damping="0.5" friction="0.1"/> </joint>


<!-- Joint 2: elbow_flex — flexes forearm (rotates about Y axis) --> <joint name="elbow_flex" type="revolute"> <parent link="upper_arm_link"/> <child link="forearm_link"/> <!-- Origin at the elbow: 30 cm below shoulder (bottom of upper arm) --> <origin xyz="0 0 -0.30" rpy="0 0 0"/> <axis xyz="0 1 0"/> <limit lower="-0.087"   <!-- -5 degrees (prevents hyperextension) --> upper="2.967"    <!-- +170 degrees --> effort="30.0" velocity="3.14"/> <dynamics damping="0.3" friction="0.05"/> </joint>


<!-- Joint 3: wrist_roll — rotates hand about Z axis (pronation/supination) --> <joint name="wrist_roll" type="revolute"> <parent link="forearm_link"/> <child link="hand_link"/> <!-- Origin at the wrist: 26 cm below elbow --> <origin xyz="0 0 -0.26" rpy="0 0 0"/> <!-- Rotation axis: Z (roll = forearm rotation) --> <axis xyz="0 0 1"/> <limit lower="-3.141"   <!-- -180 degrees --> upper="3.141"    <!-- +180 degrees --> effort="15.0" velocity="6.28"/> <dynamics damping="0.2" friction="0.02"/> </joint>


<!-- ============================================================ --> <!-- ROS2_CONTROL INTEGRATION                                       --> <!-- ============================================================ --> <ros2_control name="HumanoidArmSystem" type="system"> <hardware> <plugin>humanoid_arm_hardware/HumanoidArmHardware</plugin> </hardware> <joint name="shoulder_pitch"> <command_interface name="velocity"/> <state_interface name="position"/> <state_interface name="velocity"/> </joint> <joint name="elbow_flex"> <command_interface name="velocity"/> <state_interface name="position"/> <state_interface name="velocity"/> </joint> <joint name="wrist_roll"> <command_interface name="velocity"/> <state_interface name="position"/> <state_interface name="velocity"/> </joint> </ros2_control>


</robot>

4.6 MoveIt 2 Integration

MoveIt 2 [5] is the standard motion planning framework for ROS 2. It provides:
  • Inverse kinematics (IK) solvers (KDL, TRAC-IK, BioIK)
  • Collision detection and avoidance (FCL — Flexible Collision Library)
  • Motion planners (OMPL — Open Motion Planning Library, CHOMP, PILZ Industrial)
  • A Python and C++ API for specifying goals in joint space or Cartesian space

4.6.1 MoveGroupInterface in Python

<h1>moveit2_arm_planner.py</h1>

<h1>Plans and executes motions for the 3-DOF humanoid arm using MoveIt 2.</h1> <h1>ROS 2 Humble | Python 3.10+ | moveit_py</h1> # <h1>Installation:</h1> <h1>  sudo apt install ros-humble-moveit ros-humble-moveit-py</h1> # <h1>Note: moveit_py is the Python bindings for MoveIt 2 introduced in ROS 2 Humble.</h1> <h1>The older MoveGroupInterface (C++) is also available via the moveit_commander</h1> <h1>package, but moveit_py is the recommended Python interface for Humble.</h1>


from __future__ import annotations


from typing import Optional


from rclpy.node import Node


<h1>moveit_py provides Python bindings to MoveIt 2's core planning API</h1> from moveit.planning import MoveItPy from moveit.core.robot_state import RobotState


class HumanoidArmMoveItClient(Node): """ Uses MoveIt 2 to plan and execute motions for the 3-DOF humanoid arm.


Prerequisites: - MoveIt 2 configuration package for humanoid_arm must be running (generated by the MoveIt Setup Assistant) - move_group action server must be active - ros2_control controllers must be loaded and active """


def __init__(self) -> None: super().__init__('humanoid_arm_moveit_client')


# Initialize MoveItPy — connects to the running move_group node self._moveit = MoveItPy(node_name='moveit_py_client')


# Get the planning component for the 'arm' planning group # Planning group name must match what was configured in Setup Assistant self._arm = self._moveit.get_planning_component('arm')


self.get_logger().info('MoveIt 2 arm planning component ready')


def plan_and_execute_joint_goal( self, joint_positions: dict[str, float], velocity_scaling: float = 0.3, acceleration_scaling: float = 0.1, ) -> bool: """ Plans and executes a joint space goal.


Args: joint_positions: Dict mapping joint name to target position in radians. velocity_scaling: Fraction of maximum velocity to use (0.0–1.0). acceleration_scaling: Fraction of maximum acceleration to use (0.0–1.0).


Returns: True if planning and execution succeeded. """ self.get_logger().info(f'Planning joint space goal: {joint_positions}')


# Set the start state to the current robot state self._arm.set_start_state_to_current_state()


# Create a goal state from the joint positions dictionary goal_state = self._build_joint_state(joint_positions) self._arm.set_goal_state(robot_state=goal_state)


# Plan the motion # PlanRequestParameters allows setting planner ID and time limits from moveit.planning import PlanRequestParameters plan_params = PlanRequestParameters( self._moveit, 'ompl_rrtconnect',  # OMPL RRT-Connect planner ) plan_params.planning_time = 5.0       # seconds plan_params.max_velocity_scaling_factor = velocity_scaling plan_params.max_acceleration_scaling_factor = acceleration_scaling


plan_result = self._arm.plan(single_plan_parameters=plan_params)


if not plan_result: self.get_logger().error('Planning failed — goal may be unreachable or in collision') return False


self.get_logger().info('Plan succeeded — executing...')


# Execute the plan execute_result = self._moveit.execute( plan_result.trajectory, controllers=['joint_trajectory_controller'], )


if execute_result: self.get_logger().info('Execution completed successfully') return True else: self.get_logger().error('Execution failed') return False


def plan_cartesian_path( self, waypoints_xyz: list[tuple[float, float, float]], eef_step: float = 0.01, ) -> bool: """ Plans a Cartesian path through a series of end-effector waypoints. The path follows straight lines between waypoints in Cartesian space.


Args: waypoints_xyz: List of (x, y, z) end-effector positions in the base_link frame (meters). eef_step: Maximum distance between consecutive Cartesian waypoints for interpolation (meters). Smaller values = smoother path.


Returns: True if a valid path covering all waypoints was planned and executed. """ from geometry_msgs.msg import Pose import copy


self.get_logger().info( f'Planning Cartesian path through {len(waypoints_xyz)} waypoints' )


# Build a list of Pose messages from (x, y, z) tuples # Orientation is kept fixed (pointing downward — suitable for grasp approach) poses = [] for x, y, z in waypoints_xyz: pose = Pose() pose.position.x = x pose.position.y = y pose.position.z = z # Fixed orientation: end-effector pointing straight down pose.orientation.x = 0.0 pose.orientation.y = 1.0 pose.orientation.z = 0.0 pose.orientation.w = 0.0 poses.append(pose)


# Compute Cartesian path via MoveIt's compute_cartesian_path # fraction = fraction of path that is feasible (1.0 = fully feasible) self._arm.set_start_state_to_current_state()


plan_result, fraction = self._arm.plan_cartesian_path( waypoints=poses, max_step=eef_step, jump_threshold=0.0,  # 0.0 disables jump checking (enable for safety in production) )


self.get_logger().info(f'Cartesian path planned: {fraction * 100:.1f}% feasible')


if fraction < 0.95: self.get_logger().warn( f'Only {fraction * 100:.1f}% of Cartesian path is feasible. ' 'Consider adjusting waypoints or checking for collisions.' ) return False


execute_result = self._moveit.execute( plan_result.trajectory, controllers=['joint_trajectory_controller'], )


return bool(execute_result)


def _build_joint_state(self, joint_positions: dict[str, float]) -> RobotState: """Creates a RobotState with the specified joint positions.""" robot_model = self._moveit.get_robot_model() robot_state = RobotState(robot_model) robot_state.set_to_default_values()


for joint_name, position in joint_positions.items(): robot_state.set_joint_positions(joint_name, [position])


robot_state.update() return robot_state


def main(args: Optional[list[str]] = None) -> None: rclpy.init(args=args) node = HumanoidArmMoveItClient()


# Example 1: Plan and execute a joint space goal # Move to: shoulder_pitch = 0.5 rad, elbow_flex = 1.2 rad, wrist_roll = 0.0 rad success = node.plan_and_execute_joint_goal( joint_positions={ 'shoulder_pitch': 0.5, 'elbow_flex': 1.2, 'wrist_roll': 0.0, }, velocity_scaling=0.3, ) node.get_logger().info(f'Joint space goal: {"success" if success else "failed"}')


# Example 2: Plan a Cartesian path (grasp approach trajectory) # Move end-effector through three waypoints above a table surface cartesian_success = node.plan_cartesian_path( waypoints_xyz=[ (0.30, 0.0, 0.40),   # Approach point (0.30, 0.0, 0.25),   # Pre-grasp position (0.30, 0.0, 0.20),   # Grasp position ], eef_step=0.01, ) node.get_logger().info( f'Cartesian path: {"success" if cartesian_success else "failed"}' )


node.destroy_node() rclpy.shutdown()


if __name__ == '__main__': main()

4.7 Nav2 for Bipedal Navigation

Nav2 (Navigation 2) [6] is the navigation stack for ROS 2. It provides autonomous navigation for mobile robots: path planning (global), local obstacle avoidance (local), behavior trees for task sequencing, and recovery behaviors. Nav2 is designed primarily for ground-contact wheeled robots, and adapting it to bipedal humanoids requires awareness of its assumptions and limitations.

4.7.1 Nav2 Architecture Overview

Nav2 consists of several major components:

BT Navigator — The top-level behavior tree executor that sequences the navigation task. Planner Server — Computes a global path from the robot's current position to the goal using planners such as NavFn (Dijkstra-based), Smac Planner, and Theta\*. Controller Server — Executes the global path by computing local velocity commands using DWB (Dynamic Window Approach), TEB (Timed Elastic Band), or MPPI (Model Predictive Path Integral) controllers. Costmap 2D — Maintains a 2D occupancy grid of the environment with inflation layers and obstacle layers derived from laser scans or depth images. Recovery Server — Handles stuck-robot situations with behaviors like spin, back-up, and clear costmap. Figure 4.3 — Nav2 Architecture for a Wheeled Robot 
                        /navigate_to_pose (action)

│ ▼ ┌──────────────────────┐ │    BT Navigator      │ │  (behavior tree XML) │ └──────┬───────┬───────┘ │       │ ┌────────────┘       └────────────────┐ │                                     │ ▼                                     ▼ ┌───────────────────────┐           ┌─────────────────────────┐ │   Planner Server      │           │   Controller Server      │ │   (global path plan)  │           │   (local velocity cmds)  │ │                       │           │                          │ │  action: /compute_    │           │  action: /follow_path    │ │  path_to_pose         │           │  topic: /cmd_vel         │ └──────────┬────────────┘           └──────────┬───────────────┘ │                                   │ │        ┌──────────────────┐       │ └───────►│  Costmap 2D      │◄──────┘ │  (global + local) │ │                  │ │  /scan (LiDAR)   │ │  /map (AMCL)     │ └──────────────────┘ │ ┌─────────────────┘ ▼ ┌────────────────────┐        ┌──────────────────────┐ │  Recovery Server   │        │  Robot Base           │ │  (spin, backup,    │        │  Controller           │ │   clear costmap)   │        │  ← /cmd_vel topic     │ └────────────────────┘        └──────────────────────┘
Figure 4.3: Nav2 navigation stack architecture. The BT Navigator orchestrates goal-directed navigation by calling the Planner Server (global path), Controller Server (local velocity commands), and Recovery Server (failure handling). Costmap 2D ingests LiDAR and map data to provide obstacle-aware planning and control.

4.7.2 Caveats for Bipedal Humanoids

Nav2's default assumptions break down in important ways for bipedal humanoids:

Footprint model. Nav2 models the robot's footprint as a 2D polygon on the ground plane. A humanoid robot's stable footprint changes dramatically during walking — it is a point during single-support phase and a convex hull of both feet during double-support. Nav2 cannot model this dynamic footprint without custom modifications. Velocity commands. Nav2 outputs geometry_msgs/Twist messages on /cmd_vel with linear x/y velocities and angular z velocity. A wheeled robot's motion controller maps these directly to wheel speeds. A bipedal humanoid's gait controller (based on ZMP, LIPM, or a reinforcement learning policy) receives much more complex inputs than cmd_vel. A translation layer — sometimes called a "gait adapter" — is required between Nav2's velocity commands and the humanoid's walking controller. Stability awareness. Nav2 plans paths assuming the robot can follow any path that does not collide with obstacles. A biped may be unable to follow a curved path at the same speed as a straight path, or may be destabilized by sudden direction changes. A production humanoid navigation system would need to incorporate stability constraints into the planning layer. Research solutions. Academic work on humanoid navigation with Nav2 — such as the Honda ASIMO extensions and work using the PAL Robotics TALOS platform [12] — typically replaces the controller server's velocity interface with a bipedal gait command interface and adds a stability costmap layer. These are active research areas as of 2026.

4.7.3 BehaviorTree XML Example

Nav2 uses BehaviorTree.CPP [7] for task sequencing. The following is a simplified BehaviorTree XML that illustrates a navigate-to-pose mission with a single recovery behavior:

<!--

navigate_with_recovery.xml Simplified Nav2 behavior tree for a humanoid navigation task. Nav2 Humble | BehaviorTree.CPP v3


Tree structure: RecoveryNode attempts NavigateToPose. If NavigateToPose fails, it spins in place and retries once. --> <root main_tree_to_execute="MainTree">


<!-- Follow the computed path using the local controller -->


<!-- Recovery subtree: executed when NavigateWithReplanning fails -->


</ReactiveFallback>


</RecoveryNode>


</BehaviorTree> </root>

4.8 Complete Example — Joint State Subscriber with Velocity Command Publisher

This section presents a self-contained, complete ROS 2 node that integrates the patterns from this chapter: subscribing to joint states, implementing a simple proportional controller, and publishing velocity commands via the ros2_control forward velocity controller interface.

<h1>arm_position_controller_node.py</h1>

<h1>Complete example: reads joint states, runs P-controller, publishes velocity commands.</h1> <h1>ROS 2 Humble | Python 3.10+ | rclpy</h1> # <h1>This node can be tested with the ros2_control mock hardware interface:</h1> <h1>  ros2 run controller_manager ros2_control_node --ros-args</h1> <h1>      -p robot_description:="$(cat humanoid_arm.urdf)"</h1> # <h1>Then load controllers:</h1> <h1>  ros2 control load_controller joint_state_broadcaster</h1> <h1>  ros2 control load_controller forward_velocity_controller</h1> <h1>  ros2 control set_controller_state joint_state_broadcaster active</h1> <h1>  ros2 control set_controller_state forward_velocity_controller active</h1>


from __future__ import annotations


from typing import Optional


from rclpy.node import Node from rclpy.executors import MultiThreadedExecutor from rclpy.callback_groups import MutuallyExclusiveCallbackGroup from rclpy.qos import QoSProfile, ReliabilityPolicy, HistoryPolicy from sensor_msgs.msg import JointState from std_msgs.msg import Float64MultiArray


<h1>Target joint positions (radians) — a relaxed arm pose</h1> TARGET_POSITIONS: dict[str, float] = { 'shoulder_pitch': 0.3,    # 17 degrees forward 'elbow_flex':     0.8,    # ~46 degrees bent 'wrist_roll':     0.0,    # Neutral wrist rotation }


<h1>Proportional controller gains (rad/s per rad of position error)</h1> KP: dict[str, float] = { 'shoulder_pitch': 1.5, 'elbow_flex':     1.5, 'wrist_roll':     2.0, }


<h1>Velocity limits (rad/s) — must not exceed URDF joint velocity limits</h1> MAX_VELOCITY: dict[str, float] = { 'shoulder_pitch': 1.0, 'elbow_flex':     1.0, 'wrist_roll':     2.0, }


JOINT_ORDER = ['shoulder_pitch', 'elbow_flex', 'wrist_roll']


class ArmPositionControllerNode(Node): """ Complete arm position controller node.


Subscribes to /joint_states (from JointStateBroadcaster). Runs a proportional position controller at 100 Hz. Publishes velocity commands to /forward_velocity_controller/commands.


Uses MultiThreadedExecutor so the 100 Hz control loop is not blocked by the joint state callback processing. """


def __init__(self) -> None: super().__init__('arm_position_controller')


# --- Callback groups for concurrent execution --- self._sensor_cbg = MutuallyExclusiveCallbackGroup() self._control_cbg = MutuallyExclusiveCallbackGroup()


# --- Shared state --- self._state_lock = threading.Lock() self._current_positions: dict[str, float] = { name: 0.0 for name in JOINT_ORDER } self._joint_states_received: bool = False


# --- Subscription --- sensor_qos = QoSProfile( reliability=ReliabilityPolicy.BEST_EFFORT, history=HistoryPolicy.KEEP_LAST, depth=5, ) self._joint_state_sub = self.create_subscription( msg_type=JointState, topic='/joint_states', callback=self._joint_state_callback, qos_profile=sensor_qos, callback_group=self._sensor_cbg, )


# --- Publisher to forward velocity controller --- self._velocity_pub = self.create_publisher( msg_type=Float64MultiArray, topic='/forward_velocity_controller/commands', qos_profile=10, )


# --- Control loop at 100 Hz --- self._control_timer = self.create_timer( timer_period_sec=0.01, callback=self._control_loop, callback_group=self._control_cbg, )


# --- Diagnostic timer at 1 Hz --- self._diag_timer = self.create_timer( timer_period_sec=1.0, callback=self._log_state, )


self.get_logger().info( 'ArmPositionControllerNode started. ' 'Waiting for joint states on /joint_states...' )


def _joint_state_callback(self, msg: JointState) -> None: """ Updates current joint positions from the JointStateBroadcaster. Uses lock to protect shared state from concurrent control loop access. """ with self._state_lock: for name, position in zip(msg.name, msg.position): if name in self._current_positions: self._current_positions[name] = position self._joint_states_received = True


def _control_loop(self) -> None: """ Proportional position controller running at 100 Hz. Reads current joint positions, computes velocity commands, publishes. """ with self._state_lock: if not self._joint_states_received: return  # Do not command until we have state feedback current = dict(self._current_positions)


velocity_commands: list[float] = []


for joint_name in JOINT_ORDER: error = TARGET_POSITIONS[joint_name] - current[joint_name] raw_command = KP[joint_name] * error


# Apply velocity clamping for safety max_vel = MAX_VELOCITY[joint_name] clamped_command = max(-max_vel, min(max_vel, raw_command)) velocity_commands.append(clamped_command)


# Publish to the forward velocity controller cmd_msg = Float64MultiArray() cmd_msg.data = velocity_commands self._velocity_pub.publish(cmd_msg)


def _log_state(self) -> None: """Logs current joint positions and velocity errors at 1 Hz.""" with self._state_lock: current = dict(self._current_positions)


for joint_name in JOINT_ORDER: error_deg = (TARGET_POSITIONS[joint_name] - current[joint_name]) error_deg_display = error_deg * (180.0 / 3.14159) self.get_logger().info( f'{joint_name}: ' f'current={current[joint_name]:.3f} rad, ' f'target={TARGET_POSITIONS[joint_name]:.3f} rad, ' f'error={error_deg_display:.1f} deg' )


def main(args: Optional[list[str]] = None) -> None: rclpy.init(args=args) node = ArmPositionControllerNode()


executor = MultiThreadedExecutor(num_threads=3) executor.add_node(node)


try: executor.spin() except KeyboardInterrupt: node.get_logger().info('Shutting down arm controller') finally: # Publish zero velocities before shutting down — safety measure zero_msg = Float64MultiArray() zero_msg.data = [0.0, 0.0, 0.0] node._velocity_pub.publish(zero_msg)


executor.shutdown() node.destroy_node() rclpy.shutdown()


if __name__ == '__main__': main()

4.9 Chapter Summary

This chapter translated ROS 2 architecture into practical humanoid robot control software.

We developed production-quality Python nodes with type annotations and proper QoS configuration, establishing the structural patterns that make ROS 2 code maintainable at scale. The deep dive into MultiThreadedExecutor and callback groups revealed why single-threaded execution is inadequate for humanoid control: a 100 Hz control loop and a 500 Hz sensor stream must run concurrently without blocking each other, protected by locks over shared state.

The ros2_control framework received detailed treatment: the hardware interface abstraction that decouples control algorithms from specific hardware, the conceptual structure of StateInterface and CommandInterface objects, and how URDF tags connect the software framework to the robot description. A complete, syntactically valid URDF for a 3-DOF humanoid arm was provided — including inertial properties, collision geometry, and joint limits — as the foundation for all simulation and planning work.

MoveIt 2 integration demonstrated both joint space goal planning and Cartesian path planning via moveit_py, covering the practical API calls needed to command an arm from a Python node. The Nav2 section balanced architecture overview with an honest account of the significant adaptations required to use a wheeled-robot navigation stack with a biped — particularly the dynamic footprint problem, the velocity command translation requirement, and the missing stability constraints.

The chapter closed with a complete, self-contained proportional position controller that synthesizes multi-threading, joint state subscription, and velocity command publishing into a runnable, testable node.

Chapter 5 addresses the security layer that protects all of this communication infrastructure: SROS2 and DDS-Security for authenticated, encrypted, and access-controlled communication between humanoid robot nodes.
