[9] A. Erez, Y. Tassa, and E. Todorov, "Simulation tools for model-based robotics: Comparison of Bullet, Havok, MuJoCo, ODE and PhysX," in Proc. IEEE Int. Conf. Robotics and Automation (ICRA), 2015, pp. 4397–4404. doi: 10.1109/ICRA.2015.7139807.

[10] Open Robotics, "ros_gz: ROS 2 and Gazebo Integration," GitHub, 2023. [Online]. Available: https://github.com/gazebosim/ros_gz. [Accessed: Mar. 2026].

Chapter 7: Unity — Human-Robot Interaction Simulation

Part III — Digital Twins — Simulation Environments
Version 1.0 — Draft | Author: Muhammad Faisal Laiq Siddiqui

7.1 Introduction — Why Photorealistic HRI Simulation Matters

A robot that functions flawlessly in a physics simulator may fail the moment it encounters a human being. This is not a metaphor. Documented deployments of service robots and collaborative manipulators have shown that human behavior introduces a category of challenge that rigid-body physics engines alone cannot capture [1]. People approach robots from unexpected angles. They reach across a robot's planned path. They stop, reverse, gesture, and crowd. They are variable in a way that a static simulation environment never is.

Human-Robot Interaction (HRI) research addresses the question of how robots and people can share space, communicate intent, and coordinate tasks safely and efficiently. To study HRI experimentally, researchers need a simulation environment that can represent both a physically accurate robot and behaviorally realistic human participants — together, in the same scene, with sufficient visual fidelity that perception algorithms behave as they would in the real world.

This is precisely what game engines provide. A game engine renders scenes at photorealistic quality, animates human character models with motion-captured movement data, scripts crowd behaviors using AI navigation systems, and runs all of this at interactive frame rates. The leading game engine for robotics HRI simulation is Unity, developed by Unity Technologies.

Unity brings three capabilities to HRI simulation that physics-first simulators like Gazebo cannot match:

First, photorealistic rendering via the High Definition Render Pipeline (HDRP) produces scenes indistinguishable from real indoor environments, allowing computer vision models trained in simulation to transfer to physical cameras without significant domain adaptation.

Second, behavioral simulation through Unity's NavMesh AI navigation and Animator state machines allows human avatars to walk, avoid obstacles, gesture, and respond to the robot's behavior — creating the dynamic crowd scenarios that HRI researchers need to evaluate social navigation algorithms.

Third, rapid scene construction through the Unity Editor's GUI and access to the Unity Asset Store's thousands of photorealistic environment assets and character models allows researchers to build complex test environments in hours rather than weeks.

What follows is a practical guide to Unity-based humanoid robot simulation — from project setup through URDF import, HRI scenario scripting, and the bidirectional ROS 2 bridge that makes Unity a live extension of a running robot software stack.

7.2 Unity Robotics Hub Setup

7.2.1 Installing Unity 2022 LTS

Unity 2022 LTS (Long-Term Support) is the recommended version for robotics development. LTS releases receive security and stability patches for two years after release, providing the stability required for research software that must remain reproducible across publications.

Installation procedure:

  1. Download and install Unity Hub from https://unity.com/download.
  2. In Unity Hub, navigate to Installs → Install Editor.
  3. Select Unity 2022.3.x LTS (choose the latest 2022.3 patch release).
  4. Under Modules, select:
- Linux Build Support (IL2CPP) — required for building ROS-deployable simulation clients

- Windows Build Support — for Windows deployment - Visual Studio (or VS Code) for script editing

  1. Click Install and wait for the download to complete.

7.2.2 Adding the Robotics Package via Package Manager

Unity's Package Manager allows packages to be added from the Unity Registry, Git URLs, or local disk paths. The Unity Robotics Hub packages are installed via Git URL.

  1. Create a new Unity project: New Project → 3D (HDRP) → name it HumanoidHRISim.
  2. Open Window → Package Manager.
  3. Click the + button → Add package from git URL.
  4. Add the following packages in order:
https://github.com/Unity-Technologies/ROS-TCP-Connector.git?path=/com.unity.robotics.ros-tcp-connector

https://github.com/Unity-Technologies/URDF-Importer.git?path=/com.unity.robotics.urdf-importer
  1. After installation, verify that Robotics appears in the Package Manager's In Project list with both ROS TCP Connector and URDF Importer shown.

7.2.3 Project Structure

A well-organized Unity robotics project follows this folder structure:

Assets/

Robots/ Humanoid/ URDF/          ← URDF file and mesh assets Materials/     ← Robot surface materials Environments/ Office/          ← Office environment scene assets Warehouse/ Humans/ Avatars/         ← Human character prefabs Animations/      ← Motion capture animation clips Scripts/ Robot/           ← Robot control C# scripts Human/           ← Human avatar behavior scripts ROS/             ← ROS message handler scripts Prefabs/ Robot.prefab     ← Assembled robot prefab Human.prefab     ← Human avatar prefab Scenes/ HRI_Office.unity ← Main HRI test scene ROSMessages/       ← Auto-generated ROS message C# classes

The ROSMessages folder is populated automatically by the ROS-TCP Connector when it generates C# wrapper classes from ROS message definitions.

7.3 URDF Import into Unity

7.3.1 Using the URDF Importer Package

The URDF Importer converts a URDF robot description into a Unity scene hierarchy. The importer:

  • Creates a GameObject hierarchy matching the URDF link tree
  • Attaches ArticulationBody components to each link
  • Configures joint limits and axis directions from the URDF definitions
  • Imports mesh geometry from STL or DAE files referenced by the URDF

Import procedure:

  1. Copy the robot's URDF file and all referenced mesh files into Assets/Robots/Humanoid/URDF/.
  2. In the Unity Editor, right-click on the URDF file in the Project window.
  3. Select Import Robot from URDF.
  4. In the import dialog, set:
- Mesh Decomposer: VHACD (for convex decomposition of collision meshes)

- Axis Type: Y-Axis (Unity uses Y-up convention; URDF uses Z-up)

  1. Click Import URDF. Unity creates a prefab with the complete robot hierarchy.

7.3.2 ArticulationBody Components

Unity's ArticulationBody is the physics component designed specifically for simulating robotic kinematic chains [2]. Unlike Rigidbody, which uses an impulse-based solver prone to error accumulation in long chains, ArticulationBody implements a reduced coordinate articulation solver (Featherstone-style) that handles robot arm and leg chains with significantly lower drift.

Key ArticulationBody properties:

PropertyDescriptionTypical value (humanoid hip joint)
MassLink mass in kg5.0
LinearDampingVelocity damping0.05
AngularDampingAngular velocity damping0.05
JointTypeFixed, Prismatic, or RevoluteRevolute
AxisJoint rotation axis (local frame)(1, 0, 0) for pitch

7.3.3 Setting Joint Drives

A joint drive in Unity implements a PD controller on the joint's degree of freedom. The drive is configured by the ArticulationDrive struct:

// C# — Configure drive for a revolute joint

ArticulationBody joint = GetComponent<ArticulationBody>();


ArticulationDrive drive = joint.xDrive; drive.stiffness = 10000f;    // Proportional gain (N·m/rad) drive.damping = 100f;        // Derivative gain (N·m·s/rad) drive.forceLimit = 300f;     // Maximum torque (N·m) drive.lowerLimit = -90f;     // Lower joint limit (degrees) drive.upperLimit = 90f;      // Upper joint limit (degrees)


joint.xDrive = drive;

Stiffness of 10,000 N·m/rad and damping of 100 N·m·s/rad are representative starting values for a hip joint; tuning is required per joint based on the robot's mass distribution and desired control bandwidth.

7.3.4 C# Code Snippet for Joint Control

The following MonoBehaviour component provides position control for a single joint, receiving target angles from ROS 2 topics:

// Scripts/Robot/JointController.cs

using System.Collections.Generic; using UnityEngine; using Unity.Robotics.ROSTCPConnector; using RosMessageTypes.Std;


public class JointController : MonoBehaviour { [Header("Joint Configuration")] public string jointName; public ArticulationBody articulationBody;


[Header("Control Parameters")] public float stiffness = 10000f; public float damping = 100f; public float forceLimit = 300f;


[Header("ROS")] public string rosTopicName;


private ROSConnection _ros; private float _targetAngleDegrees = 0f;


void Start() { // Configure the ArticulationDrive var drive = articulationBody.xDrive; drive.stiffness = stiffness; drive.damping = damping; drive.forceLimit = forceLimit; articulationBody.xDrive = drive;


// Subscribe to ROS topic for target position _ros = ROSConnection.GetOrCreateInstance(); _ros.Subscribe<Float64Msg>(rosTopicName, OnJointCommandReceived);


Debug.Log($"[JointController] {jointName} initialized. Listening on {rosTopicName}"); }


private void OnJointCommandReceived(Float64Msg message) { // Convert radians (ROS convention) to degrees (Unity convention) _targetAngleDegrees = (float)(message.data * Mathf.Rad2Deg); }


void FixedUpdate() { // Apply target position to the articulation drive var drive = articulationBody.xDrive; drive.target = _targetAngleDegrees; articulationBody.xDrive = drive; } }

This script is attached to each joint GameObject in the robot hierarchy. The [Header] attributes organize the Inspector view, and the FixedUpdate method ensures drive targets are updated synchronously with the physics step.

7.4 Building HRI Test Scenarios

7.4.1 Populating the Scene

An HRI test scenario requires three elements in the scene: the environment, the robot, and human avatars.

Environment: From the Unity Asset Store or a custom modeled environment, import an office or warehouse layout into the scene. Assign physics materials (concrete, tile, carpet) to floor surfaces to correctly simulate friction. Configure NavMesh baking: Window → AI → Navigation → Bake with agent radius 0.35 m and step height 0.3 m, covering all walkable floor surfaces. Robot: Drag the imported humanoid robot prefab into the scene. Position it at the start of a corridor or at a waypoint. Attach the JointController component to each joint GameObject and configure the ROS topic names. Human Avatars: Import human character prefabs with Humanoid rig configuration (required for Unity's Animator). Attach a NavMeshAgent component to each avatar with:
  • Speed: 1.4 m/s (typical pedestrian walking speed)
  • Angular Speed: 120 degrees/s
  • Stopping Distance: 0.5 m

7.4.2 Scripting the Human Avatar Walking Toward the Robot

// Scripts/Human/PedestrianBehavior.cs

using UnityEngine; using UnityEngine.AI;


public class PedestrianBehavior : MonoBehaviour { [Header("Navigation")] public Transform[] waypoints; public float waypointTolerance = 0.5f;


[Header("Interaction")] public float robotDetectionRadius = 3.0f; public LayerMask robotLayer;


private NavMeshAgent _agent; private Animator _animator; private int _currentWaypoint = 0; private bool _robotDetected = false;


// Animator parameter hashes (cached for performance) private static readonly int SpeedHash = Animator.StringToHash("Speed"); private static readonly int IsStoppedHash = Animator.StringToHash("IsStopped");


void Start() { _agent = GetComponent<NavMeshAgent>(); _animator = GetComponent<Animator>();


if (waypoints.Length > 0) { _agent.SetDestination(waypoints[0].position); } }


void Update() { DetectRobot(); UpdateAnimation(); AdvanceWaypoint(); }


private void DetectRobot() { // Check for robot within detection radius Collider[] hits = Physics.OverlapSphere( transform.position, robotDetectionRadius, robotLayer );


if (hits.Length > 0 && !_robotDetected) { _robotDetected = true; // Stop and wait for robot to clear path _agent.isStopped = true; Debug.Log($"[PedestrianBehavior] Robot detected — stopping pedestrian."); } else if (hits.Length == 0 && _robotDetected) { _robotDetected = false; _agent.isStopped = false; Debug.Log($"[PedestrianBehavior] Path clear — resuming pedestrian."); } }


private void UpdateAnimation() { float speed = _agent.velocity.magnitude; _animator.SetFloat(SpeedHash, speed); _animator.SetBool(IsStoppedHash, _agent.isStopped); }


private void AdvanceWaypoint() { if (waypoints.Length == 0 || _agent.isStopped) return;


float distanceToWaypoint = Vector3.Distance( transform.position, waypoints[_currentWaypoint].position );


if (distanceToWaypoint < waypointTolerance) { _currentWaypoint = (_currentWaypoint + 1) % waypoints.Length; _agent.SetDestination(waypoints[_currentWaypoint].position); } } }

7.4.3 Testing Robot Response

The scenario script below coordinates the robot and human avatar, triggers a proximity event, commands the robot to stop, then navigate around the human:

// Scripts/Robot/SocialNavigationController.cs

using UnityEngine; using UnityEngine.AI; using Unity.Robotics.ROSTCPConnector; using RosMessageTypes.Geometry;


public class SocialNavigationController : MonoBehaviour { [Header("Navigation")] public Transform goal; public float personalSpaceRadius = 1.5f; public LayerMask humanLayer;


[Header("ROS")] public string cmdVelTopic = "/cmd_vel";


private NavMeshAgent _agent; private ROSConnection _ros; private bool _humanInPath = false;


void Start() { _agent = GetComponent<NavMeshAgent>(); _ros = ROSConnection.GetOrCreateInstance(); _ros.RegisterPublisher<TwistMsg>(cmdVelTopic);


if (goal != null) _agent.SetDestination(goal.position); }


void Update() { CheckPersonalSpace(); PublishVelocity(); }


private void CheckPersonalSpace() { Collider[] humans = Physics.OverlapSphere( transform.position, personalSpaceRadius, humanLayer );


if (humans.Length > 0 && !_humanInPath) { _humanInPath = true; _agent.isStopped = true; Debug.Log("[SocialNav] Human in personal space — robot stopping.");


// Re-plan around the human after brief pause Invoke(nameof(ReplanAroundHuman), 2.0f); } }


private void ReplanAroundHuman() { // NavMesh will automatically find path around obstacle // if human has a NavMeshObstacle component _agent.isStopped = false; _humanInPath = false; Debug.Log("[SocialNav] Replanning path around human."); }


private void PublishVelocity() { // Publish robot velocity to ROS 2 for external monitoring Vector3 vel = _agent.velocity; var twistMsg = new TwistMsg { linear = new Vector3Msg { x = vel.z,   // Unity Z = ROS forward y = -vel.x,  // Unity X = ROS left z = 0.0 }, angular = new Vector3Msg { x = 0, y = 0, z = 0 } }; _ros.Publish(cmdVelTopic, twistMsg); } }

7.4.4 Scene Description

Figure 7.1 — HRI Test Scene: Office Corridor

Figure 7.1: Unity HDRP office corridor scene rendered with photorealistic lighting. Top-down schematic shows the NavMesh walkable area (grey), the original planned path (straight arrows), and the replanned socially-compliant path (arc left) triggered when the human avatar enters the robot's detection radius.

The scene demonstrates the key HRI challenge of socially compliant navigation: the robot must not merely avoid collision (a purely geometric task) but must also respect human personal space and communicate intent through motion — slowing before stopping, taking a wide arc rather than cutting sharply past the person.

7.5 ROS-TCP Connector

The ROS-TCP Connector provides bidirectional communication between Unity and a ROS 2 system. It operates over a TCP socket connection, with a ROS 2 node (ros_tcp_endpoint) on the ROS side acting as the message router.

7.5.1 Setup: ROS TCP Endpoint

On the ROS 2 side, install and launch the endpoint:

<h1>Install the endpoint package</h1>

sudo apt-get install ros-humble-ros-tcp-endpoint


<h1>Launch with default settings (port 10000)</h1> ros2 run ros_tcp_endpoint default_server_endpoint \ --ros-args -p ROS_IP:=192.168.1.100 -p ROS_TCP_PORT:=10000
ROS_IP must be the IP address of the machine running the endpoint — visible to the Unity Editor host. For development on a single machine, use 127.0.0.1.

7.5.2 Unity Side: ROSConnection

Configure the ROS connection in Unity:

  1. Robotics → ROS Settings in the menu bar.
  2. Set ROS IP Address to the endpoint IP.
  3. Set ROS Port to 10000.
  4. Set Protocol to ROS2.

In code, obtain the singleton ROSConnection:

ROSConnection ros = ROSConnection.GetOrCreateInstance();

7.5.3 Publishing Joint States from Unity to ROS 2

// Scripts/ROS/JointStatePublisher.cs

using System; using UnityEngine; using Unity.Robotics.ROSTCPConnector; using RosMessageTypes.Sensor; using RosMessageTypes.Std; using System.Collections.Generic;


public class JointStatePublisher : MonoBehaviour { [Serializable] public struct JointEntry { public string jointName; public ArticulationBody body; }


[Header("Joints")] public List<JointEntry> joints;


[Header("ROS")] public string topicName = "/humanoid/joint_states"; public float publishRateHz = 50f;


private ROSConnection _ros; private float _publishInterval; private float _timeSinceLastPublish = 0f;


void Start() { _ros = ROSConnection.GetOrCreateInstance(); _ros.RegisterPublisher<JointStateMsg>(topicName); _publishInterval = 1f / publishRateHz; }


void FixedUpdate() { _timeSinceLastPublish += Time.fixedDeltaTime; if (_timeSinceLastPublish < _publishInterval) return; _timeSinceLastPublish = 0f; PublishJointStates(); }


private void PublishJointStates() { int n = joints.Count; var msg = new JointStateMsg { header = new HeaderMsg { stamp = new RosMessageTypes.BuiltinInterfaces.TimeMsg { sec = (int)Time.time, nanosec = (uint)((Time.time - (int)Time.time) * 1e9) }, frame_id = "base_link" }, name = new string[n], position = new double[n], velocity = new double[n], effort = new double[n] };


for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) { var body = joints[i].body; msg.name[i] = joints[i].jointName;


// Unity stores position in degrees; ROS expects radians msg.position[i] = body.jointPosition[0] * Mathf.Deg2Rad; msg.velocity[i] = body.jointVelocity[0] * Mathf.Deg2Rad; msg.effort[i] = body.driveForce[0]; }


_ros.Publish(topicName, msg); } }

7.5.4 Subscribing to ROS 2 Velocity Commands in Unity

// Scripts/ROS/CmdVelSubscriber.cs

using UnityEngine; using Unity.Robotics.ROSTCPConnector; using RosMessageTypes.Geometry;


public class CmdVelSubscriber : MonoBehaviour { [Header("ROS")] public string topicName = "/cmd_vel";


[Header("Motion")] public float linearScale = 1.0f; public float angularScale = 1.0f;


private ROSConnection _ros; private Vector3 _linearVelocity = Vector3.zero; private float _angularVelocity = 0f; private Rigidbody _rb;


void Start() { _ros = ROSConnection.GetOrCreateInstance(); _ros.Subscribe<TwistMsg>(topicName, OnCmdVelReceived); _rb = GetComponent<Rigidbody>(); }


private void OnCmdVelReceived(TwistMsg msg) { // Convert ROS frame (forward=X, left=Y) to Unity frame (forward=Z, right=X) _linearVelocity = new Vector3( -(float)msg.linear.y * linearScale, 0f, (float)msg.linear.x * linearScale ); _angularVelocity = -(float)msg.angular.z * angularScale; }


void FixedUpdate() { if (_rb == null) return;


// Apply velocities to robot base _rb.velocity = transform.TransformDirection(_linearVelocity); _rb.angularVelocity = new Vector3(0f, _angularVelocity, 0f); } }
Figure 7.2 — ROS-TCP Connector Data Flow

Figure 7.2: ROS-TCP Connector data flow between Unity Engine and ROS 2. A single TCP connection on port 10000 carries bidirectional message traffic. Unity publishes joint states and camera images to ROS 2; ROS 2 Nav2 sends velocity commands back to Unity's NavMesh controller.

7.6 Advantages of Unity for Robotics

7.6.1 Photorealistic Rendering (HDRP)

Unity's High Definition Render Pipeline supports physically based materials, global illumination via real-time ray tracing, volumetric fog, and screen-space reflections [3]. In an HDRP scene, indoor environments appear visually authentic — the same quality used in architectural visualization and AAA game titles.

This visual quality directly impacts the transferability of trained perception models. When a depth estimation network or person detector is trained on Unity HDRP images, the domain gap to real camera footage is substantially smaller than when training on Gazebo's Ogre2 renderer output.

7.6.2 Behavioral Simulation

Unity's AI navigation stack provides NavMesh-based pathfinding for human avatars, with crowd simulation via NavMeshAgent instances that dynamically avoid each other and static obstacles [4]. Combined with Unity's Animator system — which supports motion-captured humanoid animations, blend trees for walk-run-stop transitions, and Inverse Kinematics for hand and foot placement — human avatars move with natural fluidity.

This behavioral realism is essential for studying proxemics (the study of human spatial behavior) in HRI scenarios: how close will a person approach a stationary robot, what approach angle do people prefer, and how do they interpret the robot's motion as indicating intent.

7.6.3 Physics Performance

For scenarios involving many rigid bodies — a robot manipulating objects in a warehouse with hundreds of boxes, for instance — Unity's PhysX-based physics engine offers competitive performance with parallel computation across CPU cores [5]. The ArticulationBody solver is particularly efficient for long kinematic chains.

7.6.4 Large Asset Ecosystem

The Unity Asset Store contains thousands of commercially licensed 3D environments, character models, animation packs, and material libraries. A realistic hospital ward, an airport departure hall, or a grocery store can be acquired and configured in hours. This dramatically reduces the time-to-experiment for HRI researchers who need specific environments.

7.7 Disadvantages of Unity

7.7.1 Less Robotics-Native Than Gazebo

Unity was designed as a game engine, not a robot simulator. Concepts native to robotics — URDF model descriptions, tf2 coordinate frames, sensor topic naming conventions, ROS message types — are all layered on top of Unity's game-centric architecture via third-party packages [6]. This creates impedance mismatches: the URDF importer requires manual correction of axis conventions, SDF features have no Unity equivalent, and Gazebo-specific sensor models (contact sensors, joint limit enforcement via DART) must be re-implemented in C#.

7.7.2 Commercial Licensing

Unity operates on a commercial licensing model. Unity Personal is free for organizations below a revenue threshold, but Unity Pro is required for larger teams and commercial products. Pricing has changed multiple times in recent years, and the September 2023 Runtime Fee announcement — which proposed per-install fees for distributed applications — caused significant disruption in the Unity community before being partially reversed [7]. Research groups should evaluate current licensing terms before building long-term infrastructure on Unity.

7.7.3 ROS Integration Latency

The ROS-TCP Connector routes all messages through a TCP socket, introducing measurable latency relative to ROS 2's DDS-native communication. For high-frequency control loops (e.g., a 1 kHz torque controller), the TCP bridge is not suitable. The connector is best used for lower-frequency interactions: publishing joint states at 50 Hz, receiving navigation goals, streaming camera images at 30 Hz. High-frequency control must remain within the Unity physics loop.

7.7.4 Limited Physics Accuracy vs Dedicated Simulators

Unity's PhysX implementation is optimized for game-quality physics, not engineering-accuracy simulation. Joint constraint violations, energy drift in long-running simulations, and inconsistent behavior at the boundaries of joint limits are known issues [8]. For precise controller development requiring accurate force-torque simulation, Gazebo's DART backend or MuJoCo's generalized coordinate physics are more reliable choices.

7.8 Unity vs Gazebo Comparison Table

CriterionUnity 2022 LTSGazebo Harmonic

Axis                 Unity (U)  Gazebo (G)   Winner ───────────────────────────────────────────────────── Rendering Quality       9          5           Unity Physics Accuracy        4          7           Gazebo ROS 2 Integration       6         10           Gazebo Human Simulation        9          5           Unity Sensor Realism          8          6           Unity Ease of Use             8          7           Unity RL / Training Scale     6          5           Unity Licensing Cost         Free*      Free         Tie


Overall:  Unity (U)  ██████████████████████████ (60/80) Gazebo (G) ████████████████████████   (45/80) * Unity free tier for non-commercial


Radar Summary: Rendering (10) U=9  G=5 / \ Human (9) U /   \ G Physics (7) Sim U=9   /     \   G=7 /         \ Sensor (8) U     ●     G ROS2 (10) Real U=8 \     center / G=10 \         / Ease (8) U \   / G (7) \ / RL Training U=6  G=5


Use Unity when: human avatars, photorealistic perception datasets, XR/VR Use Gazebo when: ROS 2 CI/CD, hardware-in-the-loop, physics-heavy control
Figure 7.3: Capability comparison between Unity HDRP and Gazebo Harmonic across six dimensions. Unity leads on rendering and human simulation; Gazebo leads on ROS 2 integration and physics accuracy. The two simulators are best viewed as complementary tools rather than substitutes.

7.9 Chapter Summary

Unity brings a set of capabilities to humanoid robotics simulation that physics-first simulators cannot provide: photorealistic rendering via HDRP, behaviorally realistic human avatars via NavMesh and Animator, and rapid scene construction via the Editor GUI and Asset Store. The ROS-TCP Connector bridges Unity's internal messaging with ROS 2, enabling the same robot software stack to receive sensor data and send commands regardless of whether the robot is physical or simulated.

The HRI scenario framework presented in this chapter — pedestrian detection via Physics.OverlapSphere, social navigation via NavMesh replanning, and bidirectional ROS 2 communication via the ROS-TCP Connector — provides a complete foundation for studying how humanoid robots navigate crowded human environments.

Unity is not, however, a universal solution. Its physics engine prioritizes performance over accuracy. Its ROS integration introduces latency unsuitable for high-frequency control. And its commercial licensing creates sustainability questions for open research projects.

The final simulator in Part III addresses a different frontier entirely: the use of synthetic data generation and reinforcement learning training in a physically accurate, photorealistic environment. NVIDIA Isaac Sim, presented in Chapter 8, is built not on a game engine but on NVIDIA's Omniverse platform — an industrial-grade, GPU-accelerated simulation framework with a physics engine (PhysX 5) capable of both game-quality rendering and engineering-accurate simulation. It is the environment in which the next generation of humanoid walking controllers is being trained.

Chapter 8: NVIDIA Isaac Sim — Photorealistic Simulation

Part III — Digital Twins — Simulation Environments
Version 1.0 — Draft | Author: Muhammad Faisal Laiq Siddiqui

8.1 Introduction — Why NVIDIA Built Isaac Sim and What Makes It Different

When NVIDIA announced Isaac Sim as a core product in its robotics ecosystem, the announcement carried the weight of a company that had spent a decade building the GPU infrastructure underlying modern AI. NVIDIA did not build a robot simulator because it is a robotics company — it built one because it recognized a fundamental bottleneck in the path from AI research to deployed robotic systems: the training data problem.

Reinforcement learning for robot locomotion requires millions of training steps. A humanoid robot attempting to learn bipedal walking from scratch needs to fall, recover, adjust, and iterate at a pace that physical hardware simply cannot support — both because of hardware wear and because wall-clock time constraints make millions of physical steps impractical. The solution is simulation, but simulation has historically been hampered by the sim-to-real gap: policies trained in visually simple or physically imprecise simulators fail on physical robots because the robot's sensors encounter a real world that looks and feels nothing like the training environment.

Isaac Sim attacks this problem from two directions simultaneously. First, it uses NVIDIA's Omniverse platform to render photorealistic scenes at speeds practical for training — scenes where lighting, materials, and geometry are physically based and visually indistinguishable from the real world. Second, it uses NVIDIA PhysX 5, a physics engine with research-grade rigid body and articulated body dynamics, to simulate robot physics accurately enough that trained policies transfer to hardware with minimal fine-tuning.

The third pillar is acceleration. Isaac Sim runs on NVIDIA GPUs, and through its integration with Isaac Lab (formerly OmniIsaacGymEnvs), it can run thousands of parallel simulation instances on a single GPU — compressing the wall-clock time required to train a walking policy from months to hours [1].

What follows is a practical guide to Isaac Sim 4.x — covering the Omniverse platform architecture, scene setup via the Python API, synthetic data generation with the Replicator API, reinforcement learning training for locomotion via Isaac Lab, and the sim-to-real pipeline that connects trained policies to physical robots.

8.2 Omniverse Architecture

8.2.1 USD: Universal Scene Description

Isaac Sim is built on Omniverse, NVIDIA's platform for collaborative, physically based 3D simulation. The foundational technology of Omniverse is USD (Universal Scene Description), originally developed by Pixar Animation Studios and open-sourced in 2016 [2].

USD is not merely a file format — it is a scene composition framework. A USD scene is a layered hierarchy of prims (primitive objects) organized in a stage. Each prim carries attributes (properties like position, scale, material binding) and relationships (references to other prims). The key innovation of USD is layer composition: multiple USD layers can be stacked and overridden in a non-destructive way, allowing different teams to work on different aspects of a scene (geometry, animation, physics) without modifying each other's data.

For robotics, USD provides:

  • UsdPhysics — the physics schema for rigid bodies, joints, and collision meshes
  • UsdGeom — geometry representation for meshes, capsules, and primitives
  • UsdShade — physically based material and shader definitions
  • UsdSkel — skeletal animation (relevant for deformable human avatars)

Robot descriptions imported from URDF are converted to USD prims, with each URDF link becoming a prim and each joint becoming a PhysicsJoint attribute on the USD stage.

8.2.2 Nucleus Server for Asset Management

Nucleus is the NVIDIA Omniverse asset management server. It provides versioned storage for USD assets, collaborative editing via a real-time collaboration protocol, and a web-based asset browser. In a simulation workflow:
  • Robot USD models are stored on Nucleus and referenced by simulation scripts via omniverse:// URIs
  • Environment assets (buildings, furniture, props) are streamed from NVIDIA's hosted Nucleus server (omniverse://assets.nvidia.com)
  • Trained policy weights and domain randomization configurations are versioned alongside scene assets

For local development without a Nucleus server, Isaac Sim supports local file paths and ships with a local Nucleus cache.

8.2.3 Kit SDK and Extensions

NVIDIA Omniverse is built on Kit, a modular application framework that composes capabilities from extensions — Python or C++ modules that extend the simulation with new functionality. Isaac Sim is a Kit application with a curated set of extensions:

ExtensionFunction
omni.isaac.corePython API for robots, scenes, and simulation control
omni.isaac.sensorCamera, LiDAR, IMU, contact sensor simulation
omni.replicator.coreSynthetic data generation and domain randomization
omni.isaac.motion_generationMotion planning (RMPflow, trajectory optimization)
omni.isaac.ros2_bridgeROS 2 communication bridge
omni.physxPhysX 5 integration

Extensions are loaded at startup via Kit's configuration files and can be enabled/disabled without modifying the simulator's core.

8.2.4 PhysX 5 Physics Engine Integration

Isaac Sim uses NVIDIA PhysX 5, a GPU-accelerated physics engine capable of simulating rigid bodies, articulated robots, deformable objects, fluids, and particle systems simultaneously [3]. For humanoid robotics, the relevant capabilities are:

Articulated body dynamics: PhysX 5 implements a Featherstone-style reduced coordinate articulation solver on the GPU. Multiple robot instances can be simulated in parallel on a single GPU, enabling batch reinforcement learning training. Temporal Gauss-Seidel (TGS) solver: The contact constraint solver in PhysX 5 uses a temporal Gauss-Seidel approach that is more stable than the legacy PGS solver, particularly for stiff contacts and complex foot-ground interactions. Continuous collision detection (CCD): For fast-moving robot links, CCD prevents tunneling — the artifact where a fast-moving object passes through a thin surface in a single timestep.

8.3 Isaac Sim Scene Setup

8.3.1 Launching Isaac Sim

Isaac Sim 4.x can be launched in two modes:

GUI mode (development and debugging): 
<h1>From the Isaac Sim installation directory</h1>

./isaac-sim.sh
Headless mode (training and CI): 
./isaac-sim.sh --headless --no-window

For Python scripting workflows, Isaac Sim can be launched from a standalone Python script that initializes the Kit application:

<h1>standalone_sim.py — Entry point for headless Isaac Sim sessions</h1>

from isaacsim import SimulationApp


<h1>Initialize the simulation application</h1> <h1>(must be done before any omni.* imports)</h1> simulation_app = SimulationApp({ "headless": True, "width": 1280, "height": 720, "renderer": "RayTracedLighting",  # or "PathTracing" for highest quality })


<h1>Now safe to import Isaac Sim extensions</h1>


from omni.isaac.core import World


<h1>... simulation code ...</h1>


simulation_app.close()

8.3.2 Importing a Robot from URDF/USD

<h1>scripts/setup_robot.py</h1>

from isaacsim import SimulationApp simulation_app = SimulationApp({"headless": False})


from omni.isaac.core import World from omni.isaac.core.robots import Robot from omni.isaac.core.utils.stage import add_reference_to_stage


<h1>Create simulation world</h1> world = World(stage_units_in_meters=1.0) world.scene.add_default_ground_plane()


<h1>Method 1: Import from URDF</h1> <h1>The URDF importer converts the URDF to USD on the Nucleus server</h1> result, prim_path = omni.kit.commands.execute( "URDFParseAndImportFile", urdf_path="/path/to/humanoid.urdf", import_config={ "merge_fixed_joints": False, "fix_base": False,           # Floating base for humanoid "import_inertia_tensor": True, "distance_scale": 1.0, "density": 0.0,             # Use URDF inertias "default_drive_type": "position", "default_drive_strength": 10000.0, "default_position_drive_damping": 100.0, "self_collision": False,     # Disable for performance "create_physics_scene": True, }, dest_path="/World/Humanoid", )


<h1>Method 2: Reference an existing USD asset</h1> add_reference_to_stage( usd_path="omniverse://localhost/robots/humanoid/humanoid.usd", prim_path="/World/Humanoid" )


<h1>Wrap the USD prim in an Isaac Core Robot object</h1> robot = Robot(prim_path="/World/Humanoid", name="humanoid") world.scene.add(robot)


<h1>Initialize world (creates physics context, initializes all prims)</h1> world.reset()


print(f"Robot DOF count: {robot.num_dof}") print(f"Robot DOF names: {robot.dof_names}")

8.3.3 Configuring Ground Plane, Lighting, and Physics Scene

<h1>scripts/configure_scene.py</h1>

from omni.isaac.core.utils.rotations import euler_angles_to_quat from pxr import UsdGeom, UsdLux, Gf, PhysxSchema, UsdPhysics


stage = omni.usd.get_context().get_stage()


<h1>Configure physics scene parameters</h1> physics_scene_prim = stage.GetPrimAtPath("/physicsScene") if not physics_scene_prim.IsValid(): physics_scene_prim = stage.DefinePrim("/physicsScene", "PhysicsScene")


physx_scene_api = PhysxSchema.PhysxSceneAPI.Apply(physics_scene_prim) physx_scene_api.CreateSolverTypeAttr("TGS")          # Temporal Gauss-Seidel physx_scene_api.CreateTimeStepsPerSecondAttr(500)     # 500 Hz physics physx_scene_api.CreateGpuFoundLostPairsCapacityAttr(1024) physx_scene_api.CreateGpuMaxRigidContactCountAttr(524288)


<h1>Gravity</h1> physics_scene_api = UsdPhysics.Scene.Apply(physics_scene_prim) physics_scene_api.CreateGravityDirectionAttr().Set(Gf.Vec3f(0, 0, -1)) physics_scene_api.CreateGravityMagnitudeAttr().Set(9.81)


<h1>Dome light (environment lighting from an HDR image)</h1> dome_light = UsdLux.DomeLight.Define(stage, "/World/Lighting/DomeLight") dome_light.CreateIntensityAttr(1000.0) dome_light.CreateTextureFileAttr( "omniverse://localhost/environments/indoor_office.hdr" )


<h1>Directional light (sun equivalent for indoor simulation)</h1> distant_light = UsdLux.DistantLight.Define(stage, "/World/Lighting/DistantLight") distant_light.CreateIntensityAttr(3000.0) distant_light.CreateAngleAttr(0.53) UsdGeom.Xformable(distant_light).AddRotateXYZOp().Set(Gf.Vec3f(-45, 0, 45))

8.3.4 Python API Example: Running the Simulation Loop

<h1>scripts/run_simulation.py</h1>

from isaacsim import SimulationApp simulation_app = SimulationApp({"headless": False})


from omni.isaac.core import World from omni.isaac.core.robots import Robot


world = World(physics_dt=1.0/500.0, rendering_dt=1.0/30.0) world.scene.add_default_ground_plane()


robot = Robot(prim_path="/World/Humanoid", name="humanoid") world.scene.add(robot) world.reset()


<h1>Get robot state dimensions</h1> num_dof = robot.num_dof print(f"Simulating {num_dof}-DOF humanoid robot")


<h1>Example: hold all joints at zero position</h1> target_positions = np.zeros(num_dof)


for i in range(1000): # Apply joint position targets robot.set_joint_position_targets(target_positions)


# Step the simulation world.step(render=True)


if i % 100 == 0: # Read current joint positions and velocities joint_positions = robot.get_joint_positions() joint_velocities = robot.get_joint_velocities() base_pose, base_orientation = robot.get_world_pose() print(f"Step {i}: base height = {base_pose[2]:.3f} m")


simulation_app.close()

8.4 Synthetic Data Generation

A central use case for Isaac Sim that distinguishes it from other simulators is synthetic data generation — using the simulation to automatically produce labeled training datasets for perception models. Training a human detection model or a person pose estimator on real camera data requires manual annotation of thousands of images. Synthetic data from Isaac Sim can produce millions of labeled images automatically, with ground-truth bounding boxes, depth maps, semantic segmentation masks, and instance segmentation — no manual annotation required.

8.4.1 Domain Randomization

Domain randomization is the technique of randomly varying simulation parameters during data generation to make the synthetic data distribution broad enough to cover the real-world distribution [4]. For humanoid robot perception, relevant parameters to randomize include:
  • Texture randomization: Replace floor, wall, and object textures with random materials from a library of thousands
  • Lighting randomization: Vary the intensity, color temperature, and position of light sources
  • Camera pose randomization: Sample camera positions and orientations from a distribution around the nominal mounting position
  • Object pose randomization: Randomize the positions, orientations, and scales of objects in the scene
  • Distractor objects: Add random objects not present in the target task to improve model robustness

8.4.2 Replicator API for Synthetic Data Generation

Isaac Sim's Replicator API (omni.replicator.core) provides a high-level Python interface for defining and executing domain randomization pipelines:

<h1>scripts/generate_synthetic_data.py</h1>

from isaacsim import SimulationApp simulation_app = SimulationApp({ "headless": True, "renderer": "RayTracedLighting", })


<h1>Load the simulation scene with human and humanoid robot</h1> omni.usd.get_context().open_stage( "omniverse://localhost/scenes/hri_scene.usd" )


with rep.new_layer(): # Define randomizable elements


# Camera mounted at robot head position camera = rep.create.camera( position=(0.1, 0, 1.5), look_at=(1.0, 0, 1.0), focal_length=35.0, )


# Randomize lighting lights = rep.get.light() with rep.trigger.on_frame(num_frames=5000): with lights: rep.modify.attribute( "intensity", rep.distribution.uniform(500, 3000) ) rep.modify.attribute( "color", rep.distribution.uniform( (0.8, 0.8, 0.8), (1.0, 1.0, 1.0) ) )


# Randomize human avatar pose (sample from a distribution of poses) human_prim = rep.get.prim_at_path("/World/HumanAvatar") with human_prim: rep.randomizer.rotation( distribution="uniform", min_rot=(0, -10, -180), max_rot=(0, 10, 180), ) rep.randomizer.scatter_2d( surface_prims=[rep.get.prim_at_path("/World/Floor")], min_distance=0.5, )


# Randomize floor texture floor_prim = rep.get.prim_at_path("/World/Floor") with floor_prim: rep.randomizer.texture( textures=rep.utils.get_usd_files( "omniverse://localhost/materials/floors/" ) )


# Configure render products (output frames) render_product = rep.create.render_product(camera, (1920, 1080))


# Configure annotators (ground-truth labels) rgb_annotator = rep.AnnotatorRegistry.get_annotator("rgb") depth_annotator = rep.AnnotatorRegistry.get_annotator("distance_to_image_plane") bbox2d_annotator = rep.AnnotatorRegistry.get_annotator("bounding_box_2d_tight") semantic_annotator = rep.AnnotatorRegistry.get_annotator( "semantic_segmentation", init_params={"colorize": True} )


rgb_annotator.attach([render_product]) depth_annotator.attach([render_product]) bbox2d_annotator.attach([render_product]) semantic_annotator.attach([render_product])


# Write output to disk in Pascal VOC format writer = rep.WriterRegistry.get("BasicWriter") writer.initialize( output_dir="/datasets/hri_synthetic/", rgb=True, distance_to_image_plane=True, bounding_box_2d_tight=True, semantic_segmentation=True, ) writer.attach([render_product])


<h1>Execute the randomization pipeline</h1> rep.orchestrator.run() simulation_app.close()

8.4.3 Annotator Outputs

The Replicator framework produces the following ground-truth annotations automatically:

AnnotatorOutput formatUse case
rgbPNG image (H×W×3)Training data input
distance_to_image_planeFloat32 array (H×W)Depth estimation training
bounding_box_2d_tightJSON with class labels and pixel coordinatesObject detection training
semantic_segmentationColorized PNG + label mapSemantic segmentation training
instance_segmentationPer-instance maskInstance segmentation
normalsFloat32 array (H×W×3)Surface normal estimation
motion_vectorsFloat32 array (H×W×2)Optical flow training
Figure 8.1 — Synthetic Data Generation Pipeline 
                          ISAAC REPLICATOR PIPELINE

┌────────────────┐    ┌─────────────────────────────┐    ┌──────────────────┐ │  USD Scene     │───►│  Replicator Domain          │───►│  RTX Renderer    │ │  (base scene)  │    │  Randomizer                 │    │  (RTRT / path    │ │                │    │                             │    │   tracing)       │ │  • robot URDF  │    │  Per-frame randomization:   │    │                  │ │  • table/obj.  │    │  • texture materials        │    │  Output: RGBA    │ │  • lighting rig│    │  • lighting color/intensity │    │  image  4K/30fps │ └────────────────┘    │  • object poses ± 15cm      │    └────────┬─────────┘ │  • robot joint config       │             │ │  • camera viewpoint         │             ▼ └─────────────────────────────┘  ┌──────────────────┐ │  Annotators      │ │  (ground truth)  │ │                  │ │  • BoundingBox2D │ │  • Depth map     │ │  • Segmentation  │ │  • Normals       │ └────────┬─────────┘ │ ▼ ┌──────────────────┐ │  Dataset Writer  │ │                  │ │  COCO JSON       │ │  PNG images      │ │  HDF5 tensors    │ └────────┬─────────┘ │ ▼ ┌──────────────────┐ │ Neural Network   │ │ Training         │ │ (TAO / PyTorch)  │ └──────────────────┘
Figure 8.1: Isaac Replicator synthetic data generation pipeline. A base USD scene is domain-randomized per frame (textures, lighting, object poses), rendered with RTX ray tracing, annotated with ground truth labels (bounding boxes, depth, segmentation), written to disk in COCO/HDF5 format, and fed to neural network training.

8.5 Isaac Lab: Reinforcement Learning Training for Locomotion

8.5.1 Isaac Lab Architecture

Isaac Lab (formerly OmniIsaacGymEnvs, renamed in Isaac Sim 4.0) is the reinforcement learning framework built on Isaac Sim for training robot policies [5]. Its architecture has three layers: Environment layer: Each training environment is a Python class that inherits from DirectRLEnv or ManagerBasedRLEnv. The environment defines the observation space, action space, reward function, reset conditions, and physics configuration. Task layer: A task configuration file (typically a Python dataclass) specifies all hyperparameters: number of parallel environments, episode length, reward scales, and curriculum settings. Manager layer: The ManagerBasedRLEnv pattern decomposes environment logic into modular managers: ObservationManager, RewardManager, TerminationManager, and CurriculumManager. This separation allows reward components to be added and removed without modifying the environment class.

Isaac Lab runs N parallel environments on the GPU simultaneously. A single NVIDIA RTX 4090 can run thousands of humanoid instances in parallel, with each instance stepping independently. This massive parallelism is what makes GPU-accelerated RL training practical.

8.5.2 Defining a Locomotion Task

<h1>tasks/locomotion/humanoid_walk_env_cfg.py</h1>

from dataclasses import dataclass, field from typing import List


from omni.isaac.lab.envs import ManagerBasedRLEnvCfg from omni.isaac.lab.managers import ( ObservationGroupCfg, ObservationTermCfg, RewardTermCfg, TerminationTermCfg, CurriculumTermCfg, SceneEntityCfg, ) from omni.isaac.lab.scene import InteractiveSceneCfg from omni.isaac.lab.assets import ArticulationCfg from omni.isaac.lab.terrains import TerrainImporterCfg


@dataclass class HumanoidWalkEnvCfg(ManagerBasedRLEnvCfg): """Configuration for humanoid bipedal walking task."""


# Simulation settings sim: sim_utils.SimulationCfg = field( default_factory=lambda: sim_utils.SimulationCfg( dt=0.005,            # 200 Hz physics render_interval=4,   # Render every 4 physics steps gravity=(0.0, 0.0, -9.81), ) )


# Number of parallel environments scene: InteractiveSceneCfg = field( default_factory=lambda: InteractiveSceneCfg( num_envs=4096, env_spacing=2.5,     # Spacing between parallel envs (meters) ) )


# Robot configuration robot: ArticulationCfg = field( default_factory=lambda: ArticulationCfg( prim_path="/World/envs/env_.*/Robot", spawn=sim_utils.UsdFileCfg( usd_path="omniverse://localhost/robots/humanoid.usd", ), init_state=ArticulationCfg.InitialStateCfg( pos=(0.0, 0.0, 1.05), joint_pos={ ".hip.": 0.0, ".knee.": 0.3, ".ankle.": -0.2, }, ), ) )


# Episode settings episode_length_s: float = 20.0  # seconds


# Observation space definition observations: ObservationGroupCfg = field( default_factory=lambda: ObservationGroupCfg( policy=ObservationGroupCfg( base_lin_vel=ObservationTermCfg( func=mdp.base_lin_vel, noise=mdp.GaussianNoiseCfg(std=0.1) ), base_ang_vel=ObservationTermCfg( func=mdp.base_ang_vel, noise=mdp.GaussianNoiseCfg(std=0.2) ), projected_gravity=ObservationTermCfg( func=mdp.projected_gravity, noise=mdp.GaussianNoiseCfg(std=0.05) ), velocity_commands=ObservationTermCfg( func=mdp.generated_commands, params={"command_name": "base_velocity"}, ), joint_pos=ObservationTermCfg( func=mdp.joint_pos_rel, noise=mdp.GaussianNoiseCfg(std=0.01) ), joint_vel=ObservationTermCfg( func=mdp.joint_vel_rel, noise=mdp.GaussianNoiseCfg(std=1.5) ), actions=ObservationTermCfg(func=mdp.last_action), ) ) )


# Reward function definition rewards: RewardTermCfg = field( default_factory=lambda: RewardTermCfg( # Forward velocity tracking reward track_lin_vel_xy_exp=RewardTermCfg( func=mdp.track_lin_vel_xy_exp, weight=1.0, params={"command_name": "base_velocity", "std": 0.25}, ), # Angular velocity tracking reward track_ang_vel_z_exp=RewardTermCfg( func=mdp.track_ang_vel_z_exp, weight=0.5, params={"command_name": "base_velocity", "std": 0.25}, ), # Upright posture reward upright_reward=RewardTermCfg( func=mdp.upright_body_reward, weight=0.5, ), # Penalties lin_vel_z_l2=RewardTermCfg(func=mdp.lin_vel_z_l2, weight=-2.0), ang_vel_xy_l2=RewardTermCfg(func=mdp.ang_vel_xy_l2, weight=-0.05), joint_torques_l2=RewardTermCfg(func=mdp.joint_torques_l2, weight=-1e-4), joint_acc_l2=RewardTermCfg(func=mdp.joint_acc_l2, weight=-2.5e-7), action_rate_l2=RewardTermCfg(func=mdp.action_rate_l2, weight=-0.01), # Foot contact reward (encourage alternating contact pattern) foot_air_time=RewardTermCfg( func=mdp.foot_air_time, weight=0.125, params={ "sensor_cfg": SceneEntityCfg("contact_forces"), "command_name": "base_velocity", "threshold": 0.5, }, ), ) )


# Termination conditions terminations: TerminationTermCfg = field( default_factory=lambda: TerminationTermCfg( time_out=TerminationTermCfg(func=mdp.time_out, time_out=True), base_contact=TerminationTermCfg( func=mdp.illegal_contact, params={ "sensor_cfg": SceneEntityCfg("contact_forces"), "threshold": 1.0, }, ), ) )

8.5.3 Running PPO Training

Isaac Lab integrates with RSL-RL, a lightweight reinforcement learning library optimized for GPU-parallel environments. The following command launches a PPO training run:

<h1>Train humanoid walking policy with PPO</h1>

python scripts/train.py \ --task=Isaac-Velocity-Flat-Humanoid-v1 \ --num_envs=4096 \ --max_iterations=5000 \ --headless \ --video \ --video_length=200 \ --video_interval=1000


<h1>Resume training from checkpoint</h1> python scripts/train.py \ --task=Isaac-Velocity-Flat-Humanoid-v1 \ --num_envs=4096 \ --resume \ --checkpoint=logs/rsl_rl/humanoid_walk/run_001/model_4000.pt

Key PPO hyperparameters for locomotion (stored in the task configuration):

<h1>config/ppo_humanoid.py</h1>

from omni.isaac.lab_tasks.utils.wrappers.rsl_rl import RslRlPpoActorCriticCfg


ppo_cfg = RslRlPpoActorCriticCfg( class_name="ActorCritic", actor_hidden_dims=[512, 256, 128], critic_hidden_dims=[512, 256, 128], activation="elu",


# PPO parameters num_steps_per_env=24, max_iterations=5000, save_interval=200, clip_param=0.2, entropy_coef=0.01, num_learning_epochs=5, num_mini_batches=4, learning_rate=1e-3, schedule="adaptive", gamma=0.99, lam=0.95, desired_kl=0.01, max_grad_norm=1.0, )

8.5.4 Monitoring with TensorBoard

Training metrics are logged automatically to TensorBoard:

<h1>Launch TensorBoard to monitor training</h1>

tensorboard --logdir=logs/rsl_rl/humanoid_walk/ --port 6006

Key metrics to monitor during locomotion training:

  • Episode reward mean: Should increase monotonically in early training, then plateau
  • Episode length mean: Should increase as the robot learns to stay upright for longer
  • Foot air time: Indicates the quality of the gait pattern — irregular values suggest the robot is hopping rather than walking
  • Joint torques L2: Should decrease as the robot learns energy-efficient motion
Figure 8.2 — Isaac Lab PPO Training Pipeline 
  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐

│                      GPU — NVIDIA RTX 4090 / A100                          │ │                                                                             │ │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐                               │ │  │  4096 Parallel Environments (Isaac Sim)  │                               │ │  │                                          │       ┌────────────────────┐ │ │  │  [Env 0001][Env 0002]...[Env 4096]       │──────►│  Observation       │ │ │  │  each: physics step at 200 Hz            │  obs  │  Buffer            │ │ │  │  joint states, base orientation,         │       │  (4096 × 48 dim)   │ │ │  │  foot contact, velocity                  │◄──────│                    │ │ │  └─────────────────────────────────────────┘  act  └────────────────────┘ │ │            │ reward signal                              │ obs batch         │ │            ▼                                            ▼                   │ │  ┌───────────────────┐                       ┌──────────────────────────┐  │ │  │  Reward Function  │                       │  PPO Agent               │  │ │  │                   │                       │                          │  │ │  │  + forward vel    │                       │  Actor network:          │  │ │  │  - The PPO agent updates its actor-critic network every 4096 steps. Training progress is monitored via TensorBoard; the trained policy is exported to ONNX for deployment on Jetson AGX Orin hardware.

8.6 Sim-to-Real Transfer Pipeline

Training a policy in simulation and deploying it on physical hardware — the sim-to-real transfer — is the ultimate test of simulation fidelity. A policy that cannot transfer provides no practical value regardless of its simulated performance [6]. Isaac Sim's transfer pipeline consists of three stages: policy export, initial deployment, and hardware fine-tuning.

8.6.1 Exporting the Trained Policy to ONNX

PyTorch policies must be exported to ONNX (Open Neural Network Exchange) format for deployment on hardware that may not have a full PyTorch installation:

<h1>scripts/export_policy.py</h1>

from omni.isaac.lab_tasks.utils.wrappers.rsl_rl import RslRlOnPolicyRunnerCfg


<h1>Load the trained checkpoint</h1> checkpoint_path = "logs/rsl_rl/humanoid_walk/run_001/model_5000.pt" checkpoint = torch.load(checkpoint_path, map_location="cpu")


<h1>Extract and reconstruct the actor network</h1> actor_state_dict = { k.replace("actor.", ""): v for k, v in checkpoint["model_state_dict"].items() if k.startswith("actor.") }


<h1>Define actor architecture (must match training config)</h1> class ActorNetwork(torch.nn.Module): def __init__(self, obs_dim: int, action_dim: int): super().__init__() self.network = torch.nn.Sequential( torch.nn.Linear(obs_dim, 512), torch.nn.ELU(), torch.nn.Linear(512, 256), torch.nn.ELU(), torch.nn.Linear(256, 128), torch.nn.ELU(), torch.nn.Linear(128, action_dim), )


def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor: return self.network(x)


<h1>Instantiate and load weights</h1> OBS_DIM = 48    # Match the observation space dimension from training ACTION_DIM = 12  # Number of joint targets


actor = ActorNetwork(OBS_DIM, ACTION_DIM) actor.load_state_dict(actor_state_dict) actor.eval()


<h1>Export to ONNX</h1> dummy_input = torch.zeros(1, OBS_DIM) torch.onnx.export( actor, dummy_input, "deployments/humanoid_walk_policy.onnx", export_params=True, opset_version=17, input_names=["observation"], output_names=["action"], dynamic_axes={ "observation": {0: "batch_size"}, "action": {0: "batch_size"}, }, ) print("Policy exported to humanoid_walk_policy.onnx")


<h1>Verify the export</h1>


session = ort.InferenceSession("deployments/humanoid_walk_policy.onnx") test_obs = np.zeros((1, OBS_DIM), dtype=np.float32) action = session.run(["action"], {"observation": test_obs})[0] print(f"Test inference output shape: {action.shape}") print(f"Test inference output: {action}")

8.6.2 Deploying to Physical Robot

The deployment controller runs ONNX inference at the control rate (typically 50–200 Hz) and sends joint position commands to the hardware's low-level controller:

<h1>robot/deploy_policy.py</h1>

from robot_sdk import RobotHardwareInterface  # Hardware-specific SDK


<h1>Load policy</h1> session = ort.InferenceSession( "deployments/humanoid_walk_policy.onnx", providers=["CPUExecutionProvider"], )


<h1>Initialize hardware interface</h1> robot = RobotHardwareInterface() robot.initialize() robot.enable_motors()


print("Deployment controller running. Press Ctrl+C to stop.") OBS_DIM = 48 obs_buffer = np.zeros(OBS_DIM, dtype=np.float32) prev_action = np.zeros(12, dtype=np.float32)


CONTROL_DT = 0.02  # 50 Hz control loop


try: while True: loop_start = time.monotonic()


# Read sensors from hardware imu_data = robot.get_imu() joint_pos = robot.get_joint_positions()   # (12,) array in radians joint_vel = robot.get_joint_velocities()  # (12,) array in rad/s


# Assemble observation vector (must match training) obs_buffer[0:3]   = imu_data.linear_velocity      # base linear velocity obs_buffer[3:6]   = imu_data.angular_velocity     # base angular velocity obs_buffer[6:9]   = imu_data.projected_gravity    # projected gravity vector obs_buffer[9:12]  = [0.3, 0.0, 0.0]              # velocity command (example) obs_buffer[12:24] = joint_pos                     # joint positions obs_buffer[24:36] = joint_vel                     # joint velocities obs_buffer[36:48] = prev_action                   # last action


# Run policy inference action = session.run( ["action"], {"observation": obs_buffer.reshape(1, -1)} )[0].flatten()


# Scale action to joint position targets action_scaled = action * 0.5  # Action scale factor (tune per robot)


# Send to hardware robot.set_joint_position_targets(action_scaled) prev_action = action_scaled.copy()


# Maintain control rate elapsed = time.monotonic() - loop_start sleep_time = CONTROL_DT - elapsed if sleep_time > 0: time.sleep(sleep_time)


except KeyboardInterrupt: print("Stopping deployment controller.") robot.disable_motors() robot.shutdown()

8.6.3 Fine-Tuning on Real Hardware

The initial sim-to-real deployment rarely achieves the same performance as simulation due to unmodeled dynamics: motor friction that varies with temperature, gearbox backlash, flexible link deformation, and sensor noise characteristics that differ from simulation. Fine-tuning addresses this gap.

The two most effective fine-tuning strategies for humanoid locomotion are:

System identification: Measure physical robot parameters (motor friction, joint stiffness, inertia tensors) using controlled experiments and update the simulation model to match. This improves the fidelity of future simulation training runs and reduces the sim-to-real gap for subsequent policies. Rapid online adaptation (RMA): A two-phase training approach where the base policy is trained in simulation with domain randomization, and a small adaptation module is trained online on hardware data to correct for the specific hardware instance [7]. The adaptation module learns a compact representation of hardware-specific dynamics from recent observation history, without requiring explicit system identification.

8.7.2 Omniverse Cloud

For researchers and teams without access to high-end local workstations, NVIDIA Omniverse Cloud provides Isaac Sim as a cloud service accessible via browser or API. Relevant cloud compute options:

AWS EC2 instances with NVIDIA GPUs:
  • p3.2xlarge: V100 16 GB — suitable for data generation, not for large-scale RL training
  • p4d.24xlarge: 8× A100 40 GB — suitable for large-scale Isaac Lab RL training
  • p5.48xlarge: 8× H100 80 GB — for the largest training runs
Google Cloud Platform:
  • a2-highgpu-1g: A100 40 GB — cost-effective for moderate training runs
  • a3-highgpu-8g: 8× H100 80 GB — for production-scale training
NVIDIA DGX Cloud: Pre-provisioned multi-GPU clusters with Omniverse pre-installed, accessible on demand through NVIDIA's cloud offering.

Cost considerations: Large-scale RL training on cloud hardware can be expensive. A 5,000-iteration training run on 4 A100 GPUs (approximately 24 wall-clock hours) incurs cloud compute costs that researchers should budget explicitly. The ability to checkpoint and resume training is therefore essential.

Selection guidance: The choice of simulator should follow the primary development task:
  • Locomotion and whole-body control development: Isaac Sim + Isaac Lab (GPU parallel RL) or Gazebo (open-source, ROS-native control development)
  • HRI and social navigation research: Unity (photorealistic human avatars, behavioral simulation)
  • CI/CD and automated regression testing: Gazebo (headless, open-source, no hardware dependencies)
  • Perception model training with synthetic data: Isaac Sim (Replicator API, photorealistic rendering, ground-truth annotators)
  • Multi-simulator cross-validation: Run the same policy in all three simulators and compare behavior as a sim-to-real risk mitigation strategy

8.9 Chapter Summary

The case for Isaac Sim is not that it is the easiest simulator to run — it is that no other simulator solves the training data problem at the scale humanoid locomotion requires. Photorealistic rendering, research-grade PhysX 5 contact dynamics, and 4096 parallel GPU environments on a single card are not incremental improvements over Gazebo or Unity; they are architectural preconditions for the reinforcement learning workflows that produce transferable walking policies.

We traced the Omniverse platform architecture from USD's compositional scene format, through Nucleus asset management, to the Kit SDK and its robotics extensions. We set up a simulation scene using the omni.isaac.core Python API, configured PhysX 5 with a TGS solver appropriate for humanoid contact simulation, and connected GPU-accelerated rendering to a realistic lighting model.

The synthetic data generation pipeline demonstrated how Isaac Sim's Replicator API can produce millions of annotated training images with domain randomization — closing the data bottleneck for perception model development. The Isaac Lab reinforcement learning framework showed how 4096 parallel humanoid environments can be trained simultaneously on a single GPU, with a complete PPO locomotion task definition, training commands, and TensorBoard monitoring.

The sim-to-real transfer pipeline — ONNX policy export, deployment controller, and hardware fine-tuning via rapid motor adaptation — provided the practical bridge from simulated policy to physical robot.

Together with Chapter 6 (Gazebo) and Chapter 7 (Unity), this chapter completes Part III's examination of the digital twin simulation landscape. The three simulators are not competitors; they are complementary tools in the humanoid robotics engineering toolchain, each suited to a different stage of the development lifecycle.

Part IV shifts from the simulation environment to the production AI stack running inside the robot. Chapter 9 opens Part IV with an examination of Isaac ROS — hardware-accelerated perception — the graph-based GXF execution framework and zero-copy Nitros transport layer that make real-time Visual SLAM, object detection, and point cloud processing feasible on embedded hardware. The perception substrate built in Chapter 9 is the direct output consumer of the simulation and training infrastructure built here.

Chapter 9: Isaac ROS — Hardware-Accelerated Perception

Part IV — The AI Brain — NVIDIA Isaac Platform
Version 1.0 — Draft | Author: Muhammad Faisal Laiq Siddiqui

9.1 Introduction — The Perception Bottleneck in Humanoid Robotics

A humanoid robot standing in a hospital corridor faces a problem that would stagger any programmer encountering it for the first time. In a single second, the robot must ingest approximately 30 stereo image pairs from its head-mounted cameras, a 360-degree point cloud from its chest-mounted LiDAR, IMU data streaming at 400 Hz from its torso, and joint state feedback from 28 or more motor encoders — all simultaneously. It must identify the nurse walking toward it, estimate her trajectory, locate the medication cart to its right, re-localize itself relative to a map it built during the previous shift, and issue corrective torques to its leg actuators to avoid drifting into the corridor wall, all before taking its next step in approximately 40 milliseconds.

This is the perception bottleneck: the computational demand of processing real-world sensor data at the speed required for safe, reactive humanoid behavior.

The naive solution — running all perception algorithms on a general-purpose CPU — fails immediately. A stereo depth estimation pipeline running on a modern server CPU might achieve 5–8 frames per second. A real-time humanoid navigation stack requires 25–30 Hz minimum for safe obstacle avoidance. The arithmetic does not work. The bottleneck is not algorithmic sophistication; it is raw computational throughput, specifically the kind of massively parallel throughput that only a GPU can provide.

NVIDIA's answer to this challenge is Isaac ROS: a collection of hardware-accelerated, GPU-optimized ROS 2 packages that run perception algorithms at the speeds humanoid robots require [1]. Isaac ROS does not replace the ROS 2 framework that robotics engineers already know — it extends it. A developer who understands ROS 2 publishers, subscribers, and launch files can adopt Isaac ROS incrementally, replacing CPU-bound perception nodes with GPU-accelerated equivalents while keeping the rest of their software stack unchanged.

This chapter dissects Isaac ROS in depth. We examine the architectural foundations — GXF, Nitros, and the hardware targets — before exploring the three perception domains that matter most for humanoid operation: Visual SLAM for localization and mapping, object detection and segmentation for scene understanding, and 3D point cloud processing for geometry-aware navigation. We then address the deployment of these capabilities on NVIDIA Jetson Orin hardware, analyzing the performance, power, and thermal constraints that govern what is achievable on an edge device weighing less than a kilogram.

9.2 Isaac ROS Architecture

9.2.1 What Isaac ROS Is

Isaac ROS (Robot Operating System) is a curated set of ROS 2 packages, maintained by NVIDIA, that implement computer vision and perception algorithms accelerated on NVIDIA GPUs and DLA (Deep Learning Accelerator) units [1]. The project is publicly available on GitHub under the NVIDIA-ISAAC-ROS organization and targets ROS 2 Humble Hawksbill as its primary supported distribution, running on Ubuntu 22.04 LTS.

The packages cover a broad perception surface:

  • Visual SLAM (isaac_ros_visual_slam) — camera-based localization and mapping
  • Object detection (isaac_ros_detectnet) — bounding-box detection using DetectNet v2
  • Image segmentation (isaac_ros_image_segmentation) — semantic pixel labeling
  • Stereo depth (isaac_ros_stereo_image_proc) — disparity computation and point cloud generation
  • AprilTag detection (isaac_ros_apriltag) — fiducial marker localization
  • Human pose estimation (isaac_ros_human_pose_estimation) — skeleton keypoint detection
  • Depth segmentation (isaac_ros_depth_segmentation) — ESS (Efficient Stereo Segmentation) neural network
  • nvblox (isaac_ros_nvblox) — GPU-accelerated 3D scene reconstruction

What distinguishes Isaac ROS from vanilla ROS 2 packages is not the algorithms themselves — Visual SLAM and object detection algorithms existed before Isaac ROS — but the execution substrate. Every Isaac ROS package is built on top of GXF and communicates internally via Nitros, two architectural layers described in the following sections.

9.2.2 GXF — Graph Execution Framework

The Graph Execution Framework (GXF) is NVIDIA's runtime for building and executing computation graphs on heterogeneous hardware [2]. It provides the execution engine beneath every Isaac ROS node.

In a standard ROS 2 node, computation is driven by callbacks: a subscription callback fires when a message arrives on a topic, the callback executes on a CPU thread, and the result is published to another topic. This model works well for low-rate, compute-light operations. For high-throughput perception pipelines — where a new stereo pair arrives every 33 ms and must be processed in under 10 ms to leave headroom for downstream nodes — the callback overhead and memory copy costs become significant.

GXF solves this by treating computation as a directed acyclic graph (DAG) of codelets (lightweight compute units), components (configurations and data containers), and entities (collections of components). The graph is compiled at initialization time. At runtime, GXF schedules codelet execution across CPU threads and GPU streams according to data availability, minimizing latency by eliminating unnecessary synchronization points.

For a typical Isaac ROS visual perception pipeline, GXF manages:

  1. Image ingestion — receiving raw bytes from the camera driver
  2. Format conversion — converting from sensor_msgs/Image to a GPU-native NV12 or RGB format
  3. Neural network inference — dispatching TensorRT inference on the GPU
  4. Post-processing — non-maximum suppression, coordinate transformation
  5. Output publication — writing results back to a ROS 2 topic

The entire sequence executes with minimal host-device memory transfers because GXF maintains tensor data in GPU memory throughout, using CUDA streams to overlap operations.

Figure 9.1 — GXF Pipeline Architecture 
                         GXF Graph (GPU-Resident Data Flow)

CUDA Stream 0 ┌─────────────────┐          │               ┌──────────────────────┐ │  Camera Input   │          ▼               │                      │ │  (USB / CSI)    │ ──────► [Image Decoder]  │   GPU MEMORY POOL    │ │  RAW BAYER      │          │               │                      │ └─────────────────┘          │ GPU tensor    │  [1920×1080×3 FP16]  │ │ 1920×1080×3  │  [640×640×3  FP16]   │ ▼               │  [25200×85  FP16]    │ [Resize+Normalize]     │                      │ │               └──────────────────────┘ │ 640×640×3 FP16 │               CUDA Stream 1 ▼               │ [TensorRT Inference] ◄─┘ (YOLOv8 .engine) │ │ 25200×85 FP16 │ (raw detections) ▼ [NMS Post-process] (Non-Max Suppress) │ │ Detection2DArray ▼ [ROS 2 Publisher] │ ▼ /detections (ROS 2 topic) Downstream nodes (tracker, planner)


Data stays in GPU memory throughout — no CPU copies between codelets CUDA streams allow overlapping decode/inference/post-process
Figure 9.1: GXF directed acyclic graph pipeline for object detection. Camera input passes through Image Decoder, Resize+Normalize, TensorRT Inference (YOLOv8), NMS Post-processing, and ROS 2 Publisher codelets. Tensor data remains in GPU memory throughout; CUDA streams enable overlapping operations for maximum throughput.

9.2.3 Nitros — NVIDIA Isaac Transport for ROS

Standard ROS 2 message passing over DDS (Data Distribution Service) involves serialization overhead that becomes costly for large messages. A single 1920×1080 RGB image serialized as a sensor_msgs/Image message occupies approximately 6.2 MB. At 30 Hz, this represents 186 MB/s of data that must be serialized, copied from GPU memory to CPU memory, transmitted over DDS, and then deserialized — before the receiving node can copy it back to GPU memory for processing.

Nitros (NVIDIA Isaac Transport for ROS) eliminates the bulk of this overhead through zero-copy message passing for GPU-resident data [3]. Instead of copying image data through the CPU, Nitros maintains the data in GPU memory and passes a handle (a lightweight reference structure containing a CUDA memory pointer and metadata) through the DDS layer. The receiving Isaac ROS node uses this handle to access the data directly from GPU memory without a host-device round trip.

This approach requires that both the publishing node and the subscribing node are Nitros-compatible — meaning both are built on GXF. When a Nitros-compatible node connects to a non-Nitros node (for example, a standard ROS 2 C++ node), Nitros automatically inserts a conversion node that materializes the handle into a standard ROS 2 message, preserving compatibility with the broader ROS 2 ecosystem.

The practical performance impact of Nitros is significant. In benchmarks published by NVIDIA for the Isaac ROS AprilTag package, Nitros-enabled pipelines achieve approximately 2× lower latency and 25–40% higher throughput compared to equivalent pipelines using standard ROS 2 message passing for HD resolution images [3].

Figure 9.2 — Nitros Zero-Copy Message Passing 
  STANDARD ROS 2 MESSAGE PASSING          NITROS ZERO-COPY TRANSPORT

─────────────────────────────────       ────────────────────────────────────


Node A (GPU)                            Node A (GPU) GPU Memory [6.2 MB image]               GPU Memory [6.2 MB image] │                                       │ │ ① GPU→CPU copy (~1.2 ms)             │ ① Register buffer handle ▼                                       │   (pointer + metadata, 8 bytes) CPU Buffer [6.2 MB]                        │ │                                       ▼ │ ② Serialize to DDS (~0.8 ms)       NitrosHandle [8 bytes] ▼                                       │ DDS Wire Format [6.2+ MB]                  │ ② DDS sends handle only │                                       │   (<0.01 ms, 8 bytes on wire) │ ③ Network/IPC (~0.3 ms)              ▼ ▼                                    DDS Wire [8 bytes] DDS Receive Buffer                         │ │                                       │ ③ Handle delivery │ ④ Deserialize (~0.8 ms)              ▼ ▼                                    NitrosHandle [8 bytes] CPU Buffer [6.2 MB]                        │ │                                       │ ④ Direct GPU memory access │ ⑤ CPU→GPU copy (~1.2 ms)            ▼ ▼                                    GPU Memory [6.2 MB] (same buffer) Node B GPU [6.2 MB] Node B reads directly — NO COPIES


Total latency: ~4.3 ms                 Total latency: ~0.1 ms Data moved:    24.8 MB                 Data moved:    16 bytes CPU involved:  Yes (all steps)         CPU involved:  No (buffer registration only)


At 30 Hz: 186 MB/s overhead           At 30 Hz: <0.5 KB/s overhead
Figure 9.2: Nitros zero-copy transport compared to standard ROS 2 message passing for a 1920×1080 RGB camera image (6.2 MB). Standard ROS 2 requires five data movement steps totaling ~4.3 ms latency and 24.8 MB of data movement. Nitros passes an 8-byte GPU buffer handle, reducing latency to ~0.1 ms and eliminating CPU involvement in the data path.

9.2.4 Supported Hardware

Isaac ROS is designed to run on three categories of hardware:

NVIDIA Jetson Orin family — the primary deployment target for mobile robots. The Jetson AGX Orin (32 GB and 64 GB variants), Jetson Orin NX (8 GB and 16 GB), and Jetson Orin Nano (4 GB and 8 GB) all support Isaac ROS. Each Jetson Orin SoC integrates a CPU (ARM Cortex-A78AE), an NVIDIA Ampere GPU, and one or two DLA cores, allowing perception workloads to be split between the GPU and the DLA based on latency and power requirements. Desktop NVIDIA GPU — for development and testing on a workstation. Isaac ROS runs on Ubuntu 22.04 with any NVIDIA GPU that supports CUDA 11.4+ (Turing architecture or newer). Developers routinely prototype Isaac ROS pipelines on an RTX 3090 or RTX 4090 before cross-compiling or containerizing for Jetson deployment. DRIVE Orin — NVIDIA's automotive-grade SoC, sharing the same architecture as Jetson AGX Orin but rated for automotive safety standards. While primarily targeted at autonomous vehicle applications, DRIVE Orin is occasionally used in high-end humanoid research platforms where automotive-grade reliability is required.

9.3 Visual SLAM with isaac_ros_visual_slam

9.3.1 What Visual SLAM Is

SLAM (Simultaneous Localization and Mapping) is the computational problem of building a map of an unknown environment while simultaneously determining the agent's location within that map [4]. For a humanoid robot operating in a building it has never entered, SLAM answers two questions at once: "Where am I?" and "What does this place look like?" Visual SLAM (VSLAM) solves this problem using cameras as the primary sensor, in contrast to LiDAR SLAM which relies on laser ranging. The distinction matters enormously for humanoid robots because cameras are lightweight (a global-shutter stereo camera pair weighs approximately 50–100 g), power-efficient (5–15 W), and informationally rich — a camera captures texture, color, and appearance features that enable long-term place recognition, a capability LiDAR point clouds lack due to their structural sparsity in feature-poor environments.

Isaac ROS Visual SLAM (isaac_ros_visual_slam) implements cuVSLAM, NVIDIA's GPU-accelerated visual-inertial odometry (VIO) library [5]. The algorithm fuses stereo camera images and IMU measurements using a tightly coupled optimization backend, estimating the robot's 6-DoF pose (position and orientation in 3D space) at up to 60 Hz.

The cuVSLAM processing pipeline involves:

  1. Feature extraction — detecting and describing keypoints (corners, blobs) in each camera frame using GPU-parallelized feature detectors
  2. Stereo matching — establishing correspondences between left and right camera images to recover 3D point positions
  3. IMU pre-integration — integrating IMU measurements between camera frames to predict motion
  4. Visual-inertial fusion — optimizing a sliding-window factor graph combining visual reprojection constraints and IMU dynamics
  5. Loop closure detection — recognizing previously visited locations to correct accumulated drift
  6. Map management — maintaining a sparse 3D map of tracked landmarks for relocalization

9.3.2 Setup and Launch Commands

Isaac ROS Visual SLAM is distributed as a Docker container. The recommended deployment method uses the Isaac ROS Common dev container [1]:

<h1>Pull the Isaac ROS common container (JetPack 6.x / CUDA 12.x compatible)</h1>

docker pull nvcr.io/nvidia/isaac/ros:aarch64-ros2_humble_nova_developer_kit-3.1.0


<h1>Install the visual SLAM package inside the container</h1> sudo apt-get install -y ros-humble-isaac-ros-visual-slam


<h1>Launch the visual SLAM node with default configuration</h1> ros2 launch isaac_ros_visual_slam isaac_ros_visual_slam.launch.py

For a system with a ZED 2i stereo camera, the launch file requires remapping the standard topic names:

ros2 launch isaac_ros_visual_slam isaac_ros_visual_slam.launch.py \

image_left_topic:=/zed/zed_node/left/image_rect_color \ image_right_topic:=/zed/zed_node/right/image_rect_color \ camera_info_left_topic:=/zed/zed_node/left/camera_info \ camera_info_right_topic:=/zed/zed_node/right/camera_info \ imu_topic:=/zed/zed_node/imu/data
