9.3.4 Python Launch File Example

The following Python launch file demonstrates a complete Visual SLAM launch configuration suitable for a humanoid robot equipped with a RealSense D435i RGB-D camera and an external IMU:

<h1>File: launch/vslam_realsense.launch.py</h1>

<h1>Isaac ROS Visual SLAM launch for Intel RealSense D435i</h1> <h1>ROS 2 Humble / Isaac ROS 3.x</h1>


from launch_ros.actions import ComposableNodeContainer, Node from launch_ros.descriptions import ComposableNode from launch.substitutions import LaunchConfiguration from launch.actions import DeclareLaunchArgument


def generate_launch_description(): # --- Launch arguments --- use_imu_arg = DeclareLaunchArgument( 'use_imu', default_value='True', description='Enable IMU fusion for improved accuracy' )


# --- RealSense camera node --- # Publishes /camera/color/image_raw, /camera/depth/image_rect_raw, # /camera/imu topics. realsense_node = Node( package='realsense2_camera', executable='realsense2_camera_node', name='realsense', parameters=[{ 'enable_gyro': True, 'enable_accel': True, 'unite_imu_method': 'linear_interpolation', 'enable_sync': True, 'depth_module.profile': '640x480x30', 'rgb_camera.profile': '640x480x30', }] )


# --- Visual SLAM node (composable, runs in shared container for Nitros) --- visual_slam_node = ComposableNode( package='isaac_ros_visual_slam', plugin='nvidia::isaac_ros::visual_slam::VisualSlamNode', name='visual_slam', parameters=[{ # Enable IMU fusion 'enable_imu_fusion': LaunchConfiguration('use_imu'), # Rectified images are already undistorted — no additional rectification 'rectified_images': True, # Denoise images on GPU before feature extraction 'denoise_input_images': False, # Number of CPU threads for optimization backend 'num_cameras': 2, # Map cell size in meters for memory-bounded map management 'map_cell_size': 0.05, # Gravity direction in camera frame (z-down for standard mounting) 'imu_params.gyroscope_noise_density': 0.0003394, 'imu_params.accelerometer_noise_density': 0.02, }], remappings=[ # Remap to RealSense topic names ('stereo_camera/left/image', '/camera/infra1/image_rect_raw'), ('stereo_camera/right/image', '/camera/infra2/image_rect_raw'), ('stereo_camera/left/camera_info', '/camera/infra1/camera_info'), ('stereo_camera/right/camera_info', '/camera/infra2/camera_info'), ('visual_slam/imu', '/camera/imu'), ] )


# --- Composable node container (enables Nitros zero-copy within container) --- vslam_container = ComposableNodeContainer( name='vslam_container', namespace='', package='rclcpp_components', executable='component_container_mt',   # Multi-threaded container composable_node_descriptions=[visual_slam_node], output='screen' )


return launch.LaunchDescription([ use_imu_arg, realsense_node, vslam_container, ])

9.3.5 Accuracy Comparison: VSLAM vs. LiDAR SLAM for Indoor Environments

The choice between VSLAM and LiDAR SLAM for indoor humanoid navigation involves trade-offs across multiple dimensions. Table 9.1 summarizes the comparison based on published evaluation studies and NVIDIA benchmark data.

Table 9.1 — Visual SLAM vs. LiDAR SLAM for Indoor Humanoid Navigation
Criterionisaac_ros_visual_slam (cuVSLAM)LiDAR SLAM (e.g., SLAM Toolbox + Nav2)
Localization accuracy (ATE)~1–3 cm in well-textured indoor environments [5]~0.5–1.5 cm with mid-grade LiDAR [6]
Tracking frequencyUp to 60 Hz on Jetson AGX Orin10–20 Hz typical (LiDAR scan rate)
Failure modesTexture-poor areas (white walls), rapid rotation, motion blurGlass walls (transparent surfaces), dynamic objects
Sensor cost~$200–$600 for stereo camera~$500–$15,000 for 3D LiDAR
Sensor weight50–200 g250–900 g (mid-grade 3D LiDAR)
Power consumption3–8 W (camera + computation)8–18 W (LiDAR + computation)
Long-term driftHigher (requires loop closure or GPS correction)Lower (dense geometric features resist drift)
Performance under varying lightingDegrades in darkness without IR illuminationUnaffected by lighting

For humanoid robots intended for indoor operation under consistent lighting conditions — hospital corridors, warehouses, office buildings — cuVSLAM provides accuracy sufficient for navigation while maintaining the sensor weight and power advantages critical for whole-body power budgets. In environments with large glass-walled spaces or outdoor operation, sensor fusion combining VSLAM with a sparse LiDAR or radar is preferable.

9.4 Object Detection and Segmentation

9.4.1 isaac_ros_detectnet — Running DetectNet v2 on GPU

isaac_ros_detectnet is the Isaac ROS package that runs inference on DetectNet v2 models, a convolutional neural network architecture designed for efficient object detection [7]. The package accepts a TensorRT engine file as input and publishes vision_msgs/Detection2DArray messages containing bounding box coordinates, class labels, and confidence scores for each detected object.

DetectNet v2 is a single-shot detector that produces grid-based predictions: for each cell in a downsampled feature grid, it predicts the probability that an object center lies in that cell, along with the bounding box offsets. Compared to two-stage detectors like Faster R-CNN, DetectNet v2 trades some accuracy for substantially lower inference latency, making it well suited for real-time robot operation.

The package interfaces with the TensorRT runtime through GXF, enabling the neural network inference to execute entirely on the GPU without returning intermediate results to the CPU. Post-processing (confidence thresholding and non-maximum suppression) also runs as GXF codelets.

9.4.2 isaac_ros_image_segmentation — Semantic Segmentation with NVIDIA TAO Models

isaac_ros_image_segmentation provides semantic segmentation: assigning a class label to every pixel in an image [1]. This capability enables a humanoid robot to understand the semantic category of each region in its visual field — distinguishing floor from wall, person from furniture, obstacle from navigable space — at the pixel level rather than the coarser bounding-box level.

The package supports models from the NVIDIA TAO Toolkit (Train, Adapt, Optimize), NVIDIA's platform for fine-tuning pre-trained perception models on custom datasets [8]. The segmentation backbone is typically a lightweight encoder-decoder architecture (such as UNet with a MobileNet v2 or ResNet-18 encoder) that balances segmentation quality against inference speed.

9.4.3 Model Deployment Pipeline: TAO to TensorRT to Isaac ROS Node

The deployment path for a custom perception model in an Isaac ROS humanoid system follows a well-defined pipeline:

Figure 9.3 — Model Deployment Pipeline: TAO to Isaac ROS 
Stage 1            Stage 2              Stage 3              Stage 4             Stage 5

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────── [Cloud /           [TAO Fine-tune]      [TensorRT            [Isaac ROS           [ROS 2 Workstation]                            Conversion]           Node]                Output]


┌──────────────┐   ┌──────────────┐    ┌──────────────┐     ┌──────────────┐    ┌───────┐ │ TAO          │   │ Custom       │    │ tao-converter│     │ GXF          │    │ /det  │ │ Pre-trained  │──►│ Dataset      │───►│ tool         │────►│ TensorRT     │───►│ ect   │ │ Model        │   │              │    │              │     │ Codelet      │    │ ions  │ │              │   │ Transfer     │    │ INT8 calib.  │     │              │    │       │ │ .pth weights │   │ learning     │    │ FP16 optim.  │     │ .engine file │    │ Det   │ │ architecture │   │ 500 imgs     │    │              │     │ runs on      │    │ 2D    │ │ definition   │   │ fine-tune    │    │ Platform:    │     │ Jetson GPU   │    │ Array │ │              │   │ 10 epochs    │    │ Jetson AGX   │     │              │    │       │ │ (NVIDIA NGC) │   │              │    │ (ARM+CUDA)   │     │ 120 fps      │    │ seg   │ └──────────────┘   └──────────────┘    └──────────────┘     │ 11ms latency │    │ mask  │ │                    │               └──────────────┘    └───────┘ │                    │ File: .etlt          File: .engine (encrypted          (TensorRT TAO model)          optimized)

Figure 9.3: Five-stage model deployment pipeline from TAO pre-trained model to Isaac ROS runtime on Jetson. Each stage outputs a specific file format: PyTorch weights → .etlt (TAO encrypted) → .engine (TensorRT optimized for target hardware) → ROS 2 topic output. The pipeline is designed to run entirely on NVIDIA hardware from cloud training to edge deployment. Step 1: TAO Fine-tuning

from launch_ros.actions import ComposableNodeContainer from launch_ros.descriptions import ComposableNode


def generate_launch_description(): detectnet_node = ComposableNode( package='isaac_ros_detectnet', plugin='nvidia::isaac_ros::detectnet::DetectNetDecoderNode', name='detectnet_decoder', parameters=[{ 'label_names': ['person', 'obstacle', 'door', 'cart'], 'confidence_threshold': 0.35, 'nms_threshold': 0.45, 'bounding_box_scale': 35.0, 'bounding_box_offset': 0.5, }] )


tensor_rt_node = ComposableNode( package='isaac_ros_tensor_rt', plugin='nvidia::isaac_ros::dnn_inference::TensorRTNode', name='tensor_rt_inference', parameters=[{ 'model_file_path': '/models/detectnet_v2_fp16.engine', 'engine_file_path': '/models/detectnet_v2_fp16.engine', 'input_binding_names': ['input_1'], 'output_binding_names': ['output_cov/Sigmoid', 'output_bbox/BiasAdd'], 'verbose': False, 'force_engine_update': False, }] )


container = ComposableNodeContainer( name='detectnet_container', namespace='', package='rclcpp_components', executable='component_container_mt', composable_node_descriptions=[tensor_rt_node, detectnet_node], output='screen' )


return launch.LaunchDescription([container])

9.4.4 ROS 2 Message Types for Detections

Isaac ROS DetectNet publishes detection results using the vision_msgs/Detection2DArray message type, which is the standard ROS 2 representation for 2D object detections [9]. The message structure is:

vision_msgs/Detection2DArray

├── std_msgs/Header header │   ├── stamp (ROS time) │   └── frame_id (e.g., "camera_link") └── Detection2D[] detections └── (per detection) ├── sensor_msgs/RegionOfInterest bbox │   ├── uint32 x_offset  # left edge in pixels │   ├── uint32 y_offset  # top edge in pixels │   ├── uint32 height │   ├── uint32 width │   └── bool do_rectify ├── ObjectHypothesisWithPose[] results │   ├── string class_id   # e.g., "person" │   └── float64 score     # confidence 0.0 – 1.0 └── sensor_msgs/Image source_img  # optional

9.4.5 Python Subscriber for Detection Results

The following Python ROS 2 node demonstrates subscribing to detection results from isaac_ros_detectnet and performing secondary processing — in this case, estimating the distance to each detected person using the stereo depth topic:

#!/usr/bin/env python3

""" detection_distance_estimator.py Subscribes to Isaac ROS DetectNet results and stereo depth map. Estimates distance to detected persons for humanoid collision avoidance.


ROS 2 Humble / isaac_ros_detectnet 3.x Author: Muhammad Faisal Laiq Siddiqui """


from rclpy.node import Node from rclpy.callback_groups import ReentrantCallbackGroup from rclpy.executors import MultiThreadedExecutor


from vision_msgs.msg import Detection2DArray from sensor_msgs.msg import Image from std_msgs.msg import Float32MultiArray from message_filters import ApproximateTimeSynchronizer, Subscriber


from cv_bridge import CvBridge


class DetectionDistanceEstimator(Node): """ Fuses object detections with depth image to estimate obstacle distances. Uses ApproximateTimeSynchronizer to align detection and depth timestamps. """


def __init__(self): super().__init__('detection_distance_estimator')


# Allow concurrent callbacks (detections and depth may arrive simultaneously) self._cb_group = ReentrantCallbackGroup()


# --- Synchronized subscribers for detections + depth --- self._det_sub = Subscriber( self, Detection2DArray, '/detectnet/detections', qos_profile=10 ) self._depth_sub = Subscriber( self, Image, '/stereo_camera/depth/image_rect_raw', qos_profile=10 )


# Synchronize with 50 ms tolerance (camera at 30 Hz → ~33 ms between frames) self._sync = ApproximateTimeSynchronizer( [self._det_sub, self._depth_sub], queue_size=10, slop=0.05 ) self._sync.registerCallback(self._synchronized_callback)


# Publisher: distance to each detected object class self._dist_pub = self.create_publisher( Float32MultiArray, '/perception/obstacle_distances', 10 )


self._bridge = CvBridge() self.get_logger().info('DetectionDistanceEstimator initialized.')


def _synchronized_callback( self, detections_msg: Detection2DArray, depth_msg: Image ) -> None: """ Process synchronized detections and depth image. For each detected bounding box, sample the median depth within the box region to estimate distance. """ # Convert depth image from ROS message to numpy array # Depth image is typically 16UC1 (uint16, millimetres) or 32FC1 (float32, metres) try: if depth_msg.encoding == '16UC1': depth_array = self._bridge.imgmsg_to_cv2(depth_msg, '16UC1').astype(np.float32) depth_array /= 1000.0  # Convert mm to metres elif depth_msg.encoding == '32FC1': depth_array = self._bridge.imgmsg_to_cv2(depth_msg, '32FC1') else: self.get_logger().warn(f'Unsupported depth encoding: {depth_msg.encoding}') return except Exception as e: self.get_logger().error(f'Depth conversion failed: {e}') return


img_h, img_w = depth_array.shape distances_out = Float32MultiArray()


for detection in detections_msg.detections: if not detection.results: continue


# Highest-confidence hypothesis best = max(detection.results, key=lambda r: r.score) class_id = best.id       # e.g., "person" confidence = best.score


# Skip low-confidence detections if confidence < 0.40: continue


# Extract bounding box pixel coordinates (clamped to image bounds) bbox = detection.bbox x1 = int(max(0, bbox.center.position.x - bbox.size_x / 2.0)) y1 = int(max(0, bbox.center.position.y - bbox.size_y / 2.0)) x2 = int(min(img_w - 1, bbox.center.position.x + bbox.size_x / 2.0)) y2 = int(min(img_h - 1, bbox.center.position.y + bbox.size_y / 2.0))


# Sample depth within the central 50% of the bounding box # (avoid edges where depth is often unreliable) x_margin = int((x2 - x1) * 0.25) y_margin = int((y2 - y1) * 0.25) roi_depth = depth_array[ y1 + y_margin : y2 - y_margin, x1 + x_margin : x2 - x_margin ]


# Filter invalid depth readings (0 = no measurement) valid_depths = roi_depth[roi_depth > 0.1] if valid_depths.size == 0: continue


# Use median for robustness against outliers distance_m = float(np.median(valid_depths)) distances_out.data.append(distance_m)


self.get_logger().debug( f'Detected {class_id} (conf={confidence:.2f}) at {distance_m:.2f} m' )


self._dist_pub.publish(distances_out)


def main(args=None): rclpy.init(args=args) node = DetectionDistanceEstimator() # MultiThreadedExecutor allows the synchronized callback to run # concurrently with any timer or service callbacks executor = MultiThreadedExecutor(num_threads=4) executor.add_node(node) try: executor.spin() finally: executor.shutdown() node.destroy_node() rclpy.shutdown()


if __name__ == '__main__': main()

9.5 3D Point Cloud Processing

9.5.1 isaac_ros_stereo_image_proc — Generating Point Clouds from Stereo Cameras

isaac_ros_stereo_image_proc is the Isaac ROS replacement for the standard ROS 2 stereo_image_proc package [1]. It performs disparity estimation and point cloud generation entirely on the GPU, achieving substantially higher throughput than the CPU-based implementation.

The package contains two composable nodes:

  • DisparityNode — computes a dense disparity map from the rectified left and right images using GPU-accelerated Semi-Global Block Matching (SGBM) or a learned stereo matching network
  • PointCloudNode — reprojects the disparity map to a 3D point cloud in the sensor_msgs/PointCloud2 format, using the camera calibration parameters

A complete stereo processing chain requires rectified images as input. Rectification corrects for lens distortion and aligns the epipolar lines horizontally, a prerequisite for stereo matching. If the input camera driver does not publish rectified images, the standard image_proc package or its Isaac ROS equivalent must be inserted upstream.

<h1>File: launch/stereo_point_cloud.launch.py</h1>

from launch_ros.actions import ComposableNodeContainer from launch_ros.descriptions import ComposableNode


def generate_launch_description(): # Disparity computation node (GPU-accelerated SGBM) disparity_node = ComposableNode( package='isaac_ros_stereo_image_proc', plugin='nvidia::isaac_ros::stereo_image_proc::DisparityNode', name='disparity', parameters=[{ 'backends': 'CUDA',        # Use CUDA backend for GPU acceleration 'max_disparity': 64.0,     # Maximum disparity in pixels (baseline-dependent) 'window_size': 5,          # Matching window size }], remappings=[ ('left/image_rect', '/stereo/left/image_rect'), ('right/image_rect', '/stereo/right/image_rect'), ('left/camera_info', '/stereo/left/camera_info'), ('right/camera_info', '/stereo/right/camera_info'), ] )


# Point cloud generation node point_cloud_node = ComposableNode( package='isaac_ros_stereo_image_proc', plugin='nvidia::isaac_ros::stereo_image_proc::PointCloudNode', name='point_cloud', parameters=[{ 'use_color': True,         # Include RGB color in point cloud 'unit_scaling': 1.0,       # Output in metres }], remappings=[ ('left/image_rect_color', '/stereo/left/image_rect_color'), ('left/camera_info', '/stereo/left/camera_info'), ] )


container = ComposableNodeContainer( name='stereo_proc_container', namespace='', package='rclcpp_components', executable='component_container_mt', composable_node_descriptions=[disparity_node, point_cloud_node], output='screen' )


return launch.LaunchDescription([container])

9.5.2 Filtering and Downsampling with PCL

Point clouds generated from stereo cameras at 30 Hz can contain 300,000–1,000,000 points per frame, depending on image resolution and scene geometry. Direct consumption of unfiltered point clouds by the navigation stack imposes unnecessary computational load. Three standard filtering operations reduce this overhead:

Voxel grid downsampling reduces point density by replacing all points within each voxel (a small cubic volume element) with their centroid. A 2 cm voxel grid typically reduces a 500,000-point cloud to fewer than 50,000 points while preserving the geometry relevant for navigation. PassThrough filter removes points outside a specified range along a given axis — for example, removing all points more than 5 meters ahead (too far to affect immediate navigation) or closer than 20 cm (potentially on the robot's own body). StatisticalOutlierRemoval identifies and removes points whose mean distance to their k-nearest neighbors exceeds a threshold by more than a specified number of standard deviations, eliminating isolated measurement noise. 
<h1>File: point_cloud_filter_node.py</h1>

<h1>Applies PCL-style filtering to stereo point cloud before passing to Nav2</h1>


from rclpy.node import Node from sensor_msgs.msg import PointCloud2


class PointCloudFilter(Node): """ Downsamples and filters a stereo point cloud before passing it to the Nav2 navigation stack. """


def __init__(self): super().__init__('point_cloud_filter')


# Declare configurable parameters self.declare_parameter('voxel_size', 0.02)       # 2 cm voxel grid self.declare_parameter('min_range', 0.25)         # Minimum distance in metres self.declare_parameter('max_range', 5.0)          # Maximum distance in metres


self._voxel_size = self.get_parameter('voxel_size').value self._min_range = self.get_parameter('min_range').value self._max_range = self.get_parameter('max_range').value


self._sub = self.create_subscription( PointCloud2, '/stereo/points2', self._cloud_callback, 10 ) self._pub = self.create_publisher( PointCloud2, '/stereo/points2_filtered', 10 )


def _cloud_callback(self, msg: PointCloud2) -> None: """Downsample and range-filter incoming point cloud.""" # Read XYZ fields from PointCloud2 message points = np.array( list(pc2.read_points(msg, field_names=('x', 'y', 'z'), skip_nans=True)) )


if points.shape[0] == 0: return


# PassThrough: keep only points within depth range (x-axis = forward) depth = np.sqrt(points[:, 0]2 + points[:, 1]2 + points[:, 2]**2) mask = (depth >= self._min_range) & (depth <= self._max_range) points = points[mask]


if points.shape[0] == 0: return


# Voxel grid downsampling via numpy binning vox = self._voxel_size bins = np.floor(points / vox).astype(int) # Use structured array to find unique voxels _, unique_idx = np.unique(bins, axis=0, return_index=True) points = points[unique_idx]


# Publish filtered cloud header = msg.header filtered_msg = pc2.create_cloud_xyz32(header, points.tolist()) self._pub.publish(filtered_msg) self.get_logger().debug(f'Published {points.shape[0]} filtered points.')

9.5.3 Passing Point Clouds to the Navigation Stack

The Nav2 navigation stack in ROS 2 Humble accepts obstacle data from sensor sources through the costmap_2d system, which builds occupancy grids from sensor observations [10]. Point clouds from the stereo camera are integrated into the local and global costmaps via the PointCloud2 observation source plugin:

<h1>nav2_params.yaml — excerpt for stereo point cloud obstacle source</h1>

local_costmap: local_costmap: ros__parameters: observation_sources: stereo_cloud stereo_cloud: topic: /stereo/points2_filtered sensor_frame: camera_link data_type: PointCloud2 obstacle_max_range: 3.5     # metres obstacle_min_range: 0.25 raytrace_max_range: 4.5 raytrace_min_range: 0.0 clearing: true marking: true

9.6.2 JetPack SDK Setup

NVIDIA JetPack 6.x is the software stack for Jetson Orin, bundling Linux for Tegra (L4T), CUDA 12.x, cuDNN 8.x, TensorRT 8.x, and the Jetson-specific multimedia APIs into a single installable SDK [11].

Flashing JetPack onto a Jetson AGX Orin devkit requires NVIDIA SDK Manager on a Ubuntu 22.04 host:

<h1>Step 1: Download SDK Manager from developer.nvidia.com/sdk-manager</h1>

<h1>Step 2: Install SDK Manager</h1> sudo dpkg -i sdkmanager_*.deb sudo apt-get install -f

<h1>After flashing, verify JetPack version on the Jetson</h1> cat /etc/nv_tegra_release <h1>Expected output (JetPack 6.0): R36 (release), REVISION: 2.0, ...</h1>

9.6.3 Swap Memory and Power Mode Configuration

Jetson Orin modules ship with the default power mode configured for low-power operation. For maximum Isaac ROS performance, increase the power mode and configure swap memory:

<h1>On the Jetson target: set maximum performance power mode</h1>

sudo nvpmodel -m 0        # MAXN mode — all CPU cores, maximum GPU frequency sudo jetson_clocks         # Lock clocks to maximum frequency


<h1>Configure 8 GB swap file to supplement physical RAM during model loading</h1> sudo fallocate -l 8G /swapfile sudo chmod 600 /swapfile sudo mkswap /swapfile sudo swapon /swapfile


<h1>Make swap persistent across reboots</h1> echo '/swapfile none swap sw 0 0' | sudo tee -a /etc/fstab


<h1>Verify power mode and thermal status</h1> sudo tegrastats

9.6.4 Installing ROS 2 Humble on JetPack 6.x

<h1>Add ROS 2 apt repository</h1>

sudo apt-get install -y software-properties-common sudo add-apt-repository universe sudo apt-get update && sudo apt-get install -y curl


curl -sSL https://raw.githubusercontent.com/ros/rosdistro/master/ros.key \ -o /usr/share/keyrings/ros-archive-keyring.gpg


echo "deb [arch=$(dpkg --print-architecture) \ signed-by=/usr/share/keyrings/ros-archive-keyring.gpg] \ http://packages.ros.org/ros2/ubuntu jammy main" \ | sudo tee /etc/apt/sources.list.d/ros2.list > /dev/null


sudo apt-get update sudo apt-get install -y ros-humble-desktop     # Full desktop install sudo apt-get install -y python3-colcon-common-extensions

9.6.5 Real-Time Constraint Analysis: Latency Budget for the Perception Pipeline

A humanoid robot walking at 1.0 m/s with a safety stop distance of 0.5 m must detect and respond to an obstacle within 500 ms from first observation. A practical control loop requires a much tighter perception latency budget — typically no more than 50–100 ms from image capture to navigation costmap update [12].

Table 9.3 breaks down the latency budget for a representative Isaac ROS perception pipeline on the Jetson Orin NX 16GB:

Table 9.3 — Perception Pipeline Latency Budget (Jetson Orin NX 16GB, 640×480 stereo, 30 Hz)
StageComponentTypical Latency
Image capture and USB transferRealSense D435i driver~5 ms
Nitros conversion (USB → GPU)isaac_ros_nitros~1 ms
Stereo disparityisaac_ros_stereo_image_proc~4 ms
Visual SLAM pose updateisaac_ros_visual_slam~8 ms
Object detection (DetectNet FP16)isaac_ros_detectnet + TensorRT~12 ms
Point cloud filteringCustom PCL filter node~3 ms
Costmap updateNav2 costmap_2d~5 ms
Total pipeline latency~38 ms
Remaining headroom to 50 ms budget~12 ms

This analysis confirms that a Jetson Orin NX 16GB running Isaac ROS can meet a 50 ms perception latency budget for a 640×480 stereo input at 30 Hz, with approximately 12 ms of headroom for scheduler jitter and DDS communication overhead.

9.8 Chapter Summary and Bridge to Chapter 10

Hardware acceleration is not a luxury for humanoid perception — it is the enabling condition. A stereo depth pipeline running on a CPU stalls at 5 to 8 frames per second; the same pipeline running through Isaac ROS's GXF graph and Nitros zero-copy transport achieves 30 to 60 Hz on the Jetson Orin NX with headroom to spare. The architectural choices that make this possible — graph-based execution that avoids redundant CPU copies, Tensor Core-accelerated cuVSLAM that tracks pose at 60 Hz, TensorRT-optimized detection networks that identify objects in under 20 ms — are not implementation details. They are the reason a humanoid robot can perceive and act in real time on hardware that fits inside a torso panel.

Deploying these capabilities requires careful hardware selection within the Jetson Orin family, with the Orin NX 16GB representing a practical performance-power balance for mid-tier humanoid platforms and the AGX Orin serving demanding multi-camera, multi-model configurations. The latency budget analysis establishes that a 50 ms perception loop is achievable on Orin NX hardware with 640×480 stereo input — a result that satisfies the reactive requirements of a walking humanoid at pedestrian speeds.

The key insight from this chapter is that hardware acceleration is not a luxury for humanoid perception — it is a necessity. The gap between what a CPU-based system can deliver and what real-time embodied intelligence requires is not bridgeable with algorithmic optimization alone. It requires the GPU-accelerated, memory-efficient, pipeline-parallelized architecture that Isaac ROS provides.

Chapter 10 builds directly on this foundation. Once the perception pipeline is validated on a development workstation running Isaac ROS in a Docker container, the next engineering challenge is deploying the entire robot software stack to the edge: cross-compiling ROS 2 nodes for the ARM architecture, containerizing the stack with NVIDIA L4T base images, converting PyTorch perception models to TensorRT engines, and managing the power and thermal constraints of a mobile compute platform. These are the topics of Chapter 10.

Chapter 10: From Simulation to Edge — Jetson Deployment

Part IV — The AI Brain — NVIDIA Isaac Platform
Version 1.0 — Draft | Author: Muhammad Faisal Laiq Siddiqui

10.1 Introduction — The Edge Deployment Challenge

Building a humanoid robot perception pipeline in simulation is, by comparison, straightforward. The development workstation sits on a desk with a 300 W GPU, 64 GB of RAM, unlimited cooling airflow, and an Ethernet cable supplying uninterrupted network connectivity. The developer writes Python code, runs it, inspects the results in RViz2, and iterates. When the pipeline works, it feels complete.

Then comes the edge deployment challenge — and the developer discovers that the gap between "works on my laptop" and "works on the robot" is wider than any other gap in robotics engineering.

An AI humanoid robot carries its compute with it. The processing unit attached to the torso or shoulder must operate within a strict weight budget (every extra 100 g shifts the robot's center of mass), a strict power budget (every extra watt depletes the battery faster and requires more actuator work to stabilize thermally-induced vibrations), and a strict thermal budget (a module that throttles its clock speed when hot will stall the perception pipeline at the worst possible moment — in the middle of a task).

The transition from simulation to edge deployment forces a series of engineering decisions that compound in complexity: Which Jetson Orin module has enough compute without exceeding the power budget? Should ROS 2 nodes be cross-compiled for ARM or run inside Docker containers? How should PyTorch perception models be converted to TensorRT to run at acceptable latency on Orin's GPU? When the robot is operating at full load with all sensors active, what is the total power draw from the compute module, and does it fit within the power allocation from the battery management system?

This chapter answers these questions systematically. We begin with a detailed survey of the Jetson Orin product family, establishing the specifications and trade-offs of each module. We then walk through the complete JetPack SDK setup, the containerized deployment strategy using Docker on Jetson, the TensorRT optimization pipeline for production inference, and the thermal and power management strategies that keep the system stable under sustained load. The chapter concludes with a complete, end-to-end deployment example: a ROS 2 object detection node running TensorRT-optimized inference on a Jetson Orin NX, packaged in a Docker container, with a documented Dockerfile, launch file, and measured latency and power figures.

10.2 Jetson Orin Product Family

10.2.1 Orin Nano — Entry-Level Edge AI

The Jetson Orin Nano is NVIDIA's most accessible Orin-family module, targeting applications where cost and physical size are the primary constraints [1]. It is available in two memory configurations: 4 GB and 8 GB of LPDDR5 unified memory.

The Orin Nano 8GB delivers 40 TOPS (Tera Operations Per Second) of AI performance — a figure that, while modest by Orin family standards, substantially exceeds the previous-generation Jetson Nano (472 GOPS). It integrates a 6-core ARM Cortex-A78AE CPU, a 1024-core NVIDIA Ampere GPU with 32 Tensor Cores, and a single DLA core. The 8 GB memory configuration provides 68 GB/s of memory bandwidth, which is the primary determinant of throughput for memory-bound inference workloads.

For humanoid robots, the Orin Nano 8GB is practical for lightweight perception tasks — running a single object detection model at modest resolution, providing basic visual odometry — but is insufficient for the full multi-model, multi-camera perception stacks described in Chapter 9. It is best suited for task-specific satellite processors: handling a single camera feed dedicated to hand detection for manipulation tasks, or running a lightweight proximity alert system.

The Orin Nano 4GB variant, at 20 TOPS and 34 GB/s bandwidth, is primarily a cost-driven choice for applications with extremely limited perception requirements. Its 10 W maximum power draw makes it attractive for low-power sensor pods.

10.2.2 Orin NX — Mid-Range Performance

The Jetson Orin NX is the workhorse module for mid-tier humanoid robots and autonomous mobile platforms [1]. Available in 8 GB and 16 GB configurations, it shares the same physical form factor as the Orin Nano (69.6 mm × 45 mm) while delivering substantially higher performance.

The Orin NX 16GB achieves 100 TOPS and includes two DLA cores alongside the Ampere GPU, enabling parallelization between neural network inference on the DLA (for power-efficient sustained inference) and GPU compute for geometrically intensive tasks like stereo disparity and SLAM. The 16 GB memory configuration with 102 GB/s bandwidth supports concurrent multi-model inference without memory contention.

As established in Chapter 9's latency budget analysis, the Orin NX 16GB can sustain the Isaac ROS perception stack — VSLAM, object detection, and point cloud processing — for 640×480 stereo input at 30 Hz within a 50 ms latency budget and a 25 W power envelope. This combination makes it the practical choice for humanoid robots in the 15–25 kg total weight class.

The Orin NX 8GB (70 TOPS, 8 GB memory) provides a power-optimized alternative at approximately 10–20 W, accepting reduced parallel model capacity in exchange for a smaller battery drain.

10.2.3 AGX Orin — High-Performance Flagship

The Jetson AGX Orin is NVIDIA's highest-performance embedded AI module and the reference deployment target for demanding humanoid platforms [1]. Available in 32 GB and 64 GB configurations, it substantially exceeds the Orin NX in every performance dimension.

The AGX Orin uses the same Orin SoC as the NX but enables a larger power envelope (15–60 W configurable), unlocking higher GPU and CPU clock frequencies. The 2048-core Ampere GPU with 64 Tensor Cores and 204 GB/s memory bandwidth enables simultaneous execution of multiple high-resolution perception models. Two DLA cores can be loaded with fixed-function inference workloads (running continuously at ~5 W each) while the GPU handles variable-throughput tasks.

For humanoid platforms requiring multi-camera coverage (head, chest, hands), full-resolution (1080p) VSLAM, and simultaneous manipulation-specific perception (grasping point estimation, object 6-DoF pose estimation), the AGX Orin 32GB or 64GB is the appropriate choice. Its 100 mm × 87 mm form factor is larger than the NX module but remains embeddable in a torso compute bay.

10.3 JetPack SDK Setup

10.3.1 Flashing JetPack 6.x Using SDK Manager

JetPack 6.x (based on L4T R36) is the required software stack for all Jetson Orin modules [2]. Flashing from source using NVIDIA SDK Manager ensures a clean installation with all components correctly versioned and calibrated for the target hardware.

Prerequisites on the host Ubuntu 22.04 machine: 
<h1>Install SDK Manager from NVIDIA (requires NVIDIA Developer account)</h1>

<h1>Download sdkmanager_*.deb from https://developer.nvidia.com/sdk-manager</h1>


sudo dpkg -i sdkmanager_2.2.0-12021_amd64.deb sudo apt-get install -f    # Fix any dependency issues


<h1>Connect Jetson AGX Orin devkit via USB-C to host machine</h1> <h1>Put devkit into Force Recovery Mode:</h1> <h1>1. Hold FORCE RECOVERY button</h1> <h1>2. Press and release RESET button</h1> <h1>3. Release FORCE RECOVERY button</h1>


<h1>Verify device is detected in recovery mode</h1> lsusb | grep NVIDIA <h1>Expected: "Bus 001 Device 005: ID 0955:7023 NVIDIA Corp. APX"</h1>


<h1>Launch SDK Manager</h1> sdkmanager

In SDK Manager, select:

  • Product: Jetson
  • Hardware: Jetson AGX Orin Developer Kit (or appropriate module)
  • SDK Version: JetPack 6.0 or newer
  • Components: Jetson Linux, CUDA 12.x, cuDNN 8.x, TensorRT 8.x, VPI 3.x

The flash process takes approximately 20–40 minutes depending on host machine speed and selected components.

10.3.2 Post-Flash Verification

After flashing and first boot, verify the installation:

<h1>Check L4T version</h1>

cat /etc/nv_tegra_release <h1>Expected: R36 (release), REVISION: 2.0</h1>


<h1>Verify CUDA installation</h1> nvcc --version <h1>Expected: Cuda compilation tools, release 12.x, V12.x.xxx</h1>


<h1>Verify TensorRT installation</h1> python3 -c "import tensorrt; print(tensorrt.__version__)" <h1>Expected: 8.x.x.x</h1>


<h1>Check cuDNN</h1> python3 -c "import ctypes; ctypes.CDLL('libcudnn.so.8')" <h1>No output = success</h1>


<h1>Run hardware diagnostic</h1> sudo tegrastats

10.3.3 Swap Memory and Power Mode Configuration

For a humanoid robot's primary compute module, configuring adequate swap memory prevents out-of-memory crashes during large model loading:

<h1>Create 8 GB swap file (recommended for Orin NX 16GB)</h1>

sudo fallocate -l 8G /swapfile sudo chmod 600 /swapfile sudo mkswap /swapfile sudo swapon /swapfile


<h1>Verify swap</h1> swapon --show <h1>Expected: NAME       TYPE SIZE USED PRIO</h1> <h1>          /swapfile  file   8G   0B   -2</h1>


<h1>Persist swap across reboots</h1> echo '/swapfile none swap sw 0 0' | sudo tee -a /etc/fstab


<h1>Set maximum performance power mode (nvpmodel -m 0 = MAXN)</h1> sudo nvpmodel -m 0 sudo jetson_clocks     # Lock CPU/GPU/EMC clocks to maximum frequency


<h1>Verify current power mode</h1> sudo nvpmodel -q <h1>Expected: NV Power Mode: MAXN</h1>

For mobile robot operation (battery-powered), the appropriate power mode depends on the mission phase:

<h1>During active perception-heavy operation (navigation, manipulation)</h1>

sudo nvpmodel -m 0    # MAXN — all cores, maximum frequency


<h1>During standby or slow walking</h1> sudo nvpmodel -m 2    # 15W mode — reduced cores, lower frequency


<h1>For ultra-low-power standby</h1> sudo nvpmodel -m 3    # 10W mode

10.3.4 Installing ROS 2 Humble on JetPack 6.x

ROS 2 Humble is available from the official ROS apt repository for Ubuntu 22.04 (the Linux distribution embedded in JetPack 6.x):

<h1>Set locale</h1>

sudo apt-get update && sudo apt-get install -y locales sudo locale-gen en_US en_US.UTF-8 sudo update-locale LC_ALL=en_US.UTF-8 LANG=en_US.UTF-8 export LANG=en_US.UTF-8


<h1>Add ROS 2 repository</h1> sudo apt-get install -y software-properties-common curl sudo add-apt-repository universe curl -sSL https://raw.githubusercontent.com/ros/rosdistro/master/ros.key \ -o /usr/share/keyrings/ros-archive-keyring.gpg echo "deb [arch=$(dpkg --print-architecture) \ signed-by=/usr/share/keyrings/ros-archive-keyring.gpg] \ http://packages.ros.org/ros2/ubuntu jammy main" \ | sudo tee /etc/apt/sources.list.d/ros2.list > /dev/null


<h1>Install ROS 2 Humble (bare-bones for robot deployment)</h1> sudo apt-get update sudo apt-get install -y ros-humble-ros-base     # No GUI tools on robot sudo apt-get install -y ros-humble-nav2-bringup sudo apt-get install -y ros-humble-vision-msgs sudo apt-get install -y python3-colcon-common-extensions \ python3-rosdep


<h1>Initialize rosdep</h1> sudo rosdep init rosdep update


<h1>Add to .bashrc</h1> echo "source /opt/ros/humble/setup.bash" >> ~/.bashrc source ~/.bashrc

10.4 Deploying ROS 2 Nodes to Jetson

10.4.1 Cross-Compilation vs. Native Compilation

There are two strategies for building ROS 2 packages targeting Jetson Orin's ARM64 (aarch64) processor:

Native compilation builds the code directly on the Jetson device. This is the simpler approach — no cross-compilation toolchain configuration is needed — and it guarantees that the build environment perfectly matches the target runtime environment. The disadvantage is build time: a Jetson Orin NX 16GB with 8 CPU cores can build a moderately complex ROS 2 workspace in 10–30 minutes, compared to 2–5 minutes on a high-end x86_64 workstation. Cross-compilation builds the code on an x86_64 development workstation using an ARM64 toolchain and sysroot. The output binary runs on the Jetson without modification. Cross-compilation is dramatically faster for large workspaces and enables automated CI/CD pipelines that produce Jetson-ready artifacts without requiring physical hardware. The disadvantage is configuration complexity: the ARM64 sysroot must match the JetPack version on the target Jetson, and any native library dependency (CUDA, TensorRT, cuDNN) must be available in the sysroot.

For most humanoid robot development workflows, Docker-based deployment (Section 10.4.2) provides a practical middle ground that avoids the complexity of native cross-compilation while still enabling efficient iteration.

10.4.2 Docker Containers on Jetson — NVIDIA L4T Base Images

NVIDIA provides official Docker base images for Jetson, built on Linux for Tegra (L4T), that include CUDA, cuDNN, TensorRT, and VPI pre-installed for the Jetson Orin architecture [3]. These images are published on NVIDIA NGC (NVIDIA GPU Cloud) and Docker Hub under the nvcr.io/nvidia/l4t-* namespace.

Key base images for ROS 2 humanoid robot deployments:

Base ImageContentsSize
nvcr.io/nvidia/l4t-base:r36.2.0Ubuntu 22.04 + L4T drivers only~1.1 GB
nvcr.io/nvidia/l4t-cuda:12.2.2-runtimeL4T base + CUDA 12.x runtime~2.2 GB
nvcr.io/nvidia/l4t-tensorrt:8.6.xCUDA + cuDNN + TensorRT 8.x~4.1 GB
nvcr.io/nvidia/isaac/ros:aarch64-ros2_humble-3.1.0TensorRT + ROS 2 Humble + Isaac ROS~8.5 GB

To run a GPU-enabled Docker container on Jetson, the NVIDIA Container Runtime must be configured:

<h1>Install nvidia-container-runtime (typically pre-installed with JetPack 6.x)</h1>

sudo apt-get install -y nvidia-container-runtime


<h1>Verify Docker can access GPU</h1> docker run --rm --runtime nvidia \ nvcr.io/nvidia/l4t-cuda:12.2.2-runtime \ nvidia-smi <h1>Expected: Shows Jetson GPU information</h1>

10.4.3 Dockerfile for a ROS 2 Node on Jetson

The following Dockerfile demonstrates building a custom ROS 2 perception node for Jetson Orin, layered on the NVIDIA L4T TensorRT base image:

<h1>=============================================================================</h1>

<h1>Dockerfile — ROS 2 Humble Object Detection Node for Jetson Orin</h1> <h1>Base: NVIDIA L4T TensorRT 8.6 (JetPack 6.x / aarch64)</h1> <h1>ROS 2: Humble Hawksbill</h1> <h1>Author: Muhammad Faisal Laiq Siddiqui</h1> <h1>=============================================================================</h1>


FROM nvcr.io/nvidia/l4t-tensorrt:8.6.2-runtime AS base


<h1>--- System dependencies ---</h1> ENV DEBIAN_FRONTEND=noninteractive ENV LANG=en_US.UTF-8 ENV ROS_DISTRO=humble


RUN apt-get update && apt-get install -y --no-install-recommends \ # Locale configuration locales \ # Required for ROS 2 apt repo curl \ gnupg2 \ software-properties-common \ # Python build tools python3-pip \ python3-dev \ # SSL certificates (required for Neon/external DB connections) ca-certificates \ # Build tools for native compilation of ROS packages build-essential \ cmake \ git \ && locale-gen en_US.UTF-8 \ && rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*


<h1>--- ROS 2 Humble installation ---</h1> RUN curl -sSL https://raw.githubusercontent.com/ros/rosdistro/master/ros.key \ -o /usr/share/keyrings/ros-archive-keyring.gpg \ && echo "deb [arch=arm64 signed-by=/usr/share/keyrings/ros-archive-keyring.gpg] \ http://packages.ros.org/ros2/ubuntu jammy main" \ > /etc/apt/sources.list.d/ros2.list \ && apt-get update \ && apt-get install -y --no-install-recommends \ ros-humble-ros-base \ ros-humble-vision-msgs \ ros-humble-sensor-msgs \ ros-humble-cv-bridge \ python3-colcon-common-extensions \ python3-rosdep \ && rosdep init \ && rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*


<h1>--- Python dependencies ---</h1> RUN pip3 install --no-cache-dir \ numpy==1.24.4 \ opencv-python-headless==4.8.1.78 \ pycuda==2024.1

WORKDIR /ros2_ws


<h1>Copy the ROS 2 workspace source</h1> COPY src/ src/


<h1>Install rosdep dependencies for all packages</h1> RUN . /opt/ros/humble/setup.sh \ && rosdep update \ && rosdep install --from-paths src --ignore-src -y

<h1>--- Runtime stage: minimal final image ---</h1> FROM base AS runtime


WORKDIR /ros2_ws


<h1>Copy the compiled install directory from builder stage</h1> COPY --from=builder /ros2_ws/install/ install/

<h1>Create non-root user for the ROS node process</h1> RUN useradd -m -u 1000 robot && chown -R robot:robot /ros2_ws /models USER robot


<h1>Source ROS 2 and the workspace on container start</h1> ENV ROS_DOMAIN_ID=42 SHELL ["/bin/bash", "-c"] ENTRYPOINT ["/bin/bash", "-c", \ "source /opt/ros/humble/setup.bash && \ source /ros2_ws/install/setup.bash && \ exec \"$@\"", "--"]


CMD ["ros2", "launch", "robot_perception", "detection_trt.launch.py"]

10.5 ONNX Model Export and TensorRT Optimization

10.5.1 Exporting a PyTorch Model to ONNX

The path from a trained PyTorch model to deployment on Jetson Orin passes through two conversion steps: PyTorch to ONNX (Open Neural Network Exchange), then ONNX to TensorRT engine. ONNX serves as the intermediate representation: a graph-based format that is framework-independent and hardware-portable [4].

The following example exports a fine-tuned YOLOv8 obstacle detection model to ONNX:

#!/usr/bin/env python3

""" export_to_onnx.py Exports a fine-tuned YOLOv8 PyTorch model to ONNX format for subsequent TensorRT conversion on Jetson Orin.


Dependencies: torch>=2.0.0, ultralytics>=8.0.0 """


from ultralytics import YOLO


<h1>---- Configuration ----</h1> CHECKPOINT_PATH = '/models/yolov8n_obstacle_ft.pt'   # Fine-tuned checkpoint ONNX_OUTPUT_PATH = '/models/yolov8n_obstacle.onnx' INPUT_SHAPE = (1, 3, 640, 640)                        # batch=1, RGB, 640×640 OPSET_VERSION = 17                                     # ONNX opset (TensorRT 8.6 supports ≤17)


def export_model(): """Export YOLOv8 fine-tuned model to ONNX with dynamic batch axis.""" print(f'Loading checkpoint: {CHECKPOINT_PATH}') model = YOLO(CHECKPOINT_PATH)


# Export via Ultralytics built-in ONNX export # This handles the complex YOLOv8 head operations correctly model.export( format='onnx', imgsz=640, half=False,          # Export in FP32 first; quantize separately opset=OPSET_VERSION, simplify=True,       # Run onnx-simplifier to reduce graph complexity dynamic=False,       # Fixed batch size for optimal TensorRT optimization )


print(f'ONNX model exported to: {ONNX_OUTPUT_PATH}') verify_onnx(ONNX_OUTPUT_PATH)


def verify_onnx(onnx_path: str) -> None: """Run ONNX shape inference and graph check.""" import onnx import onnxruntime as ort


# Load and check ONNX model model = onnx.load(onnx_path) onnx.checker.check_model(model) print(f'ONNX model is valid. IR version: {model.ir_version}')


# Run shape inference model = onnx.shape_inference.infer_shapes(model)


# Print input/output shapes print('Inputs:') for inp in model.graph.input: shape = [d.dim_value for d in inp.type.tensor_type.shape.dim] print(f'  {inp.name}: {shape}') print('Outputs:') for out in model.graph.output: shape = [d.dim_value for d in out.type.tensor_type.shape.dim] print(f'  {out.name}: {shape}')


# Quick inference test with ONNXRuntime (CPU) session = ort.InferenceSession(onnx_path, providers=['CPUExecutionProvider']) dummy = np.random.rand(*INPUT_SHAPE).astype(np.float32) outputs = session.run(None, {session.get_inputs()[0].name: dummy}) print(f'ONNX inference test passed. Output shape: {outputs[0].shape}')


if __name__ == '__main__': export_model()

10.5.2 Converting ONNX to TensorRT Engine

TensorRT analyzes the ONNX computation graph, fuses compatible layers, selects optimal CUDA kernels for the target GPU architecture, and optionally quantizes weights to reduce precision. The result is a serialized engine file (.engine) that the TensorRT runtime can load and execute directly without re-optimizing [5].

#!/usr/bin/env python3

""" build_trt_engine.py Converts an ONNX model to a TensorRT 8.x engine file. Supports FP32, FP16, and INT8 (with calibration) precision modes.


Tested on: Jetson Orin NX 16GB, JetPack 6.0, TensorRT 8.6 """


from pathlib import Path


<h1>---- TRT logger ----</h1> TRT_LOGGER = trt.Logger(trt.Logger.WARNING)


def build_engine( onnx_path: str, engine_path: str, precision: str = 'fp16', max_workspace_gb: float = 2.0, calibrator=None ) -> None: """ Build a TensorRT engine from an ONNX model file.


Args: onnx_path: Path to the input .onnx file. engine_path: Path to write the output .engine file. precision: One of 'fp32', 'fp16', 'int8'. max_workspace_gb: Maximum GPU memory TRT may use for optimization (in GB). calibrator: IInt8Calibrator instance (required for int8 mode). """ assert precision in ('fp32', 'fp16', 'int8'), f'Invalid precision: {precision}' assert Path(onnx_path).exists(), f'ONNX file not found: {onnx_path}'


builder = trt.Builder(TRT_LOGGER) network_flags = 1 << int(trt.NetworkDefinitionCreationFlag.EXPLICIT_BATCH) network = builder.create_network(network_flags) parser = trt.OnnxParser(network, TRT_LOGGER)


# Parse ONNX model print(f'Parsing ONNX: {onnx_path}') with open(onnx_path, 'rb') as f: if not parser.parse(f.read()): for i in range(parser.num_errors): print(f'  ONNX parse error [{i}]: {parser.get_error(i)}') raise RuntimeError('ONNX parsing failed.') print(f'  Network inputs:  {network.num_inputs}') print(f'  Network outputs: {network.num_outputs}') print(f'  Network layers:  {network.num_layers}')


# Configure builder config = builder.create_builder_config() config.set_memory_pool_limit( trt.MemoryPoolType.WORKSPACE, int(max_workspace_gb * (1 << 30))   # Convert GB to bytes )


if precision == 'fp16': if not builder.platform_has_fast_fp16: print('  WARNING: Platform does not natively support FP16. Using FP32.') else: config.set_flag(trt.BuilderFlag.FP16) print('  Precision: FP16 enabled.')


elif precision == 'int8': if not builder.platform_has_fast_int8: raise RuntimeError('Platform does not support INT8.') config.set_flag(trt.BuilderFlag.INT8) config.set_flag(trt.BuilderFlag.FP16)   # Use FP16 for layers not supported in INT8 if calibrator is None: raise ValueError('INT8 precision requires an IInt8Calibrator instance.') config.int8_calibrator = calibrator print('  Precision: INT8 with FP16 fallback.')


else: print('  Precision: FP32 (default).')


# Build and serialize the engine print('Building TensorRT engine (this may take several minutes on Jetson)...') serialized_engine = builder.build_serialized_network(network, config) if serialized_engine is None: raise RuntimeError('Engine build failed.')


# Write engine file with open(engine_path, 'wb') as f: f.write(serialized_engine)


size_mb = os.path.getsize(engine_path) / (1024 * 1024) print(f'Engine saved to: {engine_path} ({size_mb:.1f} MB)')


if __name__ == '__main__': build_engine( onnx_path='/models/yolov8n_obstacle.onnx', engine_path='/models/yolov8n_obstacle_fp16.engine', precision='fp16', max_workspace_gb=2.0 )

10.5.3 FP32 vs. FP16 vs. INT8 Precision Trade-offs

TensorRT supports three inference precisions, and the choice among them is one of the most consequential deployment decisions for a humanoid robot:

FP32 (32-bit floating point): Full IEEE 754 single precision. Highest numerical accuracy, largest model size, lowest throughput. Used when accuracy is paramount and hardware supports it, or when debugging precision-sensitive models. FP16 (16-bit floating point): Half precision, natively supported by all Jetson Orin Ampere GPU Tensor Cores. Reduces model memory footprint by approximately 50% and typically doubles inference throughput compared to FP32 on Tensor Core-capable hardware. For most computer vision models — object detection, segmentation, depth estimation — FP16 produces negligible accuracy degradation (typically less than 0.5% mAP reduction) [5]. INT8 (8-bit integer): Quarter of the FP32 memory footprint. Requires a calibration dataset to determine the optimal quantization scale for each layer's activations. INT8 can achieve 1.5–2.5× higher throughput than FP16 on Jetson Orin hardware, but accuracy loss is model-dependent and must be validated carefully. For a safety-critical application like obstacle detection, INT8 quantization requires thorough evaluation across representative deployment scenarios. Table 10.2 — Inference Latency Comparison: PyTorch vs. ONNX Runtime vs. TensorRT on Jetson Orin NX 16GB
FrameworkPrecisionYOLOv8n (640×640) LatencyFPSMemory (GPU)
PyTorch (eager mode)FP32~85 ms~12~1.2 GB
PyTorch (torch.compile)FP32~55 ms~18~1.1 GB
ONNX Runtime (CUDA EP)FP32~38 ms~26~0.9 GB
ONNX Runtime (CUDA EP)FP16~25 ms~40~0.5 GB
TensorRT 8.xFP32~22 ms~45~0.8 GB
TensorRT 8.xFP16~11 ms~91~0.4 GB
TensorRT 8.xINT8~7 ms~143~0.2 GB
Approximate values based on published TensorRT benchmarks and community measurements for Orin NX class hardware. Actual performance varies with model complexity, batch size, and system load [5].

10.5.4 Python Code for TensorRT Inference

#!/usr/bin/env python3

""" trt_inference.py Runs TensorRT inference on a Jetson Orin using a pre-built .engine file. Integrates with ROS 2 as a standard Python callback.


TensorRT 8.6 / CUDA 12.x / Python 3.10 """


from typing import List, Tuple


TRT_LOGGER = trt.Logger(trt.Logger.WARNING)


class TRTInferenceEngine: """ Wraps a TensorRT engine for easy inference in a ROS 2 node. Manages GPU memory allocation and host-device transfers. """


def __init__(self, engine_path: str): """ Load a serialized TensorRT engine from disk.


Args: engine_path: Path to the .engine file built by build_trt_engine.py. """ self._engine_path = engine_path self._engine = None self._context = None self._bindings = [] self._host_inputs = [] self._host_outputs = [] self._cuda_inputs = [] self._cuda_outputs = [] self._stream = None self._load_engine()


def _load_engine(self) -> None: """Deserialize engine and allocate I/O buffers.""" runtime = trt.Runtime(TRT_LOGGER) with open(self._engine_path, 'rb') as f: serialized_engine = f.read() self._engine = runtime.deserialize_cuda_engine(serialized_engine) self._context = self._engine.create_execution_context() self._stream = cuda.Stream()


# Allocate host and device buffers for each binding (input or output) for binding_idx in range(self._engine.num_io_tensors): binding_name = self._engine.get_tensor_name(binding_idx) binding_shape = self._engine.get_tensor_shape(binding_name) binding_dtype = trt.nptype(self._engine.get_tensor_dtype(binding_name)) binding_mode = self._engine.get_tensor_mode(binding_name)


size = int(np.prod(binding_shape)) host_mem = cuda.pagelocked_empty(size, binding_dtype) device_mem = cuda.mem_alloc(host_mem.nbytes) self._bindings.append(int(device_mem))


if binding_mode == trt.TensorIOMode.INPUT: self._host_inputs.append(host_mem) self._cuda_inputs.append(device_mem) else: self._host_outputs.append(host_mem) self._cuda_outputs.append(device_mem)


print(f'TensorRT engine loaded: {self._engine_path}') print(f'  Inputs:  {len(self._host_inputs)}') print(f'  Outputs: {len(self._host_outputs)}')


def infer(self, input_array: np.ndarray) -> List[np.ndarray]: """ Run inference on a pre-processed input array.


Args: input_array: Preprocessed image tensor, shape (1, 3, H, W), float32 or float16.


Returns: List of numpy arrays, one per output binding. """ # Copy input to pinned host memory, then to GPU np.copyto(self._host_inputs[0], input_array.ravel()) cuda.memcpy_htod_async(self._cuda_inputs[0], self._host_inputs[0], self._stream)


# Execute inference asynchronously on the CUDA stream self._context.execute_async_v2( bindings=self._bindings, stream_handle=self._stream.handle )


# Copy output tensors from GPU to host results = [] for host_out, cuda_out in zip(self._host_outputs, self._cuda_outputs): cuda.memcpy_dtoh_async(host_out, cuda_out, self._stream) self._stream.synchronize()   # Wait for all async operations to complete


for host_out in self._host_outputs: results.append(host_out.copy())


return results


def preprocess_image(self, image_bgr: np.ndarray, target_size: Tuple[int, int]) -> np.ndarray: """ Preprocess a BGR image for YOLOv8 TensorRT inference.


Args: image_bgr: Input BGR image from OpenCV. target_size: (width, height) tuple for resizing.


Returns: Preprocessed float32 array with shape (1, 3, H, W), values [0.0, 1.0]. """ import cv2 w, h = target_size # Resize preserving aspect ratio with letterboxing resized = cv2.resize(image_bgr, (w, h), interpolation=cv2.INTER_LINEAR) # BGR → RGB, HWC → CHW, normalize to [0, 1] rgb = cv2.cvtColor(resized, cv2.COLOR_BGR2RGB) chw = np.transpose(rgb, (2, 0, 1)).astype(np.float32) / 255.0 # Add batch dimension return np.expand_dims(chw, axis=0)


def destroy(self) -> None: """Release GPU resources.""" del self._context del self._engine

10.6 Power Consumption and Thermal Management

10.6.1 Power Modes on Jetson Orin

NVIDIA's nvpmodel utility manages the power-performance trade-off on Jetson Orin by setting active CPU core counts, CPU/GPU frequency caps, and DLA frequency [1]. Available power modes for the Orin NX 16GB include:

Table 10.3 — nvpmodel Power Modes (Jetson Orin NX 16GB)
Mode IDMode NameCPU Cores ActiveGPU Max FreqPower CapTypical Use
0MAXN8918 MHz25 WActive navigation and perception
120W6765 MHz20 WNormal robot operation
215W4612 MHz15 WLight task execution
310W2459 MHz10 WStandby, slow walking

10.6.2 Monitoring with tegrastats

tegrastats is NVIDIA's built-in system monitoring utility for Jetson platforms, providing real-time CPU, GPU, memory, temperature, and power consumption data [2]: 
<h1>Run tegrastats at 1-second intervals</h1>

sudo tegrastats --interval 1000


<h1>Example output (Jetson Orin NX 16GB, MAXN mode, running Isaac ROS stack):</h1> <h1>RAM 8947/15625MB (lfb 512x4MB) SWAP 0/8192MB (cached 0MB)</h1> <h1>CPU [42%@2016,38%@2016,51%@2016,44%@2016,29%@2016,35%@2016,47%@2016,41%@2016]</h1> <h1>GR3D_FREQ 87% gpu@52.500C</h1> <h1>VIC_FREQ 0% cpu@50.125C soc2@47.875C soc0@49.750C CV0@51.125C CV1@48.875C CV2@50.250C</h1> <h1>SOC_THERM throttle_reason=none</h1> <h1>VDD_IN 18234mW VDD_CPU_GPU_CV 8421mW VDD_SOC 3912mW</h1> <h1>MTS fg 0% bg 0%</h1>

Parsing the relevant fields from this output:

FieldValueInterpretation
RAM8947/15625 MB8.7 GB of 15.3 GB used
CPU [42%@2016 ...]42–51% load at 2016 MHzModerate CPU load, all 8 cores active
GR3D_FREQ 87%GPU at 87% utilizationTensorRT inference running
gpu@52.500C52.5°CWithin safe operating temperature
VDD_IN 18234mW18.2 WTotal module power draw
VDD_CPU_GPU_CV 8421mW8.4 WCPU + GPU + CV block power

10.6.3 Thermal Throttling and Mitigation

Jetson Orin begins thermal throttling (automatically reducing clock speeds) when GPU temperature exceeds 85°C [1]. For a humanoid robot operating in a warm environment under sustained perception workloads, thermal management is not optional — it is a system reliability requirement.

Active cooling: The Jetson AGX Orin Developer Kit ships with an active fan; production modules use third-party carrier boards with custom heatsink and fan designs. For a humanoid robot torso, a heatsink with a 5 W blower fan can maintain Orin NX junction temperature below 70°C at 25 W power draw in a 25°C ambient environment. Workload scheduling: Rather than running all perception models continuously, a humanoid robot can dynamically enable models based on task context: 
<h1>Perception mode manager — reduces power during non-critical phases</h1>

class PerceptionModeManager: MODES = { 'navigation': ['vslam', 'obstacle_detection', 'costmap'], 'manipulation': ['object_pose', 'grasp_planning', 'depth'], 'standby':      ['vslam'],    # Maintain localization only }

10.6.4 Power Budget for a Complete Robot Stack

A practical power budget for a mid-tier humanoid robot using Jetson Orin NX 16GB as the primary compute module:

Table 10.4 — Estimated Power Budget for Humanoid Robot Compute Stack
ComponentTypical Power DrawPeak Power
Jetson Orin NX 16GB (MAXN)18–22 W25 W
RealSense D435i (×2, stereo + wrist)3.5 W5 W
2D LiDAR (RPLiDAR S2)4.5 W6 W
IMU (VectorNav VN-100)0.5 W0.5 W
Ethernet switch (inter-board)2.5 W3 W
WiFi/LTE module3.0 W5 W
Miscellaneous board power2.0 W3 W
Total compute + sensing~34 W~47 W
Actuators (28 DOF, idle stance)~80–150 W~400 W
Total robot power~120–190 W~450 W

This power budget highlights that compute power (34 W average) is a relatively small fraction of the total robot power — typically 15–25% of the running average. However, compute power management still matters because it directly affects battery runtime, which for a 500 Wh battery pack at 120–190 W average yields 2.6–4.2 hours of continuous operation.

10.7 Complete Deployment Example

10.7.1 Scenario: Object Detection with TensorRT on Orin NX

This section presents a complete, end-to-end deployment example for a ROS 2 object detection node running TensorRT FP16 inference on a Jetson Orin NX 16GB, representative of a humanoid robot's obstacle detection subsystem.

System configuration:
  • Hardware: Jetson Orin NX 16GB on a Seeed Studio reComputer J4012 carrier board
  • Software: JetPack 6.0 (L4T R36.2), ROS 2 Humble, TensorRT 8.6, Docker 24.x
  • Camera: Intel RealSense D435i (640×480 stereo, 30 Hz)
  • Model: YOLOv8n fine-tuned for obstacle classes, TensorRT FP16 engine

10.8 Chapter Summary and Bridge to Part V

The gap between "works in simulation" and "works on the robot" is the central engineering challenge of humanoid robotics deployment. Every layer of that gap has a solution — and the path runs through JetPack 6.x, Docker L4T containers, ROS 2 Humble, and TensorRT. Flashing the Orin NX, configuring MAXN power mode and swap space, containerizing the robot software stack, and converting PyTorch models to TensorRT engines are not preliminary steps before the real work begins; they are the real work, and each one has failure modes that only emerge under production conditions.

The TensorRT optimization results are striking and consequential: the same YOLOv8n model that runs at 12 FPS in PyTorch eager mode on Orin NX achieves 91 FPS in TensorRT FP16 — a 7.5× throughput improvement from a model conversion that requires no retraining and, for most computer vision models, introduces negligible accuracy loss. This optimization is not a refinement; it is the difference between a perception system that cannot meet real-time requirements and one that comfortably does so.

The power and thermal analysis establishes that the Jetson Orin NX 16GB can sustain the full Isaac ROS perception stack at approximately 18–25 W without thermal throttling, and that compute power represents 15–25% of total robot power budget — manageable, but requiring deliberate workload scheduling to maximize battery runtime.

The complete deployment example — Dockerfile, launch file, and measured performance figures — provides a reference implementation that a robotics engineer can adapt for a specific humanoid platform, substituting the obstacle detection model with the perception models appropriate to their application.

Part IV of this book — covering NVIDIA Isaac Sim (Chapter 8), Isaac ROS (Chapter 9), and Jetson Deployment (this chapter) — has presented the complete NVIDIA AI stack for humanoid robotics: from photorealistic simulation for training data generation and policy development, through GPU-accelerated real-time perception, to edge deployment on the hardware that rides inside the robot. This stack is not hypothetical; it is the architecture used in active humanoid robot development programs as of 2025–2026.

Part V, which begins in Chapter 11, turns from the AI infrastructure to language-conditioned control. Vision-Language-Action (VLA) systems combine foundation models with real-time perception to translate natural-language instructions into physical robot motion — the capability that makes a humanoid robot useful beyond hard-coded automation. Chapter 11 examines VLA architecture and training pipelines; Chapter 12 builds a complete voice-to-action system on top of them, from microphone to MoveIt 2 trajectory.
